Rational Proof from Innate Body Awareness
Rational proof from innate attribution of body parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses innate human awareness of body parts as a rational proof for the distinction between the self and the body.

Core Idea
Your intuitive awareness of your own body proves your soul is not your body.

The author now moves from scriptural evidence to a rational proof accessible to every human being. He argues that the most basic, undeniable statements we make about our own body reveal a fundamental truth: the 'I' that owns these parts is not identical to them. This lesson unpacks that simple yet powerful argument from fitra and badiha.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author presents the proof in a straightforward manner.
The passage is short but dense; let its simplicity strike you.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms 'فطرة' (fitra), 'بديهة العقل' (badiha al-aql), 'إضافة' (idafa), and 'نفس' (nafs). Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are central to the proof's foundation.
3
Understand the logical structure
The author says that everyone innately and intuitively knows that statements like 'my head', 'my heart' are correct. This attribution implies a self distinct from the body. Denying this is denying the most evident knowledge.
The proof is not based on complex syllogism but on universal intuition.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Ask yourself: Why does the author call this knowledge 'the most apparent, complete, and manifest'? Consider how this argument applies to your own experience.
This reflection helps internalize the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني : وهو بيان أن الشواهد العقلية تدل على ما ذكرناه : فذلك لأن فطرة كل أحد ، وبديهة عقله : تحكم بصحة قوله : إن رأسي كذا ، وقلبي كذا كذا ، ودماغي كذا [ وأذني كذا وسمعي ، وبصري كذا وكذا]] وكل من نازع في صحة هذه الإضافة فقد نازع في أظهر العلوم وأكملها وأجلاها .
And as for the second station: it is an explanation that the rational evidences indicate what we have mentioned. That is because the innate disposition of everyone and the immediate intuition of his reason judge the correctness of his saying: 'My head is such-and-such, and my heart is such-and-such, and my brain is such-and-such, and my ear is such-and-such, and my hearing, and my sight are such-and-such.' And whoever disputes the correctness of this attribution has disputed the most apparent, most complete, and most manifest of sciences.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the soul's distinction from the body, presented as the second station. The author argues from fitra (innate disposition) and badiha al-aql (immediate intuition of reason). Every person naturally and intuitively makes statements like 'my head is such-and-such, my heart is such-and-such, my brain...' etc. This attribution of body parts to oneself is so obvious that denying it would be denying the most evident knowledge. The implication is that the self (the 'I') to which these parts are attributed is different from the parts themselves.

Key Terms
فطرة
بديهة العقل
إضافة
نفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof relies on complex philosophical speculation.
Correction: It relies on the innate disposition and immediate intuition common to all people.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the basis for the rational proof?
A) Logical syllogism from Aristotle
B) The innate disposition and immediate intuition common to all people
C) Consensus of scholars
D) The authority of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The innate human intuition of owning body parts proves that the self is distinct from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'my hand' or 'my brain,' do you ever pause to consider who the 'I' really is? Are you living as if your body is you, or as if you are something more?
?
If your deepest self is not your body, why do you invest so much in the body's appearance and desires while neglecting your soul? What would change if you truly felt the priority of the soul?
?
The proof says everyone innately knows the self is distinct from the body. Is that knowledge reflected in your daily choices? Do you act on that truth, or have you buried it under distractions?

Third Objection: Composition Does Not Imply Corporeality
The third objection refutes the tamathul argument by analogizing composition of genus and difference in colors to composition in souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection refutes the argument for tamathul by showing that composition through genus and difference does not necessitate corporeality.

Core Idea
Composition of genus and difference does not make something a body.

The third objection to the tamathul argument acknowledges that the original reasoning leads to composition in the soul's essence. However, it challenges the assumption that all composition implies corporeality. By drawing an analogy with colors, the objection demonstrates that logical composition (genus + difference) can exist in non-physical entities. Let's examine this objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the concession and the counterpoint
Notice that the objection first concedes (for argument's sake) that composition follows, but then denies that this entails corporeality.
3
Analyze the analogy of blackness and whiteness
See how blackness and whiteness share the genus 'color' and differ by specific differences, yet they are not bodies. This supports the claim that composition does not equal bodyhood.
4
Apply the analogy to the soul
The objection concludes that, similarly, even if the soul's essence had composition (from genus and difference), it would not become a body.
5
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by declaring the original argument weak and invalid. Make sure you see why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أن هذا يقتضي وقوع التركب في هذه الماهية ، إلا أن هذا النوع من التركب لا يقتضي الجسمية . ألا ترى أن السواد والبياض يتشاركان في اللونية ، ويتخالفان في بعض أجزاء الماهية ، فوجب أن تكون ماهية البياض والسواد مركبتين من الجنس والفصل، ومع ذلك ، فلم يلزم من حصول هذا النوع من التركب ، كونها جسمين : فكذا ههنا . فثبت : أن هذه الحجة : ضعيفة باطلة .
The third question: Suppose that this does entail composition in this essence, but this kind of composition does not entail corporeality. Do you not see that blackness and whiteness share in color-ness and differ in some parts of the essence, so it follows that the essence of whiteness and blackness are composed of genus and difference, and with that, it does not follow from obtaining this kind of composition that they are two bodies? So likewise here. So it is established that this argument is weak and invalid.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is the third objection (السؤال الثالث) to the tamathul argument. The objection grants, for the sake of argument, that the pro-tamathul reasoning would lead to composition (tarkīb) in the soul's essence. But it then argues that such composition does not necessarily entail corporeality (jismiyya). The objection uses an analogy: blackness and whiteness share in the genus of 'color-ness' (lawniyya) yet differ in specific differences (fusūl), and they are composed of genus and difference, but this does not make them bodies. Similarly, even if souls were composed of genus and difference, they would not be bodies. Therefore, the original argument's claim that composition is impossible for incorporeal substances is refuted, because composition of genus and difference does not imply composition that requires extension or corporeality. Thus the argument for tamathul is shown to be weak and invalid.

Key Terms
تركيب (tarkīb)
جسمية (jismiyya)
سواد وبياض (sawad wa bayad)
لونية (lawniyya)
جنس (jins)
فصل (fasl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any kind of composition (e.g., genus and difference) makes an entity a physical body.
Correction: Logical composition of genus and difference does not entail corporeality; abstract entities like colors and souls can be composed this way without being bodies.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy of blackness and whiteness demonstrate in the third objection?
A) That color is corporeal.
B) That composition of genus and difference does not entail corporeality.
C) That souls are like colors.
D) That the argument is strong.

Key Takeaway
The third objection shows that composition via genus and difference does not imply corporeality, thus the argument for tamathul is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that composition via genus and difference does not make something a body. Think of a belief you hold about spiritual realities (like the soul, angels, or God) that might inadvertently rely on physical analogies. How can you test that belief against this principle?
?
The third objection refuted the tamathul argument by showing logical composition doesn't imply corporeality. Have you ever clung to an argument that seemed strong but was later shown to be based on a false analogy? What does that teach you about intellectual humility?
?
If the soul's essence can be composed without being a body, then what does that reveal about your own understanding of your soul? Are you treating it as merely a 'subtle body' rather than a separate immaterial reality? What change is required in your daily life to truly honor the soul's nature?

First Proof: Constancy of Perception Despite Temperament Change
First argument that soul essences differ, based on invariant perception despite temperamental change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that soul essences differ, based on the observation that perception and character remain constant despite changes in bodily temperament.

Core Idea
Constant perception despite changing temperament proves souls differ by essence.

This proof begins with a simple observation: a person's temperament can shift dramatically from hot to cold, wet to dry, yet their basic mental and moral faculties remain unchanged. The author then uses a conditional reasoning: if temperament were the cause, a change in temperament would cause a change in perception and character. Since no such change occurs, the cause must be the soul's own essence. This logical move establishes one of the key foundations for the doctrine of the immaterial soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the examples of temperament change and the constancy of perception and character.
The passage uses two concrete examples involving choleric and phlegmatic temperaments.
2
Identify the logical structure
Recognize the conditional (if...then) reasoning: if temperament causes differences in perception/character, then changing temperament should change them. The author observes that this consequent does not happen.
3
Examine the examples
Consider the person with choleric temperament who gets phlegmatic fever, and the phlegmatic person with hot delirium. In both cases, their original perception and character remain.
These examples show that even major temperamental shifts do not alter core faculties.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the observed data contradicts the conditional's prediction, the antecedent (temperament as cause) is false. The author concludes that the difference must be due to the soul's own essence.
This is a classic modus tollens argument.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about what this means for the soul's independence from the body. If the soul's essence were tied to temperament, such changes would cause different souls, but they don't.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن الإنسان الواحد قد ينتقل من المزاج الحار ، إلى المزاج البارد ، وكذا القول في الرطب واليابس ، وأما قوة الإدراك ، وقوة الأخلاق ، فهي باقية بحالها من أول العمر إلى آخره، من غير تفاوت أصلا ، مثل إنسان صفراوي المزاج استولى عليه [ الحمى البلغمية أو الاستسقاء أو الفالج . ومثل انسان بلغمي المزاج استولى عليه (۱) ] حمى الغب والرسام الحار ، فإن كل واحد من هؤلاء ؛ يبقى على المقدار الأول الذي كان له من الإدراك والخلق ، ولو كان الاختلاف الحاصل في قوة الإدراك والفعل ، معللا باختلاف الأمزجة . لوجب أن يحصل اختلاف حال الإدراك والخلق ، عند اختلاف [ أحوال (۲) ] الأمزجة. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا : أن ذلك ليس لأجل الأمزجة ، بل لاختلاف ماهيات النفوس .
The first proof: That one individual may transition from a hot temperament to a cold temperament, and similarly for wet and dry. But the power of perception and the power of character remain in their state from the beginning of life to its end, without any variation at all. For example, a person with a choleric temperament who is overtaken by phlegmatic fever, dropsy, or paralysis; and a person with a phlegmatic temperament who is overtaken by tertian fever and hot delirium. Each of these remains at the original measure of perception and character that he had. If the difference occurring in the power of perception and action were caused by differences in temperaments, then it would be necessary that a difference in the state of perception and character should occur when the states of the temperaments differ. Since it is not so, we know that this is not due to temperaments, but due to the difference in the essences of souls.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the thesis that the diversity in souls is due to their intrinsic essences rather than bodily temperaments. The argument proceeds by observing that an individual can experience significant changes in bodily temperament (e.g., from hot to cold, from wet to dry) and yet the fundamental powers of perception and character remain unchanged throughout life. Examples are given: a person of choleric temperament suffering from phlegmatic fever or paralysis, and a person of phlegmatic temperament suffering from tertian fever or hot delirium—in both cases their original cognitive and moral faculties persist. The author then states the conditional: if the variations in perception and action were caused by differences in temperament, then whenever temperament changes, there should be a corresponding change in perception and character. Since this correlation does not happen, the cause cannot be temperament but must be the different essences of souls.

Key Terms
جواهر النفوس
أمزجة
قوة الإدراك
الأخلاق
ماهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that temperament can affect the soul at all.
Correction: The argument does not deny that temperament can affect the soul, only that it is not the essential cause of differences in soul essences.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical structure of the first proof?
A) Analogy
B) Conditional (if-then) with observation that the consequent fails
C) Proof by contradiction
D) Argument from authority

Key Takeaway
The constancy of perception and character despite temperamental shifts shows that the soul's essence is not determined by the body's temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a major health change—illness, injury, or aging—that dramatically altered your body, yet you found that your core sense of self, your ability to think, and your moral inclinations remained fundamentally unchanged? How does this lesson's proof challenge you to see your soul as more than just your physical state, and what are the implications for how you care for your soul vs. your body?
?
If your soul's essence is not determined by your temperament or bodily condition, then what is the ultimate source of your character? This proof argues that your soul's essence itself is the cause. Are you living as if your character is fixed by your biology (e.g., ‘I’m just this way because of my genes’), or are you actively cultivating your soul through choices and actions? What would change if you truly believed your essence is independent?
?
The proof claims that perception and character remain constant 'from the beginning of life to its end.' Think about your own life: is your fundamental character today the same as when you were a child? If so, what does that imply about your responsibility for your moral development? If not, how do you reconcile your change with this proof—was it an accidental change or an essential one? Examine honestly: are you the same person you were ten years ago, and what does that mean for your soul's journey?

Second Proof: Dissociation Between Bodily and Psychological Similarity
Second argument: bodily similarity does not guarantee psychological similarity, and vice versa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodily similarity does not entail psychological similarity and how this proves the independence of the two domains.

Core Idea
Bodily constitution does not determine the soul's qualities.

This passage presents the second argument against materialism. The author uses empirical observation to show that two people can be physically very different yet share the same psychological traits, or vice versa. This dissociation leads to a crucial conclusion about the independence of the soul from the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the empirical observation the author starts with.
2
Identify the two observations
The author makes two observations: (1) two persons differ in body but are similar in perception and character; (2) equality in temperament can exist without equality in psychological states. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the logical inference
From these observations, the author infers that bodily and psychological domains are not causally linked. Ask yourself: why does this dissociation prove independence?
The key is that if A always causes B, then variations in A would always produce corresponding variations in B. Here they don't.
4
Absorb the conclusion
The conclusion is that each domain is an independent principle (أصل بنفسه). Reflect on what this means for the nature of the soul.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
This argument does not deny interaction between body and soul; it denies that one is reducible to the other. Think of examples where body and soul influence each other while remaining distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنا نرى إنسانين يختلفان في التركيب البدني ، والتأليف المزاجي جداً. ، مع أنهما يتشابهان في قوة الإدراك والخلق جسداً . ولما رأينا : أن المساواة في المزاج ، قد تنفك عن المساواة في الأحوال النفسانية ، والاختلاف في المزاج قد ينفك عن الاختلاف في الأحوال النفسانية ، علمنا : أن كل واحد من هذين البابين : أصل بنفسه ، ولا تعلق لأحدهما بالآخر
The second proof: We see two persons who differ greatly in bodily composition and temperamental formation, while they are similar in the power of perception and character [literally 'as a body'?]. And when we see that equality in temperament may be detached from equality in psychological states, and difference in temperament may be detached from difference in psychological states, we know that each of these two domains is a principle in itself, and neither has any connection to the other.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument, which observes the independence of bodily constitution and psychological qualities. The author notes that two individuals can have very different bodily temperaments yet be similar in perception and character. Conversely, two individuals with similar temperaments can have different psychological states. From this dissociation, the author concludes that the bodily and psychological domains are each independent principles; one is not caused by the other.

Key Terms
التركيب البدني
التأليف المزاجي
قوة الإدراك
الأخلاق
الأحوال النفسانية
أصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims there is no interaction at all between body and soul.
Correction: The argument does not deny interaction; it only denies that their essences are causally dependent on each other. Interaction can occur between distinct substances.

Checkpoint
What is the key observation in the second proof?
A) Temperament always determines character
B) Bodily and psychological similarities can occur independently
C) Two persons always differ in both body and soul
D) Perception is a bodily function

Key Takeaway
Bodily constitution and psychological qualities are independent principles; one does not cause the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your body is tired or sick, do you allow your soul to sink into despair, or do you actively remind yourself that your psychological state is not determined by your physical condition? What concrete step can you take today to assert your soul's independence from bodily weakness?
?
Think of a time you judged someone's character based on their appearance or health. How does this proof challenge that assumption? Will you change how you perceive others after reading this lesson?
?
If your soul is truly independent of your body's temperament, then your spiritual growth is not at the mercy of your genetics or physical circumstances. What does that demand of you right now, in this moment, regarding your relationship with your Creator?

Third Proof: Bodily Organs Do Not Cause Skills
Third argument: bodily organs as instruments do not cause skills

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that bodily organs are mere instruments and their suitability does not cause the soul's skills, desires, or aversions; these traits are rooted in the soul's essence.

Core Idea
Suitable tools don't make the master; the soul's traits are intrinsic.

After establishing that bodily temperaments are not the cause of differences in desires, the author presents a third proof using an analogy from craftsmanship. He argues that just as having good tools does not give a carpenter skill, having a well-suited body does not cause the soul to have certain traits. The passage drives home that the difference in desires and aversions among people must be due to the essence of their souls, not their bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Note the key terms like 'آلات' (instruments) and 'ملكة' (mastery).
The passage is a single, continuous argument.
2
Understand the Premise
Identify the premise: bodily organs are instruments for the soul. Consider that instruments themselves do not produce actions; the agent does.
3
Analyze the Carpenter Analogy
The author gives the example of a carpenter with excellent tools. Notice that the tools' suitability does not give the carpenter the skills. This is a key point: the skill must come from the carpenter's essence, not the tools.
The analogy is meant to be intuitive and transferable.
4
Grasp the Conclusion
The conclusion directly applies the analogy: differences in desires and aversions cannot be due to bodily temperaments and organs, therefore they must be in the soul's essence (جواهرها وماهياتها).
This reinforces the thesis that souls have intrinsic differences.
5
Reflect on the Implication
Consider what this means for causality: the body is a condition, not a cause, for the soul's traits. This refutes those who attribute all differences to physical causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن الأعضاء البدنية آلات للنفوس في الأعمال المختلفة ، وكون الآلة صالحة لا يقتضي حصول ملكة تلك الأفعال في ذوات الفاعلين [ ألا ترى (٤) ] أن ( النجار ، إذا كــان و قدومه ، شديد الصلاحية للنحت ، وكان و منشاره و شديد الصلاحية للنشر ، وكانت ( مثقبته ، شديدة الصلاحية للثقب . فإن كون هذه الآلات صالحة لهذه الأعمال ، لا يوجب حصول ملكات هذه الأعمال في ذات النجار. فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن اختلاف نفوس الناس في الرغبة والنفرة في بعض الأشياء دون البعض، لا يمكن أن يكون لأجل اختلاف الأمزجة البدنية والأعضاء الآلية فوجب أن يكون ذلك الاختلاف في جواهرها وماهياتها .
The third proof: The bodily organs are instruments for souls in various actions. The fact that an instrument is suitable does not entail the acquisition of the mastery of those actions in the agent himself. Do you not see that the carpenter, if his axe is extremely suitable for carving, and his saw extremely suitable for sawing, and his drill extremely suitable for drilling, the fact that these instruments are suitable for these actions does not cause the acquisition of the skills of these actions in the carpenter's self. So it is established, by what we have mentioned, that the difference among people's souls in desire and aversion for some things rather than others cannot be due to differences in bodily temperaments and instrumental organs. Therefore, that difference must be in their essences and realities.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument, using an analogy from craftsmanship. The bodily organs are instruments used by the soul in various actions. The suitability of an instrument for a task does not give the user the skill itself. For example, a carpenter may have excellent tools (axe, saw, drill) but that does not mean the carpenter inherently possesses the skills of carving, sawing, and drilling. Similarly, having a well-tempered body does not cause the soul to have certain desires and aversions. Therefore, differences in desires and aversions cannot be due to bodily temperaments and organs; they must be due to the essences of the souls themselves.

Key Terms
آلات
ملكة
ذوات الفاعلين
الرغبة والنفرة
جواهر
ماهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that having a good physical temperament or well-suited bodily organs causes one to have certain desires, skills, or aversions.
Correction: The author argues that just as good tools don't give a carpenter skill, a good body doesn't cause the soul's traits. The soul's essence is the source.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the carpenter analogy in the third proof?
A) Good tools make a good carpenter
B) Tools are unnecessary for skill
C) Instrumental suitability does not confer skill to the agent
D) The soul is similar to a carpenter

Key Takeaway
The suitability of bodily organs does not cause the soul's skills or inclinations; these arise from the soul's own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the desires of your soul come from its essence, not your body, then what are you doing to cultivate that essence? Are you treating your physical temperament as an excuse for your character flaws, or are you taking direct responsibility to change from within?
?
When you feel aversion towards something good (like prayer or charity), do you attribute it to your 'personality' or 'mood'? This lesson demands you reconsider: could that aversion be rooted in your soul's essence, and what are you willing to do today to reshape it?
?
The carpenter's skill is not caused by his tools—so what 'tools' are you relying on instead of developing true mastery of your soul? Are you blaming your environment, health, or circumstances for your spiritual state, rather than working on your inner self?

Refuting Environmental Determinism of the Soul
Refutes environmental causes of soul differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why environment and education cannot be the exclusive causes of a person's moral and spiritual inclinations, based on observable evidence and prophetic examples.

Core Idea
Environment influences but does not determine the soul's direction.

Some argue that a person's moral character is shaped entirely by their teachers, peers, and upbringing. This lesson examines a powerful refutation: we see clear cases where individuals raised in evil become good, and those raised in good become evil. The examples of Prophet Noah and Prophet Abraham drive this point home.

Guided Path
1
1. Understand the Objection
Read the opening sentence where the author states the claim he is refuting: that desire and aversion come only from teaching, guidance, company, and friendship.
Notice the four factors listed: teaching of teachers, guidance of masters, sitting with peers, mixing with friends.
2
2. Examine the Counterexamples
The author points to common observations: children of corrupt parents sometimes incline to good, and vice versa. Consider how this contradicts a purely environmental explanation.
The phrase 'قد يكون بالضد منه' emphasizes the opposite possibility.
3
3. Reflect on the Prophetic Examples
The author invokes Noah (son who drowned in disbelief) and Abraham (idolatrous father). These are strong scriptural proofs that environment does not determine faith or character.
These examples are from the Qur'an and known to the audience.

And it cannot be said that that desire and aversion only occurred due to the teaching of teachers, the guidance of masters, the company of peers, and the association with friends. That is because we observe often that the matter occurs in these circumstances in the opposite way. For a person may be such that his parents are people of evil, corruption, and debauchery, and he grows up among them and mixes with those groups, and does not meet anyone from the people of good, yet at the beginning of his development he inclines toward good and obedience, and amazing deeds of goodness and understanding appear from him. And it may be the opposite. Consider that with Noah, peace be upon him, and his son, and Abraham, peace be upon him, and his father. Since this phenomenon is observed, we know that the occurrence of these states cannot be attributed to this cause.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. Someone might claim that differences in desire and aversion are caused by education, teaching, company, and environment. The author counters by pointing out numerous counterexamples where individuals raised in evil surroundings develop goodness, and vice versa. Scriptural examples are given: Noah (peace be upon him) had a wicked son, and Abraham (peace be upon him) had an idolatrous father. Since such phenomena are observed, the cause of psychological differences cannot be simply environmental or educational factors.

Correction: While environment can influence, it is not the sole determinant. We observe and have prophetic examples of individuals who deviate from their upbringing.

Fourth Proof: Innate Soul Differences in Children
Fourth argument: child diversity proves innate soul essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how observing natural character differences in young children serves as a proof for the existence of distinct soul essences.

Core Idea
Children's innate moral diversity points to distinct soul essences, not just environment.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from observation of young children. Before education or environment have shaped them, children already show diverse moral traits — some kind and obedient, others wicked and miserly. The author argues that this diversity must stem from the essences of their souls, not from physical temperament or external influences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow: observation, listing possible causes, elimination of two, conclusion.
The Arabic is direct and logical. Pay attention to the words 'فإما' and 'وإما' indicating alternatives.
2
Identify the three possible causes
The passage lists three candidates for why children differ: soul essences, bodily temperaments, and external factors (teachers, peers). See how each is introduced and then dismissed.
The external causes include both learning and social influence.
3
Understand why two causes are rejected
The author states the last two are invalid ('باطلان'). Think about why: temperament is ruled out by earlier arguments (from previous proofs), and external causes are ruled out because children are too young to have been significantly influenced.
This reasoning rests on the observation that diversity appears before extensive external input.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining cause is the essences of the souls themselves. This supports the doctrine that souls are distinct substances with innate characteristics.
This proof complements others by focusing on early childhood before learned behavior sets in.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إنا نرى الصبيان الصغار يختلفون في الأخلاق والأفعال فقد يكون الواحد منهم رحيماً حبيباً سخياً مطيعاً ، سلس القياد . ومنهم من يكون شريراً بخيلاً ، نماماً ، شرهاً ، وقحاً ، شكس الأخلاق . والسبب لهذه الأحوال المختلفة : إما جواهر النفوس، وإما اختلاف أمزجة الأبدان . وإما الأسباب الخارجية ، وهي التعلم من المعلمين [ والاستفادة (١) ] من القرناء والشركاء . والقسمان الأخيران باطلان ، فبقي الأول . وهو المطلوب
The fourth proof: We see that young children differ in character and actions. One of them may be merciful, loving, generous, obedient, easy to lead. And among them is one who is wicked, stingy, a slanderer, greedy, shameless, of bad character. The cause of these differing states is either the essences of souls, or differences in bodily temperaments, or external causes, which are learning from teachers and benefiting from peers and associates. The last two categories are invalid, so the first remains. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument based on observation of young children before they have been significantly influenced by education or environment. Children display diverse moral traits: some are kind and obedient, others wicked and miserly. The possible causes are three: (1) soul essences, (2) bodily temperaments, (3) external factors like teachers and peers. The author argues that the second and third options are invalid: temperament cannot be the cause because the argument from the previous proofs (especially the constancy of perception) shows temperament does not determine such traits; external factors are ruled out because children are too young to have been substantially influenced. Therefore, the cause must be the essences of the souls themselves, which differ inherently.

Key Terms

الأخلاق والأفعال
جواهر النفوس
أمزجة الأبدان
الأسباب الخارجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that education and temperament can influence character later in life.
Correction: The argument only asserts that the initial diversity present before significant external influence is due to soul essences; it does not deny that later factors can modify character.

Checkpoint

A) Because children are not taught by teachers
B) Because the differences appear before significant external influence
C) Because teachers have no effect
D) Because all children are naturally good

Key Takeaway
The initial diversity of moral character in young children points directly to distinct innate soul essences as the cause, not temperament or environment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Fifth Proof: Differences in Intellectual Abilities
Fifth proof: varying cognitive abilities point to soul essence as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the variation in intellectual abilities points to the soul as the original principle of knowledge, rather than bodily temperaments.

Core Idea
Your intellect's strengths aren't just about your brain — they point to the soul.

In this passage, Sanusi presents his fifth argument for the existence of the soul as a distinct substance. He observes that people's intellectual abilities vary in ways that cannot be explained by bodily temperaments alone. This leads him to conclude that the soul itself is the source of these differences, with the body serving only as its instrument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an observation, an example, and a conclusion.
The Arabic uses rhetorical questions to strengthen the argument.
2
Identify the empirical observation
Find the statement about souls differing in many states. Understand that this is the starting point of the proof.
3
Follow the reasoning to the conclusion
Trace how the author goes from the example to the claim that attributing differences to the soul is more appropriate than to temperaments.
Pay attention to the phrase 'especially since necessary knowledge holds...' which adds weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إنا نرى أن النفوس تختلف في أحوال كثيرة ، ولا يمكن تعليل ذلك الاختلاف ، باختلاف الأمزجة مثل : إن بعض النفوس قد تقوى على تحصيل علم دقيق غامض، وتعجز عن تحصيل علم سهل ، كالنحو والتصريف، بل قد يختلف حال الفهم في مسائل العلم الواحد ، فقد يسهل تعلم الباب الواحد من علم ، ويصعب تعلم الباب الثاني . ومن تأمل في أحوال الناس ، عرف أن الأمر كذلك . والنفس هي الأصل ، والبدن محل تصرفها ، ومنزل عملها . وإحالة هذه الأحوال المختلفة على المبدأ الأصلي ، أولى من إحالتها على اختلاف الآلات والأدوات ، لا سيما والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنه يبعد أن يقال : السبب في كون الإنسان بحيث يفهم المنطق ، أسهل ، والإلهي أصعب : هو وقوع التفاوت في الأمزجة
The fifth proof: We see that souls differ in many states, and that difference cannot be explained by differences in temperaments. For example, some souls may be capable of acquiring a subtle, deep science but incapable of acquiring an easy science like grammar and morphology. Rather, the state of understanding may differ within the very questions of a single science: learning one chapter of a science may be easy, and learning the second chapter may be difficult. Whoever reflects on the states of people knows that this is the case. The soul is the origin, the body is its place of action and the abode of its work. Attributing these different states to the original principle is more appropriate than attributing them to differences in instruments and tools, especially since necessary knowledge holds that it is far-fetched to say: the reason why a person finds logic easier and theology harder is due to the occurrence of variation in temperaments.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This is the fifth proof. It observes that souls differ in intellectual abilities in ways that cannot be explained by bodily temperaments. For example, one person may master a subtle science yet struggle with a simple one like grammar. Even within a single science, some chapters are easy and others hard. Since the soul is the origin (al-asl) of knowledge and the body is merely its place of action, it is more logical to attribute these differences to the soul itself. It is self-evident (al-`ilm al-daruri) that saying 'logic is easier because of temperament' is far-fetched.

Key Terms
النفوس (souls)
العلم (knowledge)
الأمزجة (temperaments)
الآلات والأدوات (instruments and tools)
المبدأ الأصلي (original principle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That intellectual aptitude is solely determined by physical constitution (temperament).
Correction: The author argues that temperament cannot explain the specific, non-uniform differences in ability across fields within the same person, so the soul must be the cause.

Checkpoint

A) Children's differing morals.
B) Animals' differing natures.
C) A person who can learn logic easily but finds theology difficult.
D) The same person being good at both grammar and theology.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual differences are best explained by the nature of the soul, not the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you excel in a subject that others find difficult, do you credit your physical brain or do you sense something deeper within you that makes it possible? How does this proof shift your perspective on your own abilities?
?

?

The Unity of the Soul: Aristotle vs. Galen
States the thesis of the soul's unity and presents Galen's opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing views on the soul—Aristotle's concept of one soul with multiple powers and Galen's theory of three separate souls—as the foundation for the subsequent proofs of the soul's unity.

Core Idea
The soul is either one with many powers or three distinct entities—this is the core debate.

This lesson introduces a foundational debate in Islamic philosophy: is the soul a single, unified substance that expresses itself through various powers, or is it composed of three independent souls located in different organs? The passage presents Aristotle's monistic view and Galen's pluralistic view, setting the stage for the arguments that will follow. Understanding these positions is essential before examining the evidence for the soul's unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice that the author declares Aristotle's view as 'the truth about which there is no doubt' before presenting Galen's opposing theory.
This sets a polemical tone—the author sides with Aristotle.
2
Identify the two views
Identify Aristotle's view: the soul is one and from it emanate many powers. Identify Galen's view: three souls (rational, irascible, appetitive) each with a specific locus (brain, heart, liver).
Write them down for clarity.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why the location of the soul matters. Think about how each view explains human behavior and internal conflict. The passage suggests that there is evidence for unity, which will be presented next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في بيان أن النفس واحدة. ذهب (أرسطاطاليس) وأصحابه: إلى أن النفس واحدة وتنبعث منها قوى مختلفة كثيرة، بحسب الأفعال المختلفة. وهذا هو الحق الذي لا شك فيه. وقال (جالينوس): النفوس الثلاثة: النفس النطقية. ومتعلقها الدماغ. والنفس الغضبية. ومتعلقها القلب. والنفس الشهوانية. ومتعلقها الكبد. والذي يدل على أن لكل إنسان نفساً واحدة وجوه:
The third chapter on explaining that the soul is one. Aristotle and his followers held that the soul is one and from it emanate many different powers, according to different actions. And this is the truth about which there is no doubt. Galen said: The three souls: the rational soul, its locus is the brain. The irascible soul, its locus is the heart. And the appetitive soul, its locus is the liver. And what indicates that each human has one soul are [several] proofs:
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the unity of the soul. It presents two opposing positions: Aristotle and his followers maintain that the soul is one indivisible entity from which multiple powers emanate according to different actions, and they affirm this as unquestionable truth. In contrast, Galen posits three distinct souls—rational, irascible, and appetitive—each located in a different organ (brain, heart, liver). The passage then transitions to the evidence for the soul's unity, announcing that proofs will follow. The author thus sets up the debate: is the soul one substance with multiple faculties, or are there multiple independent souls?

Key Terms
نفس
قوى
نفس نطقية
نفس غضبية
نفس شهوانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Galen's view is merely a medical theory about brain, heart, and liver functions, not a philosophical claim about the soul's nature.
Correction: In this context, Galen's view is presented as a philosophical opponent to the unity of the soul, asserting that there are three independent souls, not just organs with different functions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Aristotle and his followers on the soul?
A) The soul is three separate souls located in different organs.
B) The soul is one, and from it emanate many different powers.
C) The soul is composite and divisible.
D) The soul is purely material.

Key Takeaway
Aristotle holds the soul is one with multiple powers; Galen holds three distinct souls in different organs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel anger or desire rising, do you experience it as a foreign invasion from a separate part of you, or as an expression of your single self? How does the answer to that question affect how you take responsibility for those feelings?
?
Aristotle's view demands that you integrate your anger and desire into your unified self and train them as one team. Are you living as if your soul is fragmented, blaming different parts for your failures? What specific step can you take today to act as if your soul is truly one?
?
If the soul is one, then no part of you is a scapegoat. You cannot say 'my anger made me do it' or 'my desire led me astray' as if they were not really you. How would your life change if you stopped treating your emotions as separate agents and owned them completely as your own actions?

The Self-Referential 'I' Is One: A Proof from Immediate Knowledge
First proof: the soul is the 'I' known by immediate intuition to be one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof for the unity of the soul relies on the immediate knowledge (العلم البديهي) that the 'I' is one, and why the question of composition is set aside.

Core Idea
Your immediate awareness of 'I' proves the soul is one.

The author begins with a powerful argument from self-awareness. He claims that everyone's reference to their own 'I' is immediately known to be a single entity, without needing to examine whether it is simple or composite. This lesson unpacks that argument and its modest scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact style and the logical flow: claim, objection, response.
2
Understand the key claim about immediate knowledge
The author states that what is meant by 'soul' is what each person refers to as 'I'. He then asserts that immediate knowledge (العلم البديهي) tells us this 'I' is one, without any multiplicity. Reflect on your own self-awareness: do you directly know your 'I' as one or as many?
Immediate knowledge is foundational in Islamic theology; it does not require proof.
3
See how the objection is handled
An imaginary objector asks: why could the 'I' not be a composite of many things? The author's response is that the proof does not need to answer this; the immediate knowledge only gives us oneness. The question of composition is a separate inquiry.
This shows the proof's limited scope: it establishes unity, not simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى: إن المراد بالنفس ما يشير إليه كل أحد بقوله «أنا»، والعلم البديهي حاصل بأن ذلك الشيء واحد، ليس فيه تعدد البتة. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله (أنا) وإن كان واحداً، إلا أن ذلك الواحد يكون مركباً من أشياء كثيرة؟ قلنا: إنه لا حاجة بنا في هذا المقام إلى إبطال هذا السؤال. بل نقول: المشار إليه بقولي (أنا) معلوم بالضرورة: أنه شيء واحد. فأما أن ذلك الواحد، هل هو واحد مركب من أشياء كثيرة، أو هو في نفسه واحد، وحدة حقيقية، فلا حاجة إليه في هذا المقام.
The first proof: That what is meant by the soul is what everyone points to by saying 'I', and it is obtained by immediate knowledge that that thing is one, having no multiplicity at all. For if it is said: 'Why is it not permissible to say: that which is pointed to by everyone in saying 'I', even if it is one, except that that one is composed of many things?' We say: 'We have no need in this context to refute this question. Rather we say: that which is pointed to by my saying 'I' is known necessarily to be one thing. As for whether that one is a composite of many things or is in itself one with a true unity, there is no need for that in this context.'
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the unity of the soul. It begins by identifying the soul with the referent of each person's utterance 'I', which is known by immediate intuition (العلم البديهي) to be a single thing without any multiplicity. An objection is raised: why can't that 'I' be a composite of many things? The answer is that in this context it is unnecessary to refute that possibility. The immediate knowledge only informs us that the referent is one; whether it is simple or composite is a separate question that does not affect the proof. The argument thus relies on the undeniable unity of self-awareness, setting aside metaphysical complications.

Key Terms
نفس
أنا
علم بديهي
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is incomplete because it does not rule out composition of the soul.
Correction: The author deliberately sets aside the question of composition; the proof only establishes unity from immediate self-awareness, not simplicity or complexity.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof rely on as the basis for the soul's unity?
A) A logical syllogism from the definition of the soul.
B) Immediate knowledge (العلم البديهي) that the 'I' is one.
C) Empirical observation of brain functions.
D) The authority of Aristotle.

Key Takeaway
The unity of the soul is known by immediate self-awareness, not by analytical composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own 'I', do you experience it as a single unified reality, or do you feel pulled in different directions by your thoughts, desires, and emotions? How does this immediate knowledge of unity call you to integrate your inner life?
?
The proof sets aside the question of whether the self is composite. But in your daily actions, do you treat your soul as one thing accountable to Allah, or as a collection of competing parts? What would change if you fully owned the unity of your 'I' in your worship and character?
?
Is there a moment today when you acted as though your 'I' was fragmented—e.g., excusing a sin by saying 'part of me wanted to, part didn't'? How does the immediate knowledge of your soul's unity challenge you to take full responsibility for your choices?

Second Proof: Anger and Desire Require Perception
The second proof shows that anger and desire both require perception, so they are attributes of one essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that anger, desire, and perception must belong to a single essence.

Core Idea
Anger and desire cannot function without perception, so they share the same source.

This lesson presents the second of several proofs for the unity of the soul. The proof argues that both anger and desire, as psychological states aimed at repelling harm or seeking benefit, require awareness of what is harmful or beneficial. Since the same awareness is needed for both, the same entity must be the subject of all three attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the proof unfold in the author's own words.
Notice the key terms: غضب, شهوة, إدراك, شعور.
2
Understand the definitions
Anger is defined as a state for repelling harm, desire as a state for seeking benefit. These are intentional actions.
3
Grasp the premise: awareness is necessary
The proof states that repelling harm and seeking benefit require awareness (شعور) of what is harmful or agreeable. Without awareness, intentional action cannot occur.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since the one who gets angry must perceive, and the one who desires must perceive, perception, anger, and desire are three attributes of one essence, not three separate essences.
5
Reflect on the significance
This counters the view that anger and desire are independent faculties. They are unified through the same perceiving substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن الغضب حالة نفسانية تحدث عند محاولة دفع المنافي، والشهوة حالة نفسانية تحدث عند طلب الملائم، ثم من المعلوم بالضرورة [أن دفع المنافي، وطلب الملائم مشروط بحصول الشعور بكون ذلك الشيء] منافياً أو ملائماً. فالقوة الغضبية التي هي قوة دافعة للمنافي إن لم يكن لها شعور، يكون ذلك الشيء منافياً، امتنع كونها دافعة لذلك المنافي على سبيل الاختيار والقصد. لأن القصد إلى الجلب تارة وإلى الدفع أخرى، مشروط بالشعور بذلك الشيء. فثبت: أن الذي يغضب لا بد وأن يكون هو بعينه مدركاً. والذي يشتهي لا بد وأن يكون هو بعينه مدركاً. فثبت بهذا البرهان القاطع: أن الإدراك والغضب والشهوة: صفات ثلاثة لذات واحدة، ويمتنع كونها صفات ثلاثة لذوات ثلاثة [متباينة].
The second proof: Anger is a psychological state occurring when attempting to repel what is harmful, and desire is a psychological state occurring when seeking what is agreeable. Then it is known necessarily [that repelling the harmful and seeking the agreeable are conditioned by the occurrence of awareness that that thing] is harmful or agreeable. So if the irascible power, which is a power repelling the harmful, has no awareness that that thing is harmful, it is impossible for it to repel that harmful thing deliberately and intentionally. Because intention to bring [something] at times and to repel at other times is conditioned by awareness of that thing. Thus it is established that the one who gets angry must be, himself, perceiving. And the one who desires must be, himself, perceiving. So it is established by this conclusive proof that perception, anger, and desire are three attributes of one essence, and it is impossible for them to be three attributes of three [separate] essences.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the unity of the soul. It begins by defining anger and desire as psychological states aimed at repelling harm and seeking benefit respectively. The key premise is that both actions require awareness: one cannot intentionally repel what one does not perceive as harmful, nor seek what one does not perceive as agreeable. Therefore, the power that gets angry must also be a perceiving power, and the power that desires must also be perceiving. Hence, the same entity that perceives is the one that gets angry and desires. The conclusion is that perception, anger, and desire are three attributes of one single essence, not three attributes of three separate essences. This is presented as a conclusive proof (برهان قاطع).

Misconception: Anger and desire are independent faculties that do not rely on perception.
Correction: The proof shows that both require awareness, so they cannot be independent; they must belong to the same perceiving substance.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Perception, anger, and desire are three attributes of one single essence, not three separate essences.

Third Proof: Mutual Interference of Faculties
Third proof for unity of soul using mutual prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that mutual interference between faculties demonstrates they are not independent substances but attributes of a single soul.

Core Idea
If anger and desire prevent each other, they cannot be separate substances.

This lesson examines the third proof from the passage, which uses observation of mutual prevention between emotions to argue for the unity of the soul. The proof hinges on a rational principle about independent substances: they cannot interfere with each other's actions. By applying this principle to anger and desire, the author shows that they must be attributes of one substance, not separate entities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Read the first part: if two substances are independent, one's occupation cannot prevent the other's. This is a logical premise.
The principle is based on the definition of independence.
2
Apply the principle to the soul
Consider the hypothetical: thought, anger, desire as separate substances. Then anger should not prevent desire, and vice versa.
3
Observe the contradiction
In reality, intense desire prevents anger. That contradicts the consequence of separate substances.
This is an empirical observation.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, they are not independent principles but different attributes of one substance. The occupation of that substance with one action prevents another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إنا إذا فرضنا جوهرين مستقلين، يكون كل واحد منهما مستقلاً بفعله الخاص به، امتنع أن يكون اشتغال أحدهما بفعله الخاص به مانعاً للآخر من الاشتغال بفعله الخاص به. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو كان محل الفكر جوهراً، ومحل الغضب جوهراً ثانياً، ومحل الشهوة جوهراً ثالثاً، إلى آخره، وجب أن يكون اشتغال القوة الغضبية بفعلها مانعاً للقوة الشهوانية [من الاشتغال] بفعلها، ولا بالعكس. لكن التالي باطل. فإن اشتغال الإنسان بالشهوة، وانصبابه إليها يمنعه من الاشتغال بالغضب والانصباب إليه، فعلمنا: أن هذه الأمور الثلاثة ليست مبادىء مستقلة، بل هي صفات مختلفة لجوهر واحد، فلا جرم كان اشتغال ذلك الجوهر بأحد هذه الأفعال مانعاً له عن الاشتغال بالفعل الآخر.
The third proof: If we suppose two independent substances, each one being independent in its own action, it is impossible that the occupation of one with its own action should prevent the other from occupying itself with its own action. If this is established, we say: If the locus of thought were a substance, the locus of anger a second substance, and the locus of desire a third substance, and so on, then it would be necessary that the occupation of the irascible power with its action would prevent the appetitive power [from occupying itself] with its action, and not the contrary. But the consequent is false. For the occupation of the human with desire and his inclination toward it prevents him from occupying himself with anger and inclining toward it. So we know that these three things are not independent principles, but rather they are different attributes of one substance. Therefore, the occupation of that substance with one of these actions prevents it from occupying itself with the other action.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the unity of the soul. It begins with a general principle: if two substances are independent, the activity of one cannot prevent the activity of the other, because each operates independently. Applying this to the soul, suppose that the locus of thought, anger, and desire are three separate substances. Then the activity of anger should not prevent desire, and vice versa. However, we observe in experience that when a person is intensely occupied with desire, they are prevented from being angry, and vice versa. Therefore, the premise that they are separate substances is false. Consequently, anger, desire, and thought are not independent principles but different attributes of one single substance. The occupation of that substance with one action prevents it from another, which is exactly what we observe.

فعل
مانع
اشتغال
مبادئ مستقلة
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mutual prevention does not prove unity.
Correction: The proof relies on the impossibility of one independent substance interfering with another; if they were separate, they would not prevent each other, but they do, so they cannot be separate.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Mutual interference between faculties proves they are not independent substances but attributes of one soul.

?

Or will you admit that you, as a whole, chose to act? What concrete step will you take today to unify your soul's actions toward what pleases your Creator?

Author summarises a decisive proof for unity of the agent.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
A single soul perceives all and acts all—dispelling the myth of multiple agents.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful proof that the agent of all perception and action must be one soul. The author references an earlier decisive argument, reinforcing the unity of the divine attributes. Understanding this proof guards against a common theological error.

The proof asserts that the perceiver of all perceptibles and the capable, willing agent must be one soul.

The Brain, the Heart, and the Source of Sound
Presents three proofs that the brain produces sound and a refutation affirming the heart as the real cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three empirical arguments that the brain is the agent of sound, and the counter-argument that the heart is the true cause via the exit of breath.

Core Idea
Sound’s true cause is the heart, not the brain; the brain is merely instrumental.

In this passage, the author presents a debate about the biological origin of sound. The opponent offers three observations to prove that the brain produces sound, but the author responds by distinguishing between instrumental and real causes. Understanding this debate clarifies the kalam method of reasoning about causation in the human body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two main parts: the opponent's three arguments and the author's refutation.
2
Identify the three arguments for the brain
The opponent presents three distinct observations. Find each one and note the anatomical details used: larynx, muscles, nerves, abdominal muscles, and brain exposure experiments.
These are empirical arguments based on bodily observations.
3
Examine the refutation
The author responds by reinterpreting the evidence. He asserts that the real cause of sound is the exit of breath, which originates from the heart. The brain is only needed for the physical apparatus.
This is a classic distinction between primary cause and instrumental cause.
4
Distinguish real cause vs. instrument
Reflect on how the author distinguishes between the 'real cause' (al-sabab al-ḥaqīqī) and that which is 'needed' (muḥtāj ilayhi). This is a key conceptual tool in kalam.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider how this argument supports the view that the heart, not the brain, is the seat of the soul or the expelling power (al-quwwa al-nāqiṭa). This connects to broader theological discussions.
This lesson shows how kalam engages with empirical observations to defend a metaphysical position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا جرم يبطل الصوت. فثبت: أن الآلة لحدوث الصوت هي الحنجرة. والحنجرة مؤلفة من ثلاثة غضاريف، وهذه الغضاريف إنما تتحرك بعضلات كثيرة، وتلك العضلات إنما تتحرك بالأعصاب والأعصاب ثابتة من الدماغ. فثبت: أن فاعل الصوت هو الدماغ.
والوجه الثاني في بيان أن فاعل الصوت هو الدماغ: إنا نشاهد عضل البطن يتمدد عند التصويت بالصوت العنيف. وأما القلب، فإنه لا يناله التعب عند التصويت.
الثالث: إن القلب إذا كشف عنه ثم قبض عليه، فإنه لا يبطل من الحيوان صوته، وإن كشف عن الدماغ ثم ضغط، بطل في الحال صوت ذلك الحيوان.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة: أن مبدأ الصوت هو الدماغ، لا القلب. وعند هذا البيان يصير هذا الكلام حجة واضحة على صحة أن محل القوة الناقطة هو الدماغ.
والجواب: إنا بينا أن سبب حدوث الصوت: خروج النفس. وبينا: أن سبب خروج النفس هو القلب، فلزم القطع بأن السبب الحقيقي لحدوث الصوت هو القلب. وأما الوجوه التي ذكرتم، فهي تدل على أن الدماغ محتاج إليه في حدوث الصوت، وفي تكوين آلات حدوث الصوت، وذلك لا يقدح في قولنا.
Without a doubt, sound ceases. So it is established: that the instrument for the occurrence of sound is the larynx. And the larynx is composed of three cartilages, and these cartilages only move by many muscles, and those muscles only move by nerves, and the nerves are fixed from the brain. So it is established: that the agent of sound is the brain. The second aspect in explaining that the agent of sound is the brain: we observe that the abdominal muscle stretches when vocalizing with a violent sound. As for the heart, it does not undergo fatigue during vocalization. The third: when the heart is uncovered and then gripped, it does not nullify the animal's sound; but when the brain is uncovered and then pressed, that animal's sound is nullified immediately. So it is established by these three aspects: that the origin of sound is the brain, not the heart. And by this explanation, this statement becomes a clear proof for the validity that the locus of the expelling power is the brain. The answer: We have shown that the cause of the occurrence of sound is the exit of breath. And we have shown that the cause of the exit of breath is the heart, so it is necessary to conclude that the real cause of the occurrence of sound is the heart. As for the aspects you mentioned, they indicate that the brain is needed for the occurrence of sound and for the formation of the instruments of the occurrence of sound, and that does not harm our statement.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents three empirical arguments for the brain as the source of sound, followed by a counter-argument that affirms the heart as the true cause. The opponent first argues: (1) the larynx, which produces sound, is moved by muscles activated by nerves from the brain; (2) during loud vocalization, the abdominal muscle (connected to the brain) tires, but the heart does not; (3) exposing and pressing the brain stops sound, but doing the same to the heart does not. These lead to the conclusion that the brain is the origin of sound and the seat of the 'expelling power' (al-quwwa al-nāqiṭa). The author then responds: the real cause of sound is the exit of breath, which is caused by the heart. The brain is merely an instrument needed for the physical apparatus of sound production. The opponent's arguments only prove the brain's instrumental role, not that it is the primary cause. Thus the heart remains the principal origin.

القلب
القوة الناقطة

الحنجرة
العضلات
الأعصاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The brain is the direct cause of sound because it controls the vocal muscles.
Correction: The passage shows that the heart is the real cause through the exit of breath; the brain is only instrumental in moving the laryngeal muscles.

Checkpoint

B) The larynx

Key Takeaway
Sound originates from the heart via breath; the brain is a necessary instrument but not the primary cause.

Seventh Argument: The Heart's Central Location as Ruler
Seventh proof: heart's centrality as basis for rulership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the heart's central position in the body serves as a rational proof (ḥujja) for its role as the absolute ruler (al-raʾīs al-muṭlaq) over the body, based on equitable distribution of powers.

Core Idea
Centrality proves sovereignty: the heart rules because it is placed at the center.

This lesson examines the seventh rational proof presented by the author, which argues for the heart's supremacy over the brain by appealing to its central position. The argument uses an analogy with political rule: the absolute ruler should be centrally located to ensure fair distribution of commands. We'll explore how this teleological reasoning establishes the heart as the 'king' of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice how the passage contrasts the heart and brain positions.
2
Identify key premises
Identify the two main premises: (1) the heart is centrally located, (2) centrality is befitting for an absolute ruler to allow equitable distribution of powers. Also note the conclusion that the heart is more deserving than the brain.
The argument assumes that equitable distribution requires a central source.
3
Reflect on the argument's logic
Consider why the brain's upper position is less suited. Think about the teleological nature: this is not a physiological claim but a rational argument about optimal design.
This argument is part of a larger debate about which organ is the ruler of the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: إن القلب موضوع في موضع يقرب أن يكون وسطاً من البدن، وهذا هو اللائق بالرئيس المطلق، حتى يكون ما ينبعث منه من القوى وأصلاً إلى جميع أطراف البدن، على القسمة العادلة. والدماغ موضوع في أعلى البدن، فكان القلب أولى من الدماغ في أن يكون ملكاً للبدن على الإطلاق.
The seventh proof: The heart is placed in a position that is nearly central in the body, and this is befitting for the absolute ruler, so that what emanates from it of powers and origin reaches all parts of the body in equitable distribution. And the brain is placed in the upper part of the body, so the heart is more deserving than the brain to be the absolute king of the body.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument (ḥujja) based on analogy with political rule. The heart is located near the center of the body, which is appropriate for an absolute ruler (al-raʾīs al-muṭlaq) because from a central position, the powers and principles that emanate from it can be distributed equally to all limbs. In contrast, the brain is located at the top of the body, which is less suited for equitable distribution. Therefore, the heart is more qualified to be the sovereign of the body. The argument assumes that the body's functional organization mirrors political hierarchy, and centrality implies superiority.

Key Terms
القلب
الدماغ
الرئيس المطلق
البدن
القوى
القسمة العادلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument is a physiological claim that the heart physically pumps blood to all parts equally, or that the brain is unimportant.
Correction: This is not a physiological claim but a teleological argument about optimal design; it does not deny the brain's importance but ranks the heart higher in ruling function based on central location.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The heart is more deserving to be the absolute king of the body because its central position allows equitable distribution of powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart is designed to be the central ruler of your body, distributing spiritual life to every action. But in the chaos of your day, which parts of your life are you allowing to usurp its sovereignty—your intellect, your desires, or your fears? What would it take today to restore your heart to its rightful throne?
?
The argument says a ruler in the center ensures equitable distribution. Reflect: is your heart's love, mercy, and faith reaching every limb—your hands, your tongue, your feet—fairly? Or are some parts starved while others are overindulged? Where is the imbalance, and what one thing can you do today to correct it?
?
You cannot be a mere 'brain'—logical but disconnected. The heart rules because it is central, not distant. Is your faith lived from a cold, detached intellect, or from a heart that pulses through every decision? Right now, are you governed by your heart's conviction or by scattered thoughts? Choose one area to submit back to the heart's rule before you sleep tonight.

Eighth Argument: The Heart as Seat of Intelligence
Uses common speech attributing intelligence to the heart, with a response limiting Galen's remark to anger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how common speech about the heart is used as a proof for its cognitive role, and how Galen's observation is refuted.

Core Idea
Common speech calls the heart intelligent—and Galen's point about courage doesn't change that.

This lesson examines an argument from everyday language: people attribute intelligence and dullness to the heart, not the brain. The author then addresses a potential objection from Galen, showing that his remark about courage does not disprove the heart's role in understanding. Follow along as we unpack this concise theological proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the three parts: the common speech claim, Galen's observation, and the response.
2
Identify the common speech argument
The argument begins by noting that people describe hearts as intelligent or dull. This suggests the heart is the seat of cognitive faculties.
This is a linguistic proof from everyday usage.
3
Analyze the response to Galen
Galen's statement is presented as a possible counterargument. The response shows that Galen's point only associates the heart with anger, not that it cannot also be the place of understanding.
The response does not reject Galen but limits his observation.

The argument thus remains: common speech supports the heart's role in cognition, and Galen's remark does not refute it. The phrase 'wa-llāhu aʿlam' concludes the discussion.

The Subtlety of 'Or' in Qaf 50:37 and the Division of Intellects
Analysis of Qaf 50:37 with a classification of intellects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quran uses 'or' (أو) instead of 'and' in Qaf 50:37, and how this choice reveals a classification of intellectual powers into two types.

Core Idea
The verse's 'or' divides intellects: the perfect need no teaching, others need learning and logic.

This lesson unpacks a subtle linguistic and theological point. The verse Qaf 50:37 says 'or' (أو), but one might expect 'and' (الواو) since both a heart and attentive listening seem necessary. The author resolves this by showing that 'or' distinguishes two types of intellect: one so perfect it needs no teaching, and another that requires learning and the safeguard of logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the objection
Read the verse and understand why one might think 'and' would be more appropriate. The objection is that both a heart and giving ear are necessary.
2
Learn the classification of intellectual powers
The author divides intellectual powers into two types: one perfect, self-sufficient, and rare; the other needing learning and the 'artificial law' of logic.
The first type has abundant premises and fast composition.
3
Connect the classification to the verse
Notice that 'heart' (قلب) refers to the first type, and it is indefinite to convey perfection. 'Giving ear' refers to the second type. Thus 'or' appropriately separates the two.
This resolves the apparent problem.
4
Reflect on the subtlety
Appreciate how the author uses a linguistic detail to reveal a deep theological truth about human intellects.

The second proof: His saying, Exalted: 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart or gives ear while he is a witness.' This verse clearly indicates that the place of remembrance and understanding is the heart. Know that in this verse there is a wonderful subtlety, and its explanation is accomplished by presenting a question. For it is said: The conjunctive 'and' would be more appropriate in His saying 'or gives ear' because the heart is the place of perception of realities, and 'giving ear' is the effort and striving to acquire those perceptions and cognitions, and it is known that both matters are necessary. Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the verse Qaf 50:37. It begins by stating that the verse clearly indicates the heart as the place of understanding. Then a subtle objection is raised: why does the verse use 'or' (aw) instead of 'and' (wa), since both having a heart and giving ear are necessary? The answer explains that the intellectual powers are of two types: one perfect and independent, requiring no teaching, and the other in need of learning and logical methodology. The verse's phrase 'whoever has a heart' refers to the first type, indicated by the indefinite form for perfection. The second type is implied by 'gives ear.' Thus 'or' appropriately divides the two cases. The classification is elaborated with examples: the first type has abundant intuitive premises and fast composition, while the second needs the 'artificial law' (logic) to avoid error.
