The Disjunctive 'Or' and Two Types of Rational Faculties
The author responds to an objection about the conjunctive waw and explains the two types of rational faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disjunctive 'or' is used in the Quranic verse to distinguish two types of rational faculties: the perfect holy soul and the ordinary learning soul.

Core Idea
The single letter 'or' in the Quran divides humanity into two spiritual classes.

This lesson examines a grammatical objection about the conjunction in a Quranic verse and the profound answer that reveals two kinds of rational faculties. The author defends the disjunctive 'or' over 'and' by showing that the verse refers to two distinct paths to knowledge: one intuitive and one acquired.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, carefully read the passage to understand the grammatical objection raised: why use 'or' instead of 'and'? Then see the author's counter-argument that the disjunctive 'or' is actually more appropriate.
Notice the author's confidence in his answer.
2
Understand the two types of rational faculties
The author divides rational faculties into two kinds: one perfect and rare (holy soul), and one ordinary that needs learning and logic. Grasp the difference in quantity and quality of premises.
The first type is compared to a 'holy soul' that knows without learning.
3
Apply to the Quranic verse
See how the author maps 'heart' to the first type and 'gives ear' to the second type. The indefinite 'heart' indicates perfection. The rarity of the first explains why the Quran generally commands seeking.
The author connects this to the science of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بتقديم سؤال. فإنه يقال: إن الواو العاطفة التي بقوله: (أو ألقى السمع) لأن القلب عبارة عن محل إدراك الحقائق، وإلقاء السمع عبارة عن الجد والاجتهاد في تحصيل تلك الإدراكات والمعارف، ومعلوم: أنه لا بد من الأمرين معاً، فكان ذكر الواو العاطفة ههنا أولى من ذكره أو. والجواب: إنا نقول: بل أو القاسمة أولى ههنا من الواو العاطفة، وبيانه: أن القوى العقلية قسمان: منها ما يكون في غاية الكمال والشرف والإشراق، ويكون مخالفاً لسائر القوى العقلية بالكم والكيف. أما الكم فلأن حصول المقدمات البديهية والحسية والتجريبية بها أكثر، وأما الكيف فلأن تركيب تلك المقدمات على وجه ينساق إلى تلك النتائج أسهل وأسرع. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: مثل هذه النفس القدسية تستغني في معرفة حقائق الأشياء عن التعلم والاستعانة بالغير، إلا أن مثل هذا يكون في غاية الندرة. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو الذي لا يكون كذلك، فهو يحتاج في اكتساب العلوم النظرية إلى التعلم، والاستعانة بالغير، والتمسك بالقانون الصناعي الذي يعصمه عن الزلل. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: قوله تعالى: (إن في ذلك لذكرى لمن كان له قلب): إشارة إلى القسم الأول، وإنما ذكر القلب بلفظ التنكير ليدل ذلك على الكمال التام، بدليل قوله تعالى: (ولتجدنهم أحرص الناس على حياة) أي حياة عظيمة طويلة المدة، فكذا ههنا قوله: (لمن كان له قلب) أي لمن كان له قلب كامل في قوة الإدراك، عظيم الدرجة في الاستعداد والوقوف على عالم القدس. وأما قوله: (أو ألقي السمع وهو شهيد): فهو إشارة إلى القسم الثاني، وهو الذي يفتقر إلى الكسب والاستعانة بالغير، وهذا من الأسرار التي عليها بناء علم المنطق. وقد لاح في درج هذه الآية. ولما كان القسم الأول نادراً جداً، وكان الغالب هو القسم الثاني، لا جرم أمر الكل في أكثر الآيات بالطلب والاكتساب فقال: (أفلم يسيروا في الأرض، فتكون لهم قلوب يعقلون بها، أو آذان يسمعون بها). وقال صاحب المنطق: إن القسم الأول، وإن كان غنياً عن الاستعانة بالمنطق، إلا أنه نادر جداً، والغلبة للقسم الثاني، وكلهم محتاجون إلى المنطق. فانظر إلى هذه الأسرار العميقة، كيف تجدها في الألفاظ القرآنية.
With the presentation of a question: Indeed it is said: The conjunctive waw (waw al-'atifah) that is with His saying 'or he gives ear' (aw alqa al-sam'), because the heart (qalb) is the locus of perceiving realities, and giving ear (ilqa' al-sam') is earnestness and striving in acquiring those perceptions and knowledges, and it is known that both matters are necessary. So mentioning the conjunctive waw here is more appropriate than mentioning 'or' (aw). The answer: We say: Rather the disjunctive 'or' (aw al-qasimah) is more appropriate here than the conjunctive waw. Its explanation: The rational faculties are of two kinds: one of them is that which is in the utmost perfection, nobility, and illumination, and is different from other rational faculties in quantity (kam) and quality (kayf). As for quantity, because the attainment of self-evident, sensory, and experiential premises with it is more. As for quality, because the composition of those premises in a way that leads to those conclusions is easier and quicker. If you understand this, we say: Such a holy soul (nafs qudsiyyah) is independent in knowing the realities of things from learning and seeking assistance from others, except that such a thing is extremely rare. As for the second kind: which is not like that, it needs in acquiring theoretical sciences to learning, seeking assistance from others, and adhering to the artificial law (al-qanun al-sina'i) that protects it from error. If you understand this, we say: His saying (Exalted): 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' is an indication of the first kind. And He mentioned 'heart' with the indefinite form (al-tankir) to indicate complete perfection, as evidenced by His saying: 'And you will surely find them the most eager of people for life' - meaning a great long-lasting life. So likewise here His saying: 'for whoever has a heart' means whoever has a heart perfect in the power of perception, great in degree of preparedness and standing upon the world of sanctity. As for His saying: 'or gives ear while he is a witness' - it is an indication of the second kind, which is in need of acquisition (al-kasb) and seeking assistance from others. And this is among the secrets upon which the science of logic (al-mantiq) is built. And it has appeared in the context of this verse. And since the first kind is extremely rare, and the predominant is the second kind, inevitably He commanded all in most verses to seek and acquire, saying: 'Have they not traveled in the land to have hearts with which to understand or ears with which to hear?' And the author of logic said: The first kind, although it is independent of seeking help from logic, is extremely rare, and the predominance is for the second kind, and all are in need of logic. So look at these profound secrets, how you find them in Quranic expressions.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage addresses a grammatical objection regarding the conjunction in a Quranic verse and provides a deep theological answer. The objector argues that the conjunctive waw (meaning 'and') should be used instead of 'or' because both the heart and giving ear are necessary. The author answers that the disjunctive 'or' is actually more appropriate because it distinguishes two different types of rational faculties. He then divides rational faculties into two categories: one perfect and rare (the holy soul that grasps truths directly without learning), and the other ordinary, which requires effort, learning, and logical methodology. He then applies this to the verse: 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart' refers to the first type, while 'or gives ear while he is a witness' refers to the second type. The rarity of the first type explains why the Quran generally commands seeking and traveling. The mention of the author of logic (likely Avicenna or al-Farabi) emphasizes that even the first type, though independent of logic, is extremely rare, so logic is necessary for the majority. This shows the profound secrets in Quranic wording.

Correction: The verse distinguishes two different types of rational souls: one that directly perceives truths (heart) and another that needs to listen and learn (giving ear). Both are necessary but for different people.

First Proof: Individuation Leads to Infinite Regress
First proof: infinite regress from individuation as added attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that individuation is an added attribute leads to an infinite regress, and why the opponent's objection based on mutual composition fails.

Core Idea
If individuation is added, each individuation needs another, ad infinitum.

The author begins by rejecting the claim that multiplicity requires a distinct added attribute. He then presents the first proof: if individuation were an additional thing, then each individuation would itself require another individuation, leading to an infinite chain. Even if the opponent tries to escape by suggesting that two universal quiddities together produce particularity, the author shows this fails because universals remain universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and the literal translation. Notice the initial quote and the author's rejection.
2
Identify the core argument
The proof assumes individuation is an added attribute, then shows this forces each individuation to have another individuation, causing regress.
3
Examine the opponent's objection
The opponent suggests that two universal quiddities can mutually individuate each other. See how the author refutes this by noting universals remain universal.
This step is crucial to understand the dialectical style.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the opponent's explanation does not produce individuation. This establishes the first proof against added attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « إنه لا يحصل التعدد إلا لأجل معنى يوجب الإمتياز » . قلنا : هذا ممنوع . ويدل عليه وجوه :

الأول : إنه لو كان التعين أمراً زائداً على الذات والتعينات متساوية في ماهية أنها تعين ، وجب أن يكون تعين كل تعين زائداً عليه . ويلزم التسلسل . ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : ماهية السواد، وماهية التعين ، إذا انضمت كل واحدة منهما إلى الأخرى ، فإن كل واحدة منهما تقتضي تعين الأخرى ؟ لأنـا نقول : ماهية السواد ماهية كلية ، وماهية التعين أيضاً ماهية كلية ، والكل إذا تقيد بالكلي يبقى كلياً ، ولا يصير شخصياً . فثبت : أن القدر الذي ذكرتموه لا يوجب التعين .

الثاني : إن اختصاص هذا التعين بهذا المتعين دون ذاك ، واختصاص ذلك التعين بذلك المتعين دون هذا : مشروط بامتياز هذا التعين عن ذلك التعين . فلو كان ذلك الامتياز معللاً بحصول هذه الصفة في هذا ، وحصول تلك الصفة في ذلك ، لزم الدور ، وهو محال .

الثالث : لو كان تعين هذا المتعين زائداً عليه ، لكان هذا المتعين موجودين، لا موجوداً واحداً . ثم يكون لكل واحد منهما تعين آخر ، فتصير أربعة لا واحداً . وبهذا الطريق يلزم أن يكون الموجود الواحد غير واحد ، بل موجودات غير متناهية . وهو محال .
His statement: 'Multiplicity does not occur except due to a meaning that necessitates distinction.' We said: This is rejected. And evidence for that are several aspects:

First: If individuation were an additional thing over the essence, and individuations are equal in their quiddity of being individuation, then the individuation of every individuation would have to be additional to it, leading to infinite regress. And it cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say: the quiddity of blackness and the quiddity of individuation, when each of them is conjoined to the other, then each of them necessitates the individuation of the other? Because we say: The quiddity of blackness is a universal quiddity, and the quiddity of individuation is also a universal quiddity, and each when restricted by the universal remains universal and does not become particular. So it is established that the extent you mentioned does not necessitate individuation.

Second: The specification of this individuation to this individuated thing rather than that, and the specification of that individuation to that individuated thing rather than this, is conditioned by the distinction of this individuation from that individuation. If that distinction were caused by the occurrence of this attribute in this and the occurrence of that attribute in that, then circularity would be necessary, which is impossible.

Third: If the individuation of this individuated thing were additional to it, then this individuated thing would be two existents, not one existent. Then each of them would have another individuation, so they would become four, not one. And by this method it would follow that the one existent is not one, but rather infinite existents. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs refuting the claim that multiplicity or individuation requires a distinct meaning added to the essence. The author begins by quoting the opposing view: 'Multiplicity does not occur except due to a meaning that necessitates distinction.' He rejects this statement and then provides three arguments.

First proof: If individuation were an additional attribute, then each individuation would itself need another individuation, leading to an infinite regress. The opponent might try to avoid this by suggesting that the quiddity of individuation and the quiddity of the thing, when combined, mutually necessitate each other's individuation. The author counters that both quiddities are universal, and conjoining universals still yields a universal, not a particular. Thus the argument fails.

Second proof: The specific association of an individuation with its particular subject presupposes a distinction between that individuation and others. If this distinction were itself caused by yet another attribute, a circular loop would result, which is impossible.

Third proof: If individuation were added, the thing would become two existents: the essence and its individuation. Then each of those would need further individuation, leading to an infinite multiplication. Thus a single existent would become an infinite number, which is absurd.

Key Terms

التعدد
التسلسل
الماهية الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that adding an attribute of individuation solves distinction without regress because two universals can mutually individuate each other.
Correction: The author shows that two universals cannot produce a particular; they remain universal, so the regress problem persists.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that treating individuation as an added attribute inevitably leads to an infinite regress, and the opponent's attempt to avoid it by mutual composition fails because universals cannot produce a particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

According to this lesson, each added attribute would itself need another to be distinguished, leading to an endless chase. Where in your life are you caught in this infinite regress, and will you stop today to find your identity in something that does not need further attributes to be real?
?

But this lesson shows that universals remain universals; they never touch your true particularity. Are you building your identity on such universal categories? What would it take to seek the unchanging essence that needs no addition?

Second Proof: Distinction Leads to Circularity
Second proof: circularity from attributing distinction to added attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribution of a specific individuation to a specific subject requires a preexisting distinction, leading to circularity if that distinction is attributed to another added attribute.

Core Idea
Distinction between individuations cannot be caused by added attributes without circularity.

The second proof addresses the pairing of a particular individuation with its subject. The author argues that for individuation A to be assigned to thing A rather than thing B, there must already be a distinction between individuation A and individuation B. If that distinction were caused by further added attributes, we would have circularity, because the distinction would depend on the very thing it is meant to explain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English for the second proof. Notice the conditional structure: 'specification... is conditioned by...'
2
Understand the logical structure
The proof states that the allocation of this individuation to this thing requires a prior distinction between this individuation and that. That distinction cannot be explained by further attributes without circularity.
3
Identify the circularity
If the distinction (imtiyāz) between individuations is caused by additional attributes (such as 'this-ness' in this subject), then those attributes themselves would need to be distinguished, leading to a circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : « إنه لا يحصل التعدد إلا لأجل معنى يوجب الإمتياز » . قلنا : هذا ممنوع . ويدل عليه وجوه :

الأول : إنه لو كان التعين أمراً زائداً على الذات والتعينات متساوية في ماهية أنها تعين ، وجب أن يكون تعين كل تعين زائداً عليه . ويلزم التسلسل . ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : ماهية السواد، وماهية التعين ، إذا انضمت كل واحدة منهما إلى الأخرى ، فإن كل واحدة منهما تقتضي تعين الأخرى ؟ لأنـا نقول : ماهية السواد ماهية كلية ، وماهية التعين أيضاً ماهية كلية ، والكل إذا تقيد بالكلي يبقى كلياً ، ولا يصير شخصياً . فثبت : أن القدر الذي ذكرتموه لا يوجب التعين .

الثاني : إن اختصاص هذا التعين بهذا المتعين دون ذاك ، واختصاص ذلك التعين بذلك المتعين دون هذا : مشروط بامتياز هذا التعين عن ذلك التعين . فلو كان ذلك الامتياز معللاً بحصول هذه الصفة في هذا ، وحصول تلك الصفة في ذلك ، لزم الدور ، وهو محال .

الثالث : لو كان تعين هذا المتعين زائداً عليه ، لكان هذا المتعين موجودين، لا موجوداً واحداً . ثم يكون لكل واحد منهما تعين آخر ، فتصير أربعة لا واحداً . وبهذا الطريق يلزم أن يكون الموجود الواحد غير واحد ، بل موجودات غير متناهية . وهو محال .
His statement: 'Multiplicity does not occur except due to a meaning that necessitates distinction.' We said: This is rejected. And evidence for that are several aspects:

First: If individuation were an additional thing over the essence, and individuations are equal in their quiddity of being individuation, then the individuation of every individuation would have to be additional to it, leading to infinite regress. And it cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say: the quiddity of blackness and the quiddity of individuation, when each of them is conjoined to the other, then each of them necessitates the individuation of the other? Because we say: The quiddity of blackness is a universal quiddity, and the quiddity of individuation is also a universal quiddity, and each when restricted by the universal remains universal and does not become particular. So it is established that the extent you mentioned does not necessitate individuation.

Second: The specification of this individuation to this individuated thing rather than that, and the specification of that individuation to that individuated thing rather than this, is conditioned by the distinction of this individuation from that individuation. If that distinction were caused by the occurrence of this attribute in this and the occurrence of that attribute in that, then circularity would be necessary, which is impossible.

Third: If the individuation of this individuated thing were additional to it, then this individuated thing would be two existents, not one existent. Then each of them would have another individuation, so they would become four, not one. And by this method it would follow that the one existent is not one, but rather infinite existents. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs refuting the claim that multiplicity or individuation requires a distinct meaning added to the essence. The author begins by quoting the opposing view: 'Multiplicity does not occur except due to a meaning that necessitates distinction.' He rejects this statement and then provides three arguments.

First proof: If individuation were an additional attribute, then each individuation would itself need another individuation, leading to an infinite regress. The opponent might try to avoid this by suggesting that the quiddity of individuation and the quiddity of the thing, when combined, mutually necessitate each other's individuation. The author counters that both quiddities are universal, and conjoining universals still yields a universal, not a particular. Thus the argument fails.

Third proof: If individuation were added, the thing would become two existents: the essence and its individuation. Then each of those would need further individuation, leading to an infinite multiplication. Thus a single existent would become an infinite number, which is absurd.

Third Proof: Multiplication to Infinity
Third proof: infinite multiplication from added individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how treating individuation as an added attribute leads to the multiplication of a single existent into an infinite number of existents, which is absurd.

Core Idea
If individuation is added, a single thing becomes two, then four, then infinite.

The third proof takes a different logical angle: if individuation is an additional attribute, then the thing and its individuation are two distinct existents. Each of those would then need its own individuation, leading to four, then eight, and so on ad infinitum. Thus, a single existent would become an infinite number of existents—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English for the third proof. Notice the multiplication pattern: 1 becomes 2, then 4, then infinite.
2
Follow the chain of reasoning
The argument starts: if individuation is added, the thing and its individuation are two. Then each of these requires an individuation, so four. Continue ad infinitum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the one existent would not be one but an infinite number, which is impossible. This refutes the original claim.

Source Text

The Second Argument Against Metempsychosis: The Impossibility of Idle Souls
A dilemma argument against metempsychosis based on idleness and numerical mismatch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second argument against metempsychosis uses a logical dilemma to show that the number of souls dying must equal the number of souls being born, and how this is contradicted by observed events like floods.

Core Idea
Souls cannot be idle; if they must immediately reincarnate, numbers must match—but floods disprove it.

The second argument against metempsychosis presents a devastating dilemma. Either the soul remains idle between bodies (which cannot happen in nature) or it must immediately attach to a new body, forcing the number of dying souls to equal the number of newborn souls. Historical events like great floods prove this equality false. This lesson walks through this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The argument presents two exclusive possibilities for what happens to a soul after death. Read the Arabic passage and identify both options.
The words 'إما أن' signal the two alternatives.
2
Evaluate the first option
The first option—souls remain bare and idle—is declared false because nature has no idle things. Ask yourself why idleness is impossible in the natural order.
This relies on the principle that nature does nothing in vain.
3
Examine the second option
The second option requires immediate attachment to a new body. This logically forces the number of dying souls to equal the number of newborn souls. Consider why that equality is necessary.
4
Apply the empirical counterexample
The argument points to general floods where many more die than are born. This contradicts the required equality. See how this disproves metempsychosis.
The example shows a real-world observation that refutes the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لمنكري التناسخ : قالوا : النفوس المفارقة لأبدانها . إما أن عليها : أن تبقى مجردة مدة ، من غير أن تكن متعلقة بأبدان أخرى ، وإما أن لا يصح ذلك . والأول باطل . لأنها تكون فيما بين الوقتين : معطلة ولا معطل في الطبيعة . والثاني يوجب أن يكون عدد الهالكين ، مساوياً لعدد الكائنين ، حتى يقال : إنه عندما فسد بدن ، وفارقته نفسه. ففي تلك الحالة يكون قد استعد بدن آخر لتتعلق به تلك النفس . ومعلوم أنه ليس الأمر كذلك ، فإن في الطوفانات العامة التي عندها ينقطع النسل ، نعلم أن عدد الهالكين أكثر من عدد الكائنين .
The second argument of the deniers of metempsychosis: They said: The souls that separate from their bodies — either it is necessary that they remain bare for a period, without being attached to other bodies, or that is not valid. The first is false because they would be idle in between the two times, and there is no idle thing in nature. The second necessitates that the number of the dying equals the number of the being born, such that it is said: when a body decays and its soul separates from it, in that state another body has become prepared for that soul to be attached to it. And it is known that this is not the case, for in the general floods by which procreation is cut off, we know that the number of the dying is greater than the number of the being born.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the second argument of those who deny metempsychosis. The author presents a dilemma. The opponent must choose between two options: either the soul remains without a body for a period, or it cannot remain without a body. The first option is false because it would mean the soul is idle, and there is no idle thing in nature according to natural principles. The second option forces the conclusion that the number of souls that die must equal the number of souls that are born, because each soul immediately attaches to a new body. But this is contradicted by observed events like great floods, where many more die than are born. Thus the argument concludes that metempsychosis cannot hold.

Key Terms

معطلة
طبيعة
هالكين
كائنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument is merely an empirical observation about numbers.
Correction: It is actually a logical dilemma based on the principle that nature does nothing in vain, with an empirical counterexample used to refute a logical consequence.

Checkpoint

D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
The second argument refutes metempsychosis by showing its logical consequence of equal dying and birth numbers is contradicted by natural disasters where deaths exceed births.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting the Equal Numbers Objection
The author presents the second objection about equal numbers and dismisses it as unworthy of Avicenna.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the second objection to the second argument by appealing to our ignorance of hidden creatures, and why he considers the objection unworthy of Avicenna.

Core Idea
The objection assumes we can count all dying and living creatures, but we cannot.

In this passage, the author addresses a second objection to his previous argument. The objection suggests that the number of dying creatures always equals the number being born, even during floods. The author's refutation is simple but profound: we cannot possibly know the numbers of creatures hidden in the depths of seas and rocks, and therefore the objection is unworthy of a great philosopher like Avicenna.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Carefully read the objection: it claims that the number of dying equals the number being born, even during global floods.
Notice the wording: 'لم لا يجوز أن يقال' – it's a rhetorical challenge.
2
Identify the refutation
The author responds by pointing out that we cannot count animals in the depths of seas, cracks of rocks, bugs, and mosquitoes. Their numbers are unknown.
The animals mentioned are examples of hidden creatures.
3
Consider the dismissal
The author concludes that such an argument is not befitting Avicenna's virtue. This shows how weak the objection is in his view.
The dismissal indicates that the objection is unworthy of serious philosophical discourse.
4
Reflect on the weakness
Think about why the objection fails: it relies on an assumption that cannot be proven – that we know the total numbers of all creatures.
This lesson highlights the importance of not assuming what cannot be known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض الثاني : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : عدد الهالكين يكون على قدر عدد الكائنين ؟ وأما الطوفانات العامة . فالجواب عنها : إن عدد الحيوانات المتولدة في قعور البحور ، وشقوق الصخور، وأعداد البق والبعوض غير معلومة . ومن الذي يمكنه إحصاء هذا العدد ؟ وبالجملة : فذكر مثل هذا الدليل غير لائق بفضل الشيخ الرئيس البتة .
And the second objection: Why may it not be said that the number of the dying is equal to the number of the being born? As for the general floods, the answer to them: Indeed the number of animals generated in the depths of the seas and the crevices of rocks, and the numbers of bugs and mosquitoes, are unknown. And who can count this number? In summary, the mention of such an argument is not at all befitting the virtue of the chief [Avicenna].
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is the second objection and its answer. The objection asks why can't we assume the number of dying equals the number being born, even during floods? The answer is that we cannot count all animals hidden in seas and rocks, so the apparent discrepancy during floods is not proof of inequality. The author then dismisses the objection as unworthy of the chief philosopher (Avicenna), implying it is a weak argument.

The Third Argument: Memory as Proof Against Metempsychosis
The author presents the third argument from lack of memory, relied upon by most mutakallimūn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against metempsychosis based on the absence of past-life memory and how it relies on the observable principle that repeated actions produce firm dispositions.

Core Idea
If souls had past lives, we would remember them — since we don't, they never existed before.

This passage presents the third argument used by most mutakallimūn to deny metempsychosis (transmigration of souls). The argument is a conditional syllogism: if our souls had managed other bodies before, we would have memory of that state. Since we have no such memory, the souls did not exist prior to this body. The author then provides the key evidence for this conditional: repeated actions cause firm dispositions, which is observable.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument structure
Recognize that the argument is a conditional syllogism: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'souls managed other bodies before', Q = 'we would know that state now'.
2
Identify the key premise
The author states that the evidence for the conditional is that 'repetition of actions is a cause for the occurrence of firm dispositions.' Consider how this premise supports the conditional.
Firm dispositions (malakāt rāsikhah) include memory.
3
Reflect on the obviousness of the consequent denial
The author notes that the negation of the consequent (i.e., we do not remember past lives) is obvious. Confirm that this is universally accepted as a fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة لمنكري التناسخ . وعليه تعويل الجمهور من المتكلمين : قالوا : لو كانت نفوسنا مديرة لأبدان أخرى قبل هذا البدن ، لعرفنا الآن تلك الحالة ، وحيث لم نعرفها . وجب القطع بأنها ما كانت موجودة . أما نقيض التالي : فظاهر. وإنما الشأن في إثبات الشرطية . فنقول : الدليل عليه هو أن تكرر الأفعال سبب لحدوث الملكات الراسخة ، وهذا مشاهد
The third argument of the deniers of metempsychosis, and upon it is the reliance of the majority of the mutakallimūn: They said: If our souls had been managers of other bodies before this body, we would now know that state, and since we do not know it, it is necessary to judge that they did not exist. As for the negation of the consequent, it is obvious. The only concern is in establishing the conditional. So we say: The evidence for it is that the repetition of actions is a cause for the occurrence of firm dispositions, and this is observed.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the third argument against metempsychosis, favored by most mutakallimūn. The argument is a conditional syllogism: If our souls had managed other bodies before, we would have memory of that state (because repeated actions cause firm dispositions, which produce memory). Since we have no memory, the antecedent is false — our souls did not exist prior to this body. The author acknowledges that the negation of the consequent (no memory) is obvious, so the task is to prove the conditional (that previous existence would produce memory). The evidence is the observable principle that repetition of actions causes firm dispositions (malakāt rāsikhah), which implies memory.

Key Terms
مديرة
حالة
تكرر الأفعال
ملكات راسخة
شرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument is circular because it assumes without proof that memory would persist.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it relies on the independent empirical observation that repetition of actions causes firm dispositions. This principle is observable in everyday life (e.g., practice makes perfect) and is used as evidence for the conditional.

C) All souls are equal.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against metempsychosis relies on the observable fact that repeated actions produce firm dispositions, which would generate memory; since we have no memory of past lives, our souls did not exist before.

The Argument from Circularity for the Soul's Immortality
The author presents the second proof for the soul's immortality based on impossibility of circular dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the soul's immortality, which argues that circular dependence (dawr) between the soul and body is impossible, proving the soul's independence.

Core Idea
If the soul depended on the body, a logical circle would result — and circles are impossible.

This lesson presents the second proof for the immortality of the soul from the classical text Al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah. The author uses a logical argument based on the impossibility of circular dependence (dawr). By showing that the soul's attributes cannot be dependent on the body's welfare without creating a vicious circle, he establishes the soul's independence. The passage also draws an analogy with God's relationship to the world to clarify the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice how the argument is structured in three parts: premise, conditional leading to circle, and analogy.
Pay attention to the conditional 'if...then' structure.
2
Identify the key premise
The proof begins by stating that the soul manages the body because it is living, knowing, and powerful. This premise establishes that the body's welfare depends on the soul's attributes.
3
Understand the circularity
The argument then considers what would happen if the soul's attributes themselves depended on the body. That would create a circle: A depends on B and B depends on A, which is logically invalid.
Circularity (dawr) is a well-known logical fallacy in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the analogy and conclusion
The author compares the soul-body relation to the God-world relation. Just as God's attributes cannot depend on the world, so the soul's attributes cannot depend on the body. Therefore, the soul does not die with the body.
This analogy reinforces the proof and makes it intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لما كان المدبر لهذا الجسد ، والمتولي لإصلاح حاله هو النفس ، ومن المعلوم أن النفس إنما تتولى ذلك بسبب كونها حية عالمة قادرة ، فحينئذ يتوقف صلاح هذا الجسد على كون النفس موصوفة [ بالحياة والعلم والقدرة . فلو كانت النفس موصوفة ] بهذه الصفات [ وهذه الصفات ] موقوفة على صلاح حال البدن ، لزم توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر ) وذلك دور . والدور باطل . ومثاله : إنه لما كان حدوث العالم ، وصلاح حاله موقوفاً على كون الإله حياً عالماً قادراً ، امتنع أن يكون حصول هذه الصفات للإله ، موقوفاً على صلاح هذا العالم ، وإلا لزم الدور. فكذا ههنا . فإن النفس لا تعلق لها بالبدن ، إلا تعلقاً شبيهاً بتعلق الإله بالعالم . وذلك من أظهر الدلائل على أن النفس لا تموت [ بموت البدن ، ولا يفسد شيء من صفاتها بفساد البدن ] .
The second proof: Since the manager of this body and the one in charge of reforming its state is the soul, and it is known that the soul undertakes that only because it is living, knowing, and powerful, then the welfare of this body depends on the soul being described [with life, knowledge, and power. So if the soul were described] with these attributes [and these attributes] dependent on the welfare of the body's state, then the dependence of each one of them on the other would be necessary, and that is a circle (dawr). And the circle is invalid. And its example: Since the coming into existence of the world and the welfare of its state depend on the deity being living, knowing, and powerful, it is impossible that the acquisition of these attributes for the deity should depend on the welfare of this world; otherwise circularity would follow. So likewise here. For the soul has no connection to the body except a connection similar to the connection of the deity to the world. And that is among the clearest evidences that the soul does not die [with the death of the body, nor does anything of its attributes become corrupted with the corruption of the body].
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof (al-ḥujja al-thāniya) for the immortality of the soul. It is an argument from the impossibility of circular dependence (dawr). The author first states a premise: the soul is the director and manager of the body; it does so because it is living, knowing, and powerful. Therefore, the body's well-being depends on the soul possessing these attributes. If, however, the soul's attributes themselves depended on the body's well-being, then each would depend on the other—a vicious circle. Circularity is logically invalid (bāṭil). To illustrate, the author draws an analogy with God and the world: the world's existence depends on God being living, knowing, and powerful; it is impossible that God's attributes depend on the world, otherwise there would be circularity. Similarly, the soul's relation to the body is like God's relation to the world—the soul governs but is not dependent. Therefore, the soul does not die when the body dies, nor do its attributes perish with the body's corruption. The argument shows that the soul is an independent substance whose existence and attributes are not contingent on the body.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
دور (circularity)
بدن (body)
صفات (attributes)
إله (deity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the soul's attributes (life, knowledge, power) are dependent on the body for their existence or perfection.
Correction: The proof shows that if that were true, it would create an impossible circular dependence. Therefore, the soul's attributes must be independent of the body.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the second proof identify if the soul's attributes depended on the body's welfare?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circularity (dawr)
C) Contradiction between knowledge and power
D) Dependence of the body on the soul

Key Takeaway
The soul is an independent substance; it does not die with the body, nor do its attributes perish with the body's corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that your soul is independent and immortal. But do you actually live as though your physical desires are all that matter? If the soul's attributes (life, knowledge, power) are not contingent on the body, then why do you fear losing your physical health so much that you neglect your soul's well-being? Examine your daily habits: do they reflect a soul that will outlast the body, or a body that has enslaved the soul? What one change can you make today to honor the soul's independence?
?
The proof uses an analogy with God and the world: just as God's attributes are not dependent on creation, your soul's attributes are not dependent on your body. Do you truly live with the confidence that your core identity is not your physical existence? When you face the prospect of aging, illness, or death, do you cling to the body or trust in the enduring soul? What would it mean for you to start preparing for the soul's journey beyond the body right now?
?
You have just learned that a logical circle would result if the soul depended on the body. But in your life, do you find yourself stuck in a circle—where your spiritual state depends on your physical circumstances, and your physical circumstances depend on your spiritual state? Break that circle: identify one area where you are waiting for external change before improving your soul. Commit to acting today as if the soul leads, not the body.

The Conditional Argument for the Soul's Independence
The author presents the conditional argument and its logical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third proof for the soul's immortality, which argues that the soul does not die with the body because it does not weaken when the body weakens.

Core Idea
If the soul depended on the body, it would weaken with it; since it doesn't, it does not die.

The third proof presents a conditional argument: if the soul were subject to death like the body, it would show weakness when the body weakens. But observation shows the opposite—the soul remains strong even as the body declines. This lesson unpacks the logical reasoning behind this conditional and the principle of dependency that underlies it.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional statement
Recognize the premise: if the soul died with the body, it would weaken with it. Note the logical structure of the conditional.
2
Understand the demonstration of the conditional
The author explains that a defect in the root causes a defect in the branch—this is the causal link that supports the conditional.
3
Analyze the dependency proof
The passage argues that if X is corrupted by Y, then X is dependent on Y; otherwise X's perfection would imply independence. Follow the logic carefully.
4
Conclude the conditional's implication
Since the soul does not weaken when the body weakens, it is not dependent, and therefore does not die with the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنه لو ماتت النفس بموت البدن ، لضعفت بضعف البدن ، وهي [ لما كانت ] لا تضعف بضعفه ، وجب أن لا تموت بموت البدن . أما بيان الشرطية : فبالاستقرار الظاهر. فإنه متى وقع الخلل في الأصل ، لزم منه وقوع الخلل في الفرع . ثم نقول : إن الدليل عليه : إن كل شيء يفسد بفساد غيره ، فإنه يكون محتاجاً في ذاته إلى ذلك الغير إذ كل محتاج إلى شيء آخر ، يضعف حال الاحتياج إليه [ فضعف المحتاج إليه ] يوجب ضعف المحتاج ، إذ لو بقي كمال المحتاج ، حال نقصان المحتاج إليه ، لكانت صفة المحتاج غنية عن المحتاج إليه . والصفة أضعف من الموصوف ، وإذا حصل الاستغناء في الصفة مع ضعفها ، فبأن يحصل الاستغناء في الذات مع قوتها كان أولى . فثبت : أن كل شيء يفسد بفساد غيره ، وجب أن يظهر فيه النقصان عند نقصان غيره . فلو كانت تفسد بفساد البدن ، لوجب أن يظهر [ النقصان في النفس عند ظهور النقصان في ] البدن . وإنما قلنا : إنه ليس الأمر كذلك ، لوجوه : الأول : إن المواظبة على الأفكار الغامضة، والاستغراق فيها يوجب نقصاناً شديداً في البدن ، ولم يلزم منه حصول النقصان في النفس البتة ، بل يوجب الكمال العظيم في النفس . الثاني : إن المواظبة على الرياضة، وتجويع النفس والانقطاع عن المحسوسات والجسمانيات ، يوجب النقصان الشديد في البدن ، وذلك لا يوجب نقصان النفس ، بل يوجب حصول الكمال الشديد للنفس . الثالث : إن النوم يوجب نقصان حال البدن ، فإنه يزول عنه حسر السمع والبصر ، والقدرة على المشي والأخذ ، ولا يوجب نقصان حال النفس ، بل يوجب كمال حال النفس . فإنها في هذا الوقت قدرت على الاتصال بعالم الغيب . الرابع : إن جسد الإنسان من أول الكهولة يأخذ في الانتقاص والذبول ، وعقله [ من هذا الوقت ] لا يأخذ في الانتقاص والذبول ، بل كمال عقله ، يبتدىء في التزايد من هذا الوقت. ولهذا السبب قيل : إن بعثة أكثر الأنبياء
The third proof: If the soul died with the death of the body, it would weaken with the weakening of the body. But since it does not weaken with its weakening, it must not die with the death of the body. As for the demonstration of the conditional: it is by apparent observation. For whenever a defect occurs in the root, a defect necessarily occurs in the branch. Then we say: the evidence for it is that everything that becomes corrupted by the corruption of another is necessarily in its essence needful of that other. For everything needful of something else weakens in the state of need of it; so the weakness of the needed causes weakness in the needer. For if the perfection of the needer remained while the needed is deficient, then the attribute of the needer would be independent of the needed. And the attribute is weaker than the one described; if independence occurs in the attribute despite its weakness, then it is even more fitting that independence occurs in the essence despite its strength. So it is established: everything that becomes corrupted by the corruption of another must show deficiency when that other is deficient. So if it (the soul) were corrupted by the corruption of the body, then deficiency must appear [in the soul when deficiency appears in] the body. And we say that it is not so, for reasons: First: Persistence in profound thoughts and absorption in them causes severe deficiency in the body, but it does not cause any deficiency in the soul; rather, it causes great perfection in the soul. Second: Persistence in spiritual exercise, starving the soul, and cutting off from sensory and physical things causes severe deficiency in the body, but that does not cause deficiency of the soul; rather, it causes intense perfection of the soul. Third: Sleep causes deficiency in the state of the body, for it removes the senses of hearing and sight and the ability to walk and grasp, but does not cause deficiency in the state of the soul; rather, it causes perfection of the state of the soul. For at this time it becomes able to connect with the unseen world. Fourth: The human body from the beginning of middle age begins to diminish and wither, but his intellect [from this time] does not begin to diminish and wither; rather, the perfection of his intellect begins to increase from this time. For this reason it is said that the sending of most prophets...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) for the immortality of the soul, structured as a conditional argument followed by four empirical observations. The core reasoning: If the soul died with the body, it would weaken when the body weakens. But it does not weaken when the body weakens, so it does not die with the body. The author first demonstrates the conditional: a defect in the cause (root) necessarily causes a defect in the effect (branch). He then proves that if X is corrupted by Y's corruption, X is inherently dependent on Y. If X were independent, its perfection would remain despite Y's deficiency—but that would mean X's attribute is independent of Y, which is even more plausible for the essence. Therefore, if the soul were corrupted by the body, deficiency would appear in it when the body is deficient. But the soul does not show such deficiency; hence it is not dependent and does not die with the body. Four reasons (wujūh) support that the soul does not weaken with the body: (1) Deep thinking weakens the body but perfects the soul. (2) Ascetic practices weaken the body but perfect the soul. (3) Sleep weakens the body's senses but allows the soul to connect to the unseen. (4) Old age weakens the body but increases intellectual perfection, as seen in prophets' missions. Each reason demonstrates an inverse relationship: bodily weakness coincides with soul strength, proving the soul's independence.

Key Terms
نفس (soul)
بدن (body)
موت (death)
ضعف (weakness)
محتاج (needy/dependent)
صفة (attribute)
ذات (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the soul is immortal by nature
B) Because the soul does not weaken when the body weakens
C) Because the body is stronger than the soul
D) Because the soul is a separate substance

Key Takeaway
The soul's independence from bodily weakness proves its immortality — if it were dependent, it would weaken with the body, but it does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your body is weak or sick, do you ever feel that your soul—your core self—is also diminished? How might this argument change how you view your own identity when you are ill? What can you do today to remind yourself that your worth is not tied to your physical condition?
?
If this argument is true, then death of the body does not end you. Does your daily behavior reflect that you truly believe your soul is independent and immortal? What would change if you fully lived that conviction starting now?

Four Empirical Reasons the Soul Thrives When the Body Weakens
The author presents four empirical proofs that the soul does not weaken with the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the four empirical observations that demonstrate the soul's strength when the body weakens.

Core Idea
Deep thinking, asceticism, sleep, and old age all show the soul thriving as the body declines.

After establishing the logical argument, the author provides four concrete examples from daily life and religious experience that confirm the soul does not weaken when the body weakens. These examples illustrate an inverse relationship: bodily deficiency coincides with spiritual or intellectual perfection.

Guided Path
1
First reason: Deep thinking
Recognize that intense mental activity exhausts the body but perfects the soul. This shows bodily weakness does not affect the soul.
2
Second reason: Ascetic practices
Note that spiritual exercises (riyada) and self-denial weaken the body but strengthen the soul, indicating independence.
3
Third reason: Sleep
Observe that sleep removes bodily senses (hearing, sight, movement) but perfects the soul by connecting it to the unseen world.
4
Fourth reason: Old age
Understand that the body deteriorates in middle age while the intellect increases, as seen with prophets who were sent in later life.

Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) for the immortality of the soul, structured as a conditional argument followed by four empirical observations. The core reasoning: If the soul died with the body, it would weaken when the body weakens. But it does not weaken when the body weakens, so it does not die with the body. The author first demonstrates the conditional: a defect in the cause (root) necessarily causes a defect in the effect (branch). He then proves that if X is corrupted by Y's corruption, X is inherently dependent on Y. If X were independent, its perfection would remain despite Y's deficiency—but that would mean X's attribute is independent of Y, which is even more plausible for the essence. Therefore, if the soul were corrupted by the body, deficiency would appear in it when the body is deficient. But the soul does not show such deficiency; hence it is not dependent and does not die with the body. Four reasons (wujūh) support that the soul does not weaken with the body: (1) Deep thinking weakens the body but perfects the soul. (2) Ascetic practices weaken the body but perfect the soul. (3) Sleep weakens the body's senses but allows the soul to connect to the unseen. (4) Old age weakens the body but increases intellectual perfection, as seen in prophets' missions. Each reason demonstrates an inverse relationship: bodily weakness coincides with soul strength, proving the soul's independence.

Key Terms
أفكار (thoughts)
رياضة (spiritual exercise)
نوم (sleep)
كهولة (middle age)
غيب (unseen)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that when the body weakens, the soul must also weaken.
Correction: The four examples show the opposite: bodily weakness corresponds with soul perfection, proving the soul's independence.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth reason, what happens to the intellect in old age?
A) It diminishes with the body
B) It remains constant
C) It increases
D) It disappears

Key Takeaway
Four observable phenomena — deep thinking, asceticism, sleep, and old age — confirm the soul's independence from the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has physical exhaustion (like from deep study or fasting) accompanied a surprising mental clarity or spiritual elevation? How can you intentionally seek that state despite bodily discomfort?
?
Do you treat sleep as a mere biological necessity, or as a chance for your soul to communicate with the unseen? What would change tonight if you prepared your sleep as a spiritual encounter?
?
As you age, do you fear losing mental sharpness? The fourth reason promises intellectual growth even as the body fades. What specific step can you take this week to cultivate your intellect, trusting that it will not decline?

Proof from Strength and Weakness that the Soul Does Not Die with the Body
The author proves the soul's immortality by the contradiction of strength and weakness in body and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the soul is distinct from the body and does not die with it, based on the contradiction of strength and weakness.

Core Idea
The soul's resistance to illness proves its independence from the body.

This passage presents a rational argument for the soul's immortality based on the observation that a sick person can survive without food for many days. The author uses the apparent contradiction between the body's weakness and the strength of the resistive force to demonstrate that the soul is not identical to the body and does not perish when the body dies. This proof is foundational for understanding the soul's nature and its relation to the physical body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
The author uses a step-by-step argument, so pay attention to each step.
2
Identify the key contradiction
Find the observation about the sick person and the contradiction that arises if the resistive nature is the body. This is the core of the proof.
The contradiction is: one thing cannot be both extremely weak and extremely strong.
3
Follow the reasoning to the soul's independence
See how the author eliminates the possibility that the soul is weak, leading to the conclusion that the soul is strong and independent of the body.
The argument relies on the principle that the overcome cannot resist the overcomer.
4
Understand the final conclusion
The author concludes that since the soul is independent of the body in its essence and power, the body's death does not necessitate the soul's death.
This establishes the soul's immortality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاغتذاء ، وقد يبقى المريض منقطعاً عن الاغتذاء أياماً كثيرة ، وهو سبب لحصول الضعف . فثبت : أن البدن في غاية الضعف ، والطبيعة في غاية القوة على الدفع ، فلو كانت هذه الطبيعة هي البدن ، أو جزء من أجزاء البدن ، لزم كون الشيء الواحد موصوفاً بغاية الضعف ، وبغاية القوة . وهو محال . فثبت : أن الشيء الذي يقاوم العلة هو النفس . فنقول : النفس في تلك الحالة ، إما أن تكون مريضة ضعيفة وإما أن تكون خالية من المرض والضعف . والأول باطل . لأن النفس في هذه الحالة ، لو كانت مريضة ضعيفة ، لما قدرت على مقاومة العلة الغالبة ، والمادة القاهرة المبطلة . لأن المغلوب لا يمكنه مقاومة الغالب والمقهور لا يمكنه مقاومة القاهر فثبت بهذا البيان الظاهر : أن النفس في تلك الحالة قوية قاهرة ، سليمة عن الضعف والمرض - وظاهر أن البدن في غاية الضعف والمرض وذلك يدل على أن النفس لا تمرض بمرض البدن ولا تضعف بضعفه البتة . وإذا ثبت هذا ، وجب أن لا تموت بموته . وذلك لأن النفس لما لم تمرض بمرض البدن ، ولم تضعف بضعفه ، ظهر أن النفس غنية في ذاتها وفي كمال قدرتها وقوتها عن هذا البدن . فإذا مات البدن ، فقد مات شيء كانت النفس غنية في ذاتها وفي قدرتها وفي علمها عنه . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون موت البدن ، موجباً لموت النفس . وهذا كلام قوي مناسب . وهو بعينه يدل على أن النفس غير البدن ، وغير جميع أجزائه وأبعاضه .
Nourishment, and the sick person may remain cut off from nourishment for many days, and it is a cause of weakness. So it is established that the body is in extreme weakness, and nature is in extreme strength in repelling. So if this nature were the body, or a part of the body, then it would lead to one thing being described with extreme weakness and extreme strength, which is impossible. So it is established that the thing that resists the illness is the soul (al-nafs). We say: the soul in that state is either sick and weak or free from illness and weakness. The first is false, because if the soul were sick and weak in this state, it would not be able to resist the overpowering illness and the conquering, destructive matter (al-maddah al-qahirah al-mubtilah). For the overcome cannot resist the overcomer, and the conquered cannot resist the conqueror. So it is established by this clear demonstration that the soul in that state is strong, conquering, free from weakness and illness. And it is apparent that the body is in extreme weakness and illness, and that indicates that the soul does not become ill with the body's illness nor does it become weak with its weakness at all. And when this is established, it must be that it does not die with its death. That is because the soul, since it does not become ill with the body's illness nor become weak with its weakness, it appears that the soul is independent (ghaniyyah) in its essence and in the perfection of its power and strength from this body. So when the body dies, something has died which the soul was independent from in its essence, its power, and its knowledge. And when the matter is thus, it is impossible that the death of the body necessitates the death of the soul. And this is strong, appropriate speech. And it itself indicates that the soul is other than the body and other than all its parts and limbs.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (hujjah) that the soul (al-nafs) does not die with the death of the body. The argument begins with an observed fact: a patient can go without food for many days, which causes weakness in the body. From this, the author establishes two things: first, the body reaches extreme weakness; second, the natural resistive force (al-tabi'ah) is in extreme strength in repelling illness. If this resistive force were identical to the body or a part of it, then one single thing would be described by both extreme weakness and extreme strength, which is a contradiction and thus impossible. Therefore, the resistive force must be something other than the body, namely the soul. The author then considers the state of the soul: either it is also weak and sick, or it is free from illness and weakness. The first option is false because if the soul were weak, it could not resist the overpowering illness and the conquering matter. Since the overcome cannot resist the overcomer, the soul cannot be weak. Hence, the soul must be strong, conquering, and free from weakness and illness. This leads to the conclusion that the soul does not become ill when the body is ill, nor does it weaken when the body weakens. Consequently, it does not die when the body dies, because the soul is independent (ghaniyyah) in its essence, power, and knowledge from the body. The body's death only removes something the soul was already independent of, so the soul's existence continues. The author closes by affirming that this argument is strong and appropriate, and it directly shows that the soul is distinct from the body and all its parts.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs)
البدن (al-badan)
المادة القاهرة (al-maddah al-qahirah)
العلة الغالبة (al-illah al-ghalibah)
الطبيعة (al-tabi'ah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul becomes weak or ill when the body is ill.
Correction: The argument shows the soul remains strong and unaffected by the body's illness, demonstrating its independence.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does the author use to prove the soul is not the body?
A) The body is strong and the soul is weak.
B) The body is weak and the resistive force is strong; they cannot be the same thing.
C) The soul and body both become ill simultaneously.
D) Nature is weaker than the body.

Key Takeaway
The soul is independent of the body in essence and power, so the body's death does not cause the soul's death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is independent and does not die, why do you spend so much energy feeding your body and so little feeding your soul? What one change can you make today to nourish your soul instead?
?
Do you truly live as if death is not the end, or do your fears and daily choices reveal that you act as if this world is all there is? What would you do differently if you were absolutely certain your soul will survive your body?
?
When your body is weak, sick, or in pain, do you find your faith wavering? How can this proof that the soul remains strong despite bodily weakness become a shield for your heart during trials? Write down one concrete way you will remind yourself of this truth when you next face illness.

The Soul's Independence Ensures Its Survival
A short argument for the soul's immortality based on independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from the soul's independence to its survival after bodily death.

Core Idea
Independent existence does not require the existence of what it is independent from.

This lesson presents a concise proof for the soul's immortality based on its independence from the body. The argument uses a simple logical principle: if something is absolutely independent of something else, its corruption does not imply the corruption of the independent thing. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: premise, principle, conclusion.
The Arabic is concise; try to identify each part.
2
Identify the premise
The passage states that the soul is independent in its essence and attributes from the body. What does 'independent' mean here? It means the soul's existence does not depend on the body.
3
Apply the logical principle
Understand the principle: something absolutely independent of another does not necessarily become corrupt when that other thing becomes corrupt. Then apply it to the soul and body.
The conclusion follows necessarily: the soul does not die with the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظاهر : أن النفس غنية في ذاتها وفي صفاتها عن البدن . والغني مطلقاً عن الشيء ، لا يجب فساده عند فساد ذلك الشيء . فوجب الجزم بأن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن ، ولا تضعف بضعفه . وهو المطلوب
It is evident that the soul is independent in its essence and in its attributes from the body. And that which is absolutely independent of a thing does not necessarily become corrupt when that thing becomes corrupt. Therefore, it is necessary to be certain that the soul does not die with the death of the body, nor does it weaken with its weakening. That is the intended conclusion.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents a concise proof for the soul's immortality. It begins by asserting a premise: the soul is independent of the body both in its essence (ذات) and its attributes (صفات). Then it applies a logical principle: if something is completely independent of something else, its corruption does not entail the corruption of the independent thing. Therefore, since the soul is independent of the body, it does not die when the body dies, nor does it weaken when the body weakens. The conclusion ('وهو المطلوب') indicates that this is what was sought to be proven. The argument is straightforward and uses a causal relation between independence and survival.

Key Terms
النفس
البدن
الغني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the soul's independence means it has no connection to the body at all.
Correction: The passage only uses independence to argue against necessary corruption, not to deny interaction. The soul may still interact with the body during life.

Checkpoint

A) The soul's complexity
B) The soul's independence from the body
C) The soul's simplicity
D) The soul's inherent immortality

Key Takeaway
The soul's independence from the body entails that it does not die when the body dies.

The Eighth Proof: If the Soul Did Not Survive, Humans Would Be Inferior to Animals
A reductio ad absurdum proof that the soul must survive, otherwise humans would be the worst of animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a reductio ad absurdum argument demonstrates that the soul must survive after death, based on the absurd consequence that otherwise humans would be the worst of animals.

Core Idea
If the soul perishes at death, then reason becomes a liability, making humans lower than animals – a clear absurdity.

This proof uses a clever reductio: it assumes the opposite of what we want to prove and shows that leads to a conclusion so obviously false that the assumption cannot hold. The argument turns on the value of reason and bodily pleasures. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Get a sense of the overall argument before analyzing it.
Notice the compressed style; the proof is dense but logical.
2
Identify the conditional premise and the absurd conclusion
The proof starts with an 'if-then' statement: if the soul does not survive, then man would be the worst of animals. This is the antecedent and consequent that will be disproven.
The argument will show the consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false.
3
Understand the implication chain
Trace why, if the soul does not survive, happiness is only bodily pleasures, and reason actually diminishes those pleasures. This leads to the absurdity that reason is a defect and the rational person is baser.
This is the heart of the reductio: the assumption leads to a self-defeating conclusion.
4
See how the intuition of human superiority refutes the consequent
The author appeals to بديهة العقل (intuition of reason) which judges that man is like a king over animals. This is a self-evident truth that contradicts the absurd conclusion.
The falseness of the consequent is immediately obvious, so the conditional must be false.
5
Confirm the conclusion
Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (the soul does not survive) is also false. By negation, the soul does survive after death. Review the logical structure.
The proof is valid and sound if one accepts the premises about the purpose of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنا قد ذكرنا في باب إثبات النفس : أنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن لكان أخس الحيوانات هو الإنسان والتالي باطل فالمقدم باطل . بيان الملازمة : إن بتقدير أن لا تكون النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، كان كمال السعادة ونهايتها هو الفوز بهذه اللذات الجسمانية ، والراحات البدنية . وحصول العقل يوجب تنقيص هذه الحالة . وهي تنقيص هذه اللذات ، وتنقيص هذه الراحات والسعادات . وأما عدم العقل فإنه يوجب تكميل هذه الراحات، وقوة هذه السعادات. فإذا كان لا كمال ولا سعادة إلا هذه اللذات البدنية والخيرات الجسمية . وكان العقل سبباً لنقصانها تارة ، ولبطلانها أخرى ، وجب أن يكون العقل أخس الصفات وأدونها ، وأن يكون العاقل أخس ممن ليس بعاقل . فثبت : أنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية ، لكان الإنسان أخس الحيوانات، لكن التالي باطل . فإن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن الإنسان كالملك ، وسائر الحيوانات كالعبيد له . وإن الإنسان العاقل كالملك ، والجهال كالعبيد له . وذلك يدل على أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن
The eighth proof: We have mentioned in the chapter on the affirmation of the soul that if the soul were not to survive after the death of the body, then the worst of animals would be man—and the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. Explanation of the implication: If it is assumed that the soul does not survive after the death of the body, then the perfection and end of happiness is the attainment of these bodily pleasures and physical comforts. The possession of reason necessitates a diminution of this state, and it is a diminution of these pleasures, and a diminution of these comforts and happinesses. As for the lack of reason, it necessitates the completion of these comforts and the strength of these happinesses. Since there is no perfection or happiness except these bodily pleasures and physical goods, and reason is a cause of their deficiency sometimes and of their nullification other times, it would follow that reason is the basest and lowest of attributes, and that the rational person is baser than the one who is not rational. So it is established that if the soul were not to survive, then man would be the worst of animals, but the consequent is false, for the intuition of reason judges that man is like a king and all other animals are like slaves to him, and that the rational man is like a king and the ignorant are like slaves to him. That indicates that the soul survives after the death of the body.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof for the soul's survival, structured as a reductio ad absurdum. It begins by stating the conditional premise: if the soul does not survive after death, then the worst of animals would be man. The argument then shows why this conditional holds: without an afterlife, ultimate happiness would consist solely in bodily pleasures. In that case, reason would actually diminish such pleasures (by promoting restraint), while lack of reason would enhance them. Thus reason would be a defect, and the rational person would be inferior to the irrational. This would make man, as a rational being, the lowest of animals. However, the consequent is false because common reason intuitively holds that man is superior to animals, like a king over slaves. Therefore, the antecedent—that the soul does not survive—must be false. Hence the soul survives after bodily death. The proof relies on the universality of rational intuition (بديهة العقل).

Key Terms
النفس
السعادة
العقل
اللذات الجسمانية
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this proof denigrates bodily pleasures absolutely, implying they are evil.
Correction: The proof only argues hypothetically: if there were no afterlife, then bodily pleasures would be the only goal, making reason a liability. It does not condemn bodily pleasures in themselves.

Checkpoint
What is the main intermediate conclusion that the proof forces?
A) Reason is the best attribute
B) Without the soul's survival, reason would be a defect
C) Bodily pleasures are the ultimate happiness
D) Animals are superior to humans

Key Takeaway
The soul's survival is proven by the absurdity that without it, humans would be the worst of creatures – contradicting our rational intuition of human dignity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believed there is no life after death, would you see reason as a friend or an obstacle to your happiness? Be honest: in your daily choices, do you ever treat reason as an unwelcome restraint on your desires?
?

?
You claim by intuition that humans are superior to animals. But do your actions reflect that dignity? When you sin, you lower yourself below animals who act without reason. Will you today commit to living in a way that honors your rational soul and its eternal destiny?
