The Ninth Proof: Universal Recognition That Reducing Pleasures Leads to Perfection
A proof from universal intuitive judgment about asceticism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a universal intuitive judgment about asceticism demonstrates that the soul must survive after death.

Core Idea
If asceticism is universally respected, then happiness cannot be only in this life.

This proof appeals to a universal human intuition: we instinctively honor those who abstain from worldly pleasures. The author argues that if this judgment is sound, it implies that true happiness must lie beyond physical indulgence—otherwise, asceticism would be foolish. By showing the contradiction, the proof establishes the soul's survival.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise and evidence
Understand the universally accepted judgment that less indulgence means more perfection, and the evidence from how people treat ascetics.
2
Follow the conditional argument
See how the author sets up a conditional: if soul does not survive, then maximizing pleasure would be perfection. Then note that this contradicts the universal premise.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the explanation of happiness
The author clarifies that happiness is pleasure, and if no afterlife, then only worldly pleasure matters.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the universal premise is true, the conditional's antecedent (soul not surviving) must be false.
Thus the soul survives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إن العقول كلها حاكمة بأنه كلما كان الخوض في اللذات البدنية والخيرات الجسمية أقل، كان ذلك الإنسان أكمل . وكلما كان الخوض فيها أكثر ، كان ذلك الإنسان أخس . والدليل عليه : أن الناس إذا اعتقدوا في إنسان أنه معرض عن اللذات الجسمية ، كالأكل والشرب والوقاع ، ومعرض عن طلب الرئاسة في الدنيا والتفوق على الأقران ، فإنه يعظم قدر هذا الإنسان في عقولهم. وترى الطالبين لهذه اللذات يخدمون ذلك الإنسان المعرض عنها ، خدمة العبيد للأرباب ، ويعدون أنفسهم بالنسبة إلى أولئك المعرضين ، كالبهائم بالنسبة إلى الملائكة . حتى إن المزور إذا أراد استباع الخلق ، أظهر من نفسه - على سبيل التزوير - قلة الرغبة في هذه الخيرات البدنية ، والراحات الجسمية ، ويتوصل بها إلى استخدامهم ، والأمر والنهي عليهم . وهذا يدل على أن تقرر في عقول جميع الخلق : أن التقليل من هذه اللذات ، يوجب الكمال ، وأن التكثير منها يوجب النقصان . والمقدمة التي شهدت بصحتها بدائه العقول ، وصرائح الأفكار ، لا بد وأن تكون مقدمة حقة يقينية . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو لم تكن النفس باقية ، لكان كمال السعادة في الاستكثار من هذه اللذات، وكمال الشقاوة في التقليل منها . والتالي كاذب ، فالمقدم كاذب مثله . بيان الشرطية : إن السعادة والبهجة لا معنى لها ، إلا ما يوجب اللذة والسرور . وهذه اللذة والسرور إما أن تحصل بعد الموت ، أو قبل الموت. فإن كانت النفس لا تبقى بعد موت البدن ، امتنع حصول اللذة والبهجة والسرور بعد موت البدن ، فلم يبق للسعادة حصول إلا قبل الموت ، فوجب أن يكون الاستكثار من هذه اللذات والخيرات ، استكثاراً من السعادة ، والتقليل منها تقليلا من السعادة . وذلك يدل على صحة ما قلنا : إنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية ، لكان كمال الإنسان من الاستكثار من هذه اللذات البدنية ، والراحات الجسمانية. وقد بينا في المقدمة الأولى أن هذا باطل ، فوجب أن يكون القول بأن النفس غير باقية باطلا قطعاً . واعلم : أنا نكتب فصلاً طويلاً في بيان أن السعادات النفسانية - أعني
The ninth proof: All intellects judge that the less one indulges in bodily pleasures and physical goods, the more perfect that person is; and the more one indulges in them, the baser that person is. The evidence for this is that people, if they believe that a person abstains from bodily pleasures such as eating, drinking, and intercourse, and abstains from seeking worldly leadership and surpassing peers, then this person's status is magnified in their minds. And you see that those who seek these pleasures serve that person who abstains from them, as slaves serve masters, and they consider themselves in relation to those abstainers like beasts in relation to angels. Even an impostor, when he wants to attract followers, pretends—by way of deception—to have little desire for these bodily goods and physical comforts, and thereby achieves their service and commanding them. This indicates that it is established in the minds of all creatures that reducing these pleasures necessitates perfection, and increasing them necessitates deficiency. A premise whose correctness is attested by the intuitions of intellects and pure thoughts must be a true and certain premise. If this is established, we say: If the soul were not to survive, then the perfection of happiness would be in maximizing these pleasures, and the perfection of misery in minimizing them. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Happiness and delight have no meaning except what brings pleasure and joy. This pleasure and joy either occur after death or before death. If the soul does not survive after the death of the body, then it is impossible to obtain pleasure, delight, and joy after the death of the body, so happiness can only be obtained before death. Therefore, maximizing these pleasures and goods would be maximizing happiness, and minimizing them would be minimizing happiness. That proves the truth of what we said: if the soul were not to survive, then the perfection of man would be in maximizing these bodily pleasures and physical comforts. And we have shown in the first premise that this is false, so the statement that the soul is not surviving is necessarily false. And know: We will write a long chapter explaining that the spiritual happinesses—meaning
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof for the soul's survival, based on a universally recognized premise. It first states that all rational minds agree that the less one indulges in physical pleasures, the more perfect one is, and vice versa. The evidence given is empirical: people honor ascetics and serve them, while pleasure-seekers are seen as lower. Even deceivers pretend to be ascetic to gain followers, showing that this judgment is deeply ingrained. Since this premise is universally and intuitively certain, the argument proceeds: if the soul did not survive, then the only happiness would be worldly pleasure, so maximizing pleasure would be perfection and minimizing it would be misery. But this contradicts the universal premise. Therefore, the soul must survive. The argument is then elaborated: happiness is only attained through pleasure; if there is no afterlife, pleasure and happiness can only occur before death; thus maximizing pleasure is maximizing happiness, and minimizing it is minimizing happiness, making asceticism a path to misery. Since asceticism is actually seen as perfection, the premise that the soul does not survive is false. The passage ends with a note that the author will write a long chapter on spiritual happinesses (possibly to elaborate further).

Key Terms
العقول
اللذات البدنية
الكمال
السعادة
النفس
البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that physical pleasures are inherently evil or worthless.
Correction: The proof does not deny all value to physical pleasures; it only uses the universal judgment that reducing them is a sign of perfection to argue for the soul's survival.

Checkpoint
What universal judgment does the proof rely on?
A) Physical pleasures are always evil
B) Reducing physical pleasures indicates perfection
C) Happiness is only possible after death
D) The soul is material

Key Takeaway
The universal respect for asceticism proves that happiness cannot be limited to worldly pleasures, so the soul must survive after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that deep down you respect someone who is detached from worldly pleasures more than one who is consumed by them. Why then do you spend so much energy chasing comforts you intellectually look down upon? What would you have to believe about your own soul to change what you seek most?
?
If the universal judgment of humanity is that reducing pleasures leads to perfection, then your own heart is testifying against your actions. When you last indulged in something you knew was excessive, did you feel that nagging sense of diminished worth? What one pleasure could you cut back on starting tomorrow to begin aligning your life with your own innate conviction?
?
Imagine you were to die tonight and stand before your Creator. Would your pursuit of this world make sense then? If the soul survives, as the proof insists, then every moment spent chasing fleeting delights is a moment traded away from eternal joy. Are you living as if you truly believe that? If not, what is stopping you from making one concrete change right now?

The Conditional Proof for the Soul's Survival
Al-Razi presents a conditional argument for the soul's survival based on universal human perception of pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's modus tollens argument for the survival of the soul, based on universal human perception of pleasure and its implications.

Core Idea
Universal human consensus that minimizing pleasure is perfection proves the soul's survival.

Al-Razi constructs a powerful logical argument for the soul's survival that starts not from revelation but from a universal human observation. He notices that people universally respect those who abstain from worldly pleasures, indicating that minimizing such pleasures is seen as perfection. This observation becomes the foundation for a conditional argument that, if valid, forces the conclusion that the soul must survive death. In this lesson, we will unpack the steps of his argument and see how it works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow from observation to conclusion. Pay attention to the logical terms like 'if...then'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the conditional structure
Find the conditional statement: 'If the soul were not remaining, then the perfection of happiness would be in maximizing these pleasures.' Recognize this as the antecedent (P) and consequent (Q).
The consequent is about maximizing pleasures.
3
Understand the modus tollens
Al-Razi argues that the consequent (Q) is false because it contradicts the universal premise that minimizing pleasures is perfection. Since Q is false, the antecedent (P) must also be false. Hence the soul remains.
This is modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن طلب الرئاسة في الدنيا والتفوق على الأقران، فإنه يعظم قدر هذا الإنسان في عقولهم. وترى الطالبين لهذه اللذات يخدمون ذلك الإنسان المعرض عنها خدمة العبيد للأرباب، ويعدون أنفسهم بالنسبة إلى أولئك المعرضين كالبهائم بالنسبة إلى الملائكة. حتى إن المزور إذا أراد استباع الخلق أظهر من نفسه على سبيل التزوير قلة الرغبة في هذه الخيرات البدنية والراحات الجسمية، ويتوصل بها إلى استخدامهم والأمر والنهي عليهم. وهذا يدل على أن تقرر في عقول جميع الخلق: أن التقليل من هذه اللذات يوجب الكمال، وأن التكثير منها يوجب النقصان. والمقدمة التي شهدت بصحتها بدائه العقول وصرائح الأفكار لا بد وأن تكون مقدمة حقة يقينية. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو لم تكن النفس باقية لكان كمال السعادة في الاستكثار من هذه اللذات، وكمال الشقاوة في التقليل منها. والتالي كاذب، فالمقدم كاذب مثله. بيان الشرطية: إن السعادة والبهجة لا معنى لها إلا ما يوجب اللذة والسرور. وهذه اللذة والسرور إما أن تحصل بعد الموت أو قبل الموت. فإن كانت النفس لا تبقى بعد موت البدن امتنع حصول اللذة والبهجة والسرور بعد موت البدن، فلم يبق للسعادة حصول إلا قبل الموت، فوجب أن يكون الاستكثار من هذه اللذات والخيرات استكثاراً من السعادة، والتقليل منها تقليلاً من السعادة. وذلك يدل على صحة ما قلنا: إنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية لكان كمال الإنسان من الاستكثار من هذه اللذات البدنية والراحات الجسمانية. وقد بينا في المقدمة الأولى أن هذا باطل، فوجب أن يكون القول بأن النفس غير باقية باطلاً قطعاً.
Concerning the pursuit of leadership in the world and superiority over peers, it magnifies the status of this person in their minds. And you see the seekers of these pleasures serving that person who turns away from them, as slaves serve masters, and they consider themselves in relation to those who turn away as beasts in relation to angels. Even the fraudulent person, when he wants to attract people, displays from himself — by way of fraud — a lack of desire for these bodily goods and physical comforts, and thereby achieves using them and commanding and forbidding them. This indicates that it is established in the minds of all people that reducing these pleasures necessitates perfection, and increasing them necessitates deficiency. And a premise whose correctness is witnessed by the innate dispositions of minds and the clear thoughts must be a true, certain premise. If this is established, we say: If the soul were not remaining, then the perfection of happiness would be in maximizing these pleasures, and the perfection of misery would be in minimizing them. But the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false likewise. Explanation of the conditional: Happiness and delight have no meaning except what necessitates pleasure and joy. And this pleasure and joy either occur after death or before death. If the soul does not survive after the death of the body, then the attainment of pleasure, delight, and joy after the death of the body is impossible, so nothing remains for happiness except before death. Therefore, maximizing these pleasures and goods must be maximizing happiness, and minimizing them must be minimizing happiness. That indicates the correctness of what we said: If the soul were not remaining, the perfection of man would be in maximizing these bodily pleasures and physical comforts. And we have shown in the first premise that this is false. Therefore, the statement that the soul is not remaining must be absolutely false.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a conditional proof (modus tollens) for the survival of the soul. It begins with an observed universal human behavior: people respect those who abstain from worldly pleasures, indicating that minimizing pleasures is seen as perfection. This is presented as an innate, evident premise. The argument then constructs a conditional: if the soul does not survive death, then perfect happiness would consist in maximizing bodily pleasures (since only worldly pleasures would exist). But this consequent is false because it contradicts the established premise that minimizing pleasures is perfection. Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (the soul's non-survival) must also be false. The author carefully explains the conditional by noting that happiness is defined by pleasure, and since bodily pleasures only exist before death, without an afterlife, the only path to happiness would be maximizing them. But that conclusion is absurd given the universal human judgment, so the soul must survive.

Key Terms
نفس (nafs, soul)
بقاء (baqa', survival)
لذة (ladhha, pleasure)
سعادة (sa'ada, happiness)
شقاوة (shaqawa, misery)
مقدمة (muqaddima, premise)
تالي (tālī, consequent)
مقدم (muqaddam, antecedent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes the conclusion (circular reasoning).
Correction: The argument starts from an empirical observation accepted by all, not from the desired conclusion. It proceeds logically to deduce the soul's survival.

Checkpoint
What logical form does al-Razi use in this argument for the soul's survival?
A) Modus ponens
B) Modus tollens
C) Syllogism
D) Induction

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's modus tollens argument uses universal human intuition about pleasure to prove the soul's survival after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If minimizing worldly pleasures is universally seen as a sign of perfection, what does your own deep attachment to comfort and luxury reveal about your belief in the soul's survival? Are you living as if this life is all there is?
?
Al-Razi points out that even the fraudulent person pretends to be detached to gain influence. In what areas of your life do you outwardly display piety while inwardly craving recognition or pleasure? What would it take to align your inner desires with your claimed beliefs about the afterlife?
?
When you see someone who sincerely renounces worldly pleasures for the sake of the Hereafter, do you feel a sense of awe or discomfort? What does that reaction tell you about the state of your own soul's conviction? What step can you take today to live more consistently with the belief that the soul remains?

Eleventh and Twelfth Arguments: Resurrection and the Survival of the Soul
Al-Razi uses rational and revealed proofs for resurrection to argue for the soul's survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's two arguments for the survival of the soul after death, based on rational impossibility of bodily resurrection and scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection would require recreating the non-existent, which is impossible, so the soul must survive.

Al-Razi presents two arguments to prove that the soul survives after the death of the body. The first is a rational argument that bodily resurrection is impossible because it would involve recreating what has become non-existent. The second is a revealed argument pointing to the many Quranic verses and hadiths that affirm the soul's persistence. Together, these arguments establish the correct position in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the two arguments
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the structure: two proofs. The first is labeled 'eleventh proof', the second 'twelfth proof'.
Pay attention to the potential textual corruption in the eleventh proof.
2
Analyze the rational argument
Examine the reasoning: bodily resurrection requires i'ādat al-ma'dūm (recreating the non-existent), which is impossible. Therefore, resurrection must be of the soul. Note the confusing phrase that seems to apply the same impossibility to the soul—this is likely a textual error.
The intended conclusion is that the soul survives, not that it requires recreation.
3
Consider the revealed argument
The twelfth proof is a brief appeal to scripture. Reflect on why Al-Razi includes this separate argument and how it supports the rational conclusion.
The verses and hadith are said to have been mentioned previously, so they are not repeated here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر: إن الدلائل العقلية والنقلية متطابقة على أنه لا بد من البعث والقيامة. والقول بأن البعث والقيامة إنما يحصل لهذه الأبدان باطل. لأن ذلك لا يصح إلا مع القول بإعادة المعدوم على ما بيناه: وهو محال. فوجب أن يحصل البعث والقيامة للنفس، وذلك يقتضي إعادة المعدوم، وهو محال. فثبت بما ذكرناه: أن القول ببقاء النفس بعد موت الجسد هو القول الصحيح. الحجة الثانية عشر: الآيات والأخبار الكثيرة الدالة على أن النفس تبقى بعد موت الجسد وقد سبق ذكرها. والله أعلم.
The eleventh proof: The rational and transmitted proofs agree that resurrection and the standing (for judgment) are necessary. The statement that resurrection and the standing occur only for these bodies is false, because that is not valid except with the assertion of recreating the non-existent, as we have explained, and that is impossible. Therefore, resurrection and the standing must occur for the soul, and that necessitates recreating the non-existent, and that is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the statement of the survival of the soul after the death of the body is the correct statement. The twelfth proof: The many verses and reports indicating that the soul survives after the death of the body, and they have been mentioned previously. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments. The eleventh argument begins by noting that both reason and revelation affirm resurrection. It then argues that resurrection cannot be for bodies because that would require recreating what has been annihilated (i'ādat al-ma'dūm), which is impossible. The text then states that resurrection must be for the soul, but oddly adds that this also necessitates recreating the non-existent and is impossible. This appears to be a textual corruption; the likely intended meaning is that the impossibility of bodily resurrection forces us to accept that the soul survives and is resurrected, not that it requires recreation. The twelfth argument is a brief appeal to the many Quranic verses and hadiths that indicate the soul's survival, which the author says have been previously cited.

Key Terms
بعث (ba'th, resurrection)
قيامة (qiyama, standing/judgment)
إعادة المعدوم (i'ādat al-ma'dūm, recreation of the non-existent)
نفس (nafs, soul)
جسد (jasad, body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the eleventh argument contradicts itself by saying resurrection for the soul also requires recreating the non-existent and is impossible.
Correction: The confusing phrase is likely a textual corruption. The intended argument is that bodily resurrection is impossible, so the soul must be the subject of resurrection, and that is the correct view.

Checkpoint
According to the eleventh argument, why is resurrection for the body considered impossible?
A) The body decays and cannot be reconstituted
B) It would require recreating the non-existent, which is impossible
C) The soul is the only locus of identity
D) God does not create bodies twice

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that resurrection cannot be of the physical body because that would require recreating what has become non-existent, an impossibility, so the soul must survive and be the subject of resurrection, supported by scriptural evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul will face resurrection and judgment, what are you doing today to prepare it? Does your daily routine reflect a belief that you are more than a body? Name one concrete change you can make starting tomorrow to prioritize your soul.
?
The rational argument shows that bodily resurrection alone is impossible, so the soul must be the real subject of resurrection. When you experience pain or pleasure, do you remind yourself that your soul is the essence of who you are? How can you cultivate that awareness in your daily life?
?
The twelfth argument appeals to scriptures about the soul's survival. Have you personally read those verses and hadiths? If not, what is stopping you? Commit to looking up and reflecting on at least three of them this week.

Tenth Argument: Created for Good, Thus Soul Survives
Sanusi argues from purpose to soul's survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument based on God's wisdom and mercy that necessitates the soul's survival after bodily death.

Core Idea
Humans are created for good; this world lacks it, so the soul must survive.

Sanusi presents a proof that does not rely on scripture but on rational reflection on God's attributes. He argues that since God is Wise and Merciful, He must have created humans for a purpose of good and rest. Because this world is filled with suffering, that good must be realized after death, implying the soul's endurance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text slowly, then follow with the literal translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
The passage is concise; notice how each clause builds the case.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break the argument into its components: the premise about creation, the disjunction of possibilities, the elimination of the worldly option, and the conclusion about the soul.
This is a classic kalam argument form: premise → disjunction → elimination → conclusion.
3
Reflect on the rational necessity
Ask yourself why this argument is considered purely rational. How does it use God's attributes (Wisdom, Mercy) without quoting revelation?
This distinguishes 'aqli (rational) proofs from naqli (textual) proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : ما ذكرنا : أن الإنسان يجب أن يكون مخلوقاً للخير والراحة ، وإلا لكان إما أن يكون مخلوقاً للشر والإيذاء ؛ أو لمحض العبث . وكلاهما لا يليق بالحكيم الرحيم . فثبت : أنه مخلوق للخير والرحمة . ثم ذلك الخير والرحمة ، إما أن يكون حاصلاً قبل الموت أو بعده ، والأول باطل ، لأن الدنيا مملوءة من الشرور والآفات والمكروهات والمخافات ، فوجب أن يكون محل تلك الخيرات ما بعد الموت ، وذلك يوجب القطع بأن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن .
The tenth proof: What we mentioned: that man must be created for good and rest, otherwise he would either be created for evil and harm, or for sheer vanity. Both are unfitting for the Wise, Merciful. So it is established that he is created for good and mercy. Then that good and mercy are either obtained before death or after it, and the first is false because this world is filled with evils, afflictions, unpleasant things, and fears. So it is necessary that the place of those goods is after death, and that necessitates the certainty that the soul does not die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (al-hujjah al-'ashirah) for the survival of the soul after bodily death. It begins by stating a premise: humans must have been created for good and rest. If not, then either they are created for evil and harm or for mere vanity. Both options are incompatible with God being Wise and Merciful (al-Hakim al-Rahim). Therefore, it is established that humans are created for good and mercy. Next, that good and mercy must be realized either before death or after death. The first option is invalid because this worldly life is full of evil, afflictions, unpleasant things, and fears. Hence, the realization of those goods must be after death, which yields the conclusion that the soul does not die with the death of the body.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs: the soul)
البَدَن (al-badan: the body)
الحكيم الرحيم (al-Hakim al-Rahim: the Wise, the Merciful)
الخير والراحة (al-khayr wa al-raha: good and rest)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument denies any good in this world entirely.
Correction: The argument only says the world is 'filled with evils'—not devoid of good—so the ultimate purpose requires a hereafter.

Checkpoint
Why must the good for which humans are created be after death?
A) Because God is Wise and Merciful
B) Because the soul is immortal by nature
C) Because this world is filled with evils and afflictions
D) Because the afterlife is purely spiritual

Key Takeaway
Because God is Wise and Merciful, He created humans for good; since this world is not a place of ultimate good, the soul must survive to attain it after death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your soul will survive death and that ultimate good awaits after death, how would that change the way you face the trials and pains of this world? What is one specific thing you will do today to prepare for that good?
?
The argument says this world is full of evils, afflictions, and fears. When you experience a present difficulty, do you view it as a reason to despair or as a sign that your true purpose lies beyond? Name one fear you can hand over to God right now because you know He has prepared good for you after death.
?
The proof begins with the premise that you are created for good and rest. If you lived today as if that were absolutely true, what would you do differently? Write down one concrete action you will take tonight to align your life with that purpose.

The Eleventh Proof: Resurrection of the Soul
Sanusi proves bodily resurrection impossible, concluding resurrection is for the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that resurrection must be of the soul rather than the body, based on the impossibility of re-creating the non-existent.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is impossible; the soul must be what is resurrected.

Sanusi presents the eleventh proof, drawing on both rational and transmitted evidences to establish the necessity of resurrection. He then refutes the idea that this resurrection is for the body, showing that it would require re-creating the non-existent—a logical impossibility. The conclusion is that resurrection must be for the soul, which survives the death of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation.
Note the bracketed portion is a restoration; the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the premises
Find the two main premises: (1) rational and transmitted proofs agree on the necessity of resurrection, (2) bodily resurrection would require re-creation of the non-existent, which is impossible.
The argument assumes prior proof that re-creation of the non-existent is impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice that since resurrection cannot be for the body, it must be for the soul. Therefore, the soul survives death.
The conclusion does not mean the soul is eternal, only that it persists after bodily death.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Trace the logical flow: necessity of resurrection → bodily resurrection impossible → resurrection for soul → soul survives.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument against bodily resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : إن الدلائل العقلية والنقلية متطابقة على أنه لا بد من البعث والقيامة . والقول [ بأن البعث والقيامة إنما يحصل لهذه الأبدان باطل . لأن ذلك لا يصح إلا مع القول ] بإعادة المعدوم على ما بيناه : وهو محال . فوجب أن يحصل البعث والقيامة للنفس ، وذلك يقتضي إعادة المعدوم ، وهو محال . فثبت بما ذكرناه : أن القول ببقاء النفس بعد موت الجسد هو القول الصحيح .
The eleventh proof: Indeed, the rational and transmitted evidences agree that the Resurrection and the Afterlife must occur. And the statement [that the Resurrection and the Afterlife only occur for these bodies is false. Because that is only valid with the statement] of re-creation of the non-existent, as we have explained: and that is impossible. So it must be that the Resurrection and the Afterlife occur for the soul, and that would entail re-creation of the non-existent, which is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the statement of the survival of the soul after the death of the body is the correct statement.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof (al-hujjah al-hadiyah 'asharah) for the soul's survival. It begins by noting that both rational (aqli) and transmitted (naqli) proofs agree that Resurrection and Afterlife (al-ba'th wa al-qiyamah) are necessary. The opponent claims that this resurrection is of the bodies (al-abdan). But that would require re-creating the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum), which is proven impossible. Therefore, resurrection cannot be of the bodies. The concluding statement clarifies: since resurrection must occur and cannot be of the body, it must be of the soul. Yet the soul is already existing, so no re-creation of the non-existent is needed. Hence, the correct position is that the soul survives the death of the body.

Key Terms
البعث والقيامة (al-ba'th wa al-qiyamah: Resurrection and Afterlife)
إعادة المعدوم (i'adat al-ma'dum: re-creation of the non-existent)
النفس (al-nafs: the soul)
الجسد (al-jasad: the body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resurrection must be of the physical body.
Correction: Bodily resurrection would require re-creating the non-existent (iʿādat al-maʿdūm), which is impossible. Thus resurrection must be of the soul, which survives death.

Checkpoint
Why does the eleventh argument reject the idea that resurrection is for the body?
A) Because bodies decay and cannot be restored
B) Because it would require re-creation of the non-existent, which is impossible
C) Because the soul is already resurrected
D) Because rational proofs show only souls matter

Key Takeaway
The soul survives bodily death; resurrection is of the soul, not the body, because bodily resurrection would require the impossible re-creation of the non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body will never rise again, but your soul will, what does that mean for how you treat your body now? Are you prioritizing temporary physical pleasures over the eternal state of your soul?
?
Do you truly believe that your soul survives death? If you did, how would your fear of death change, and how would your daily choices reflect that certainty?
?
The proof says bodily resurrection is impossible. Does that make you uneasy, or does it free you from attachment to the material world? Examine your heart: are you more invested in your physical life or your spiritual journey?

Consensus of the Elite on the Soul's Survival
Sanusi uses consensus of spiritual elite to prove soul's survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of prophets, saints, and divine philosophers serves as a persuasive argument for the soul's survival after death.

Core Idea
The best of humanity all agree: the soul lives on.

In this passage, Sanusi presents his first persuasive argument for the soul's survival. Rather than relying on pure logic, he appeals to the consensus of those whose lives are most dedicated to the divine — prophets, saints, and divine philosophers. This argument draws on their authority and the consistency between their belief and their lifestyle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi defines the groups and builds his argument.
2
Identify the three elite groups
The passage names three groups: prophets (anbiya'), saints (awliya'), and divine philosophers (hukama' ilahiyyun). Understand why Sanusi separates them from natural philosophers and mathematicians.
These three groups share a focus on the afterlife.
3
Analyze the consensus claim
Sanusi claims these groups unanimously agree that the soul remains after death. Ask yourself: what evidence does he provide for this consensus?
Their lifestyle is presented as proof.
4
Reflect on the persuasive value
Consider why Sanusi considers this consensus persuasive. How does the behavior of these groups support their testimony?
The author advises studying their biographies for confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر في تقرير المجوه الاقناعية في بيان أن النفس با دعوت الجسم . الحجة الأولى : إن جمهوراً من العقلاء أطبقوا على أن أفضل أقسام البشر : الأنبياء والأولياء والحكماء الإلهيون . أما الحكماء الطبيعيون والرياضيون فالفائدة في وجودهم : لأجل مصالح الحياة العاجلة . ولما كانت هذه الحياة خسيسة ، كان الخادمون لها غير موصوفين بالشرف . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : أجمع الأولياء والأنبياء والحكماء الإلهيون : على أن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن ، وذلك لأن هؤلاء الفرق الثلاثة حرفتهم وطريقتهم : الإعراض عن الدنيا والإقبال على عالم الآخرة ، كما قال تعالى : والباقيات الصالحات خير عند ربك ثواباً ، وخير أملا ( ) وما كانوا يلتفتون إلى أحوال هذه الحياة ، وما كانوا يسعون في إصلاحها ، بل كأنه لا حرفة لهم إلا تزييف هذه الطريقة وتهجينها . ومن أراد تحقيق ذلك ، فليطالع الكتب المشتملة على شرح أحوال الأنبياء ، في الزهد في الدنيا ، والإقبال على عبادة الله تعالى . وأما الحكماء . فمن أراد معرفة أحوالهم وسيرهم في هذه الطريقة ،
The tenth chapter: On establishing the persuasive aspects in explaining that the soul [remains?] after the body. The first proof: Indeed, the majority of rational people have agreed that the best divisions of mankind are the prophets, the saints, and the divine philosophers. As for the natural philosophers and mathematicians, the benefit of their existence is for the interests of the immediate life. And since this life is base, those who serve it are not described as noble. And when this is established, we say: The saints, prophets, and divine philosophers are unanimous that the soul remains after the death of the body. This is because the craft and way of these three groups is turning away from this world and turning toward the world of the afterlife, as He said, exalted: 'And the enduring good deeds are better with your Lord in reward and better as hope' (Quran 18:46). And they never paid attention to the conditions of this life, nor did they strive to reform it. Rather, it is as if their only craft is to discredit and disparage this way. And whoever wants to verify that should study the books that explain the states of the prophets, concerning renunciation of this world and devotion to the worship of God Almighty. And as for the philosophers, whoever wants to know their states and their conduct in this way,
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (al-fasl al-'ashir) presenting persuasive arguments (al-mujwaj al-iqna'iyya) for the soul's survival after the body. The first argument (al-hujjah al-ula) is based on consensus (ijma') of the best class of people: prophets (anbiya'), saints (awliya'), and divine philosophers (hukama' ilahiyyun). These are distinguished from natural philosophers and mathematicians, who serve merely the base interests of this fleeting life. The consensus of these three noble groups is that the soul remains after death. Their entire practice—turning away from the world and towards the afterlife—supports this. The author cites Quran 18:46 as evidence that they value the enduring over the transient. He advises readers to study the biographies of prophets and saints to see their renunciation. The argument is cut off mid-sentence regarding the philosophers, but the intention is clear: the best people all agree, and their lifestyle confirms their belief.

Key Terms
المجوه الاقناعية (al-mujwaj al-iqna'iyya)
الأنبياء (al-anbiya')
الأولياء (al-awliya')
الحكماء الإلهيون (al-hukama' al-ilahiyyun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus of a select group is not authoritative.
Correction: Sanusi argues that their lifestyle and insight make their testimony reliable; their renunciation of worldly gain eliminates bias.

Key Takeaway
The consensus of prophets, saints, and divine philosophers, whose lives are devoted to the afterlife, provides strong persuasive evidence that the soul remains after death.

Third Argument for the Soul's Survival: Universal Practice of Praying for the Dead
Author presents the third argument from human practice of praying for or cursing the dead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third persuasive argument for the soul's survival based on the universal human practice of praying for or cursing the dead, and how the author refutes the objection that only the ignorant do so.

Core Idea
Universal practice of invoking good or evil upon the dead proves the soul survives.

This lesson presents the third argument from human practice. The author observes that people everywhere, from ancient times until now, consistently pray for or curse the dead. If the soul perished with the body, such actions would be meaningless. The argument is strengthened by showing that even the wise, when they follow their innate nature, engage in this practice, indicating that belief in the soul's survival is rooted in human fitra.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the opening citation of Ali ibn Abi Talib and the structure of the argument.
The argument is presented as a rhetorical proof (إقناعي).
2
Identify the core observation
Find the sentence that states the universal practice: 'We see the people of the East and the West... unanimously engaged in mentioning their dead'. Understand that this practice includes both prayer and cursing.
3
Trace the logical implication
The author argues that if the soul died with the body, then prayer and cursing would be impossible because they aim to convey good or evil to the deceased. Therefore, the universality of this practice implies the soul survives.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: universal falsehood is far-fetched.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection says only ignorant people do this; the wise do not. The response shows that even the so-called wise, when they set aside doubts and return to their fitra, will inevitably pray for or curse the dead based on how they were treated. Hence, fitra itself affirms the soul's survival.
The concept of fitra (original innate disposition) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الذي قاله الإمام علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه وأرضاه: قال المنجم والطبيب كلاهما لا تحشر الأموات. قلت: إليكما إن صح قولكما فلست بخاسر أو صح قولي فالخسار عليكم. والحجة الثالثة من الوجوه الإقناعية في بقاء النفس بعد موت البدن: إنا نرى أهل المشرق والمغرب من الزمان الأقدم إلى الآن مطبقين على ذكر موتاهم، تارة بالصلاة والرحمة والخير إن اعتقدوا فيهم الظلم والجور. ولو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن لكان هذا الكلام باطلاً، لأن الصلاة والدعاء والرحمة تضرع إلى إله العالم في إيصال خير إليه، واللعن والشتم طلب من إله العالم إيصال شر وعذاب إليه. ولو كانت النفس تموت بموت البدن وتعدم عند عدمه لكان إيصال الخير والشر إليه محالاً. فيلزم أن يقال: إن جميع أهل العالم من الزمان الأقدم قد أطبقوا على الباطل، ومعلوم أن ذلك بعيد. فإن قالوا: إن هذا الدعاء وهذا الشتم إنما يذكره أرباب الملل والنحل لجهلهم بحقائق الأشياء، وأما الحكيم العاقل الكامل فإنه لا يفعله البتة. فتقول: إن ذلك الذي نسميه بالحكيم الفاضل الكامل إذا غفل عن شبهاته وضلالاته وعاد إلى أصل فطرته ومقتضى طبيعته الأصلية، فإن كان قد ناله خير كثير من إنسان ومات ذلك الإنسان، فإنه إذا تذكر ما وصل إليه من الخيرات فلا بد وأن تحمله فطرته الأصلية على أن يذكره بالرحمة والدعاء. وإن كان قد ناله منه شر كثير فإذا تذكر تلك الأحوال فلا بد وأن يذكره باللعن والشتم. وذلك يدل على أن مقتضى الفطرة الأصلية هو الإقرار ببقاء النفس، وإن العدول عنه ليس إلا بسبب الشبهات العارضة.
And this is what the Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him, said: The astrologer and the physician both said: 'The dead will not be resurrected.' I said: 'To you both, if your statement is true then I am not a loser, or if my statement is true then the loss is upon you.' And the third proof from persuasive arguments for the survival of the soul after the death of the body: We see the people of the East and the West from the earliest times until now, unanimously engaged in mentioning their dead, sometimes with prayer, mercy, and good, if they believed them to be wrongdoers and tyrants. And if the soul were not surviving after the death of the body, this speech would be invalid, because prayer, supplication, and mercy are a humble plea to the God of the world to convey good to him, and cursing and reviling are a request from the God of the world to convey evil and punishment to him. And if the soul died with the death of the body and ceased to exist when it ceased, then conveying good and evil to it would be impossible. Then it would necessarily be said: All the people of the world from the earliest times have unanimously agreed upon falsehood, and it is known that this is far-fetched. Then if they say: This supplication and this cursing are only mentioned by the people of religions and sects due to their ignorance of the true nature of things; as for the wise, rational, and perfect person, he does not do it at all. So we say: That person whom we call the wise, virtuous, and perfect, if he is heedless of his doubts and misguidance and returns to the origin of his innate disposition and the requirement of his original nature, then if much good has come to him from a person and that person dies, then when he remembers what he received of good things, his original innate disposition will inevitably compel him to mention him with mercy and supplication. And if much evil has come to him from him, then when he remembers those circumstances, he will inevitably mention him with cursing and reviling. And that indicates that the requirement of the original innate disposition is the affirmation of the survival of the soul, and that deviation from it is only due to incidental doubts.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the survival of the soul after bodily death, framed as a rhetorical proof based on universal human practice. It begins with a citation of Ali ibn Abi Talib, stating the position of the astrologer and physician that the dead will not be resurrected, and then counters that if their claim is false, the loss is theirs. The argument proper observes that people everywhere, from the earliest times, consistently engage in praying for or cursing the dead; if the soul perished, such actions would be meaningless. The author explains that prayer is a plea to God to send good to the deceased, and cursing is a request for evil to be sent to them; but if the soul ceases to exist, such conveying of good or evil would be impossible. Hence, universal practice would be based on falsehood, which is unlikely. An objection is then anticipated: perhaps only the ignorant do this, while the wise refrain. The response is that even the so-called wise person, if he sets aside his sophistries and returns to his original innate disposition (fitra), will inevitably pray for or curse the dead depending on how he was treated. This shows that the fitra itself affirms the soul's survival, and only incidental doubts cause deviation. The argument follows a clear structure: statement of common practice, its implication, refutation of a counter-claim, and conclusion.

Key Terms
النفس
بقاء النفس
الإقناعي
الفطرة الأصلية
الشبهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is merely emotional and not logical.
Correction: It is a logical inference from universal practice to the impossibility of its being pointless.

Checkpoint

A) The wise never pray for the dead.

C) The soul is material and thus cannot survive.
D) The dead can be resurrected through astrology.

Key Takeaway
The universal human practice of praying for or cursing the dead, rooted in fitra, serves as a persuasive proof for the soul's survival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Fourth Argument: Dreams and Communication with the Dead
Author presents the fourth argument from dreams where the dead provide information and guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how reported dreams and experiences of communication with the dead provide a strong presumption (zann ghalib) for the soul's survival after death.

Core Idea
Dream reports from the dead offer empirical evidence for the soul's ongoing existence.

This lesson presents the fourth argument for the soul's survival, based not on abstract reasoning but on reported experiences. The author shares personal and historical anecdotes to build a cumulative case. We will examine these stories and understand why they produce a strong presumption (zann ghalib).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three anecdotes
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three distinct stories: the author's personal dream, the Ferdowsi dream, and the disciples of Aristotle consulting his grave.
Each anecdote illustrates that the dead can provide knowledge unavailable to the living.
2
Analyze the author's personal dream
Pay attention to the author's dream of his father, who gave him a proof about motion and the impossibility of infinite regress. Note that the author considers this proof superior to others.
This shows that the dead can offer intellectual guidance.
3
Reflect on the cumulative force of these reports
Consider why the author calls these instances a 'strong presumption' (zann ghalib). Think about how multiple consistent reports from trustworthy sources can build a plausible case even if they are not logically conclusive.
The argument appeals to common experience and credible testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: إن الإنسان قد يرى أباه وأمه في المنام، ويسألهما عن أشياء، وهما يذكران أجوبة صحيحة، وربما أرشداه إلى دفين دفنه في موضع لا يعلمه أحد. وأقول: إني حين كنت صبياً في أول التعلم، وكنت أقرأ مسألة حوادث لا أول لها. فرأيت في المنام أبي، فقال لي: أجود الدلائل: أن يقال: الحركة انتقال من حالة إلى حالة أخرى. فهي تقتضي بحسب ماهيتها كونها مسبوقة بالغير. والأول ينافي كونه مسبوقاً بالغير. فوجب أن يكون الجمع بينهما محالاً. وأقول: والظاهر أن هذا الوجه أحسن من كل ما قيل في هذه المسألة. وأيضاً: سمعت أن الفردوسي الشاعر لما صنف كتابه المسمى بشاهنامه على اسم السلطان محمود بن سبكتكين، وأنه ما قضى حقه كما يجب وما راعاه كما يليق بذلك الكتاب، ضاق قلب الفردوسي، فرأى في المنام رستم، فقال له: إنك قد مدحتني في هذا الكتاب كثيراً، وأنا في زمرة الأموات فلا أقدر على قضاء حقك. ولكن اذهب إلى الموضع الفلاني واحفر فيه، فإنك تجد فيه دفيناً، فخذه. فكان الفردوسي الشاعر يقول: إن رستم بعد موته أكثر كرماً من محمود حال حياته. وأيضاً: سمعت أن أصحاب أرسطاطاليس كل ما أشكل عليهم بحث موضوع ذهبوا إلى قبره وبحثوا في تلك المسألة فكانت المسألة والإشكال يزول. وهذه الأحوال مما تفيد ظناً غالباً أن نفوس الأموات باقية بعد موت أبدانها.
The fourth proof: A person may see his father and mother in a dream, and ask them about things, and they give correct answers, and perhaps they guide him to a treasure buried in a place no one knows. And I say: When I was a child at the beginning of learning, and I was reading the issue of events without beginning, I saw my father in a dream, and he said to me: 'The best of proofs is that one say: motion is a transition from one state to another. So by its essence it requires being preceded by another. And the first (i.e., that which is without beginning) is incompatible with being preceded by another. So it is necessary that the combination of them be impossible.' And I say: 'It appears that this aspect is better than everything that has been said on this issue.' Also, I heard that the poet Ferdowsi, when he composed his book called Shahnameh in the name of Sultan Mahmud ibn Sebuktigin, and he did not fulfill his right as required nor respect him as befitting that book, the heart of Ferdowsi became constricted, and he saw in a dream Rustam, who said to him: 'You have praised me much in this book, and I am among the dead, so I cannot repay your right. But go to such-and-such place and dig there, for you will find a treasure there; take it.' So the poet Ferdowsi used to say: 'Rustam after his death is more generous than Mahmud during his life.' Also, I heard that the disciples of Aristotle, whenever a problem of research was difficult for them, they would go to his grave and research that issue, and the issue and the difficulty would be resolved. And these states provide a strong presumption that the souls of the dead survive after the death of their bodies.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument for the soul's survival, based on empirical reports of dreams and communications with the dead. The author begins with a general observation: people see deceased parents in dreams, ask them questions, and receive correct answers or guidance to hidden treasures. This suggests that the deceased souls are aware and active. The author then provides three specific anecdotes. First, a personal account: as a child studying the problem of 'incidents without beginning' (a theological issue about the eternity of the world), he dreamed of his father, who gave him a proof that motion, being a transition, requires a prior state, making an infinite regress impossible. The author comments that this proof is superior to others. Second, the story of the poet Ferdowsi, who dreamed of the legendary hero Rustam after being disappointed by Sultan Mahmud; Rustam guided him to a treasure, showing generosity beyond the living. Third, a report that Aristotle's disciples would visit his grave to resolve difficult research problems, and the problems would be solved. The author concludes that these instances collectively produce a 'strong presumption' (zann ghalib) that souls survive after death. The argument is cumulative and appeals to common experience and credible reports rather than pure logic.

Key Terms
المنام
النفس
الظن الغالب
الحوادث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dream arguments are weak or unacceptable as evidence for the soul's survival.
Correction: While not conclusive, these reports collectively produce a strong presumption (zann ghalib) that is considered valid in this context.

The fourth argument for the soul's survival relies on reported dreams and communications with the dead, which collectively create a strong presumption of the soul's continuity.

?

?

?
The disciples visited Aristotle's grave for answers. Where do you turn when you are stuck in your understanding of faith? Does this lesson invite you to see the dead as still present in some way? How would that change your relationship with those who have passed?

The Sixth Proof: Induction from Lifespan
Sanusi presents the sixth proof using induction from lifespans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses induction (istiqra') from human and animal lifespans to argue for the soul's permanence.

Core Idea
Induction from lifespan shows that perfecting the soul preserves it.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from Sanusi's arguments for the soul's permanence. It is based on empirical observation: people who revere God and are compassionate tend to live long, while the violent die young. Sanusi extends this to animals and draws a conclusion about the soul itself. Let's examine the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure emerge naturally.
Pay attention to the flow: observation, extension, definition, conclusion.
2
Identify the two types of people and animals
Notice the contrast between those who magnify God's command and show compassion, and those who kill and harm. Similarly for harmless vs. harmful animals.
This establishes the inductive pattern.
3
Understand the terms: theoretical and practical faculties
Sanusi defines magnifying God's command as perfecting the theoretical faculty, and compassion as perfecting the practical faculty. These are key to the argument.
The theoretical faculty deals with knowledge, the practical with action.
4
Follow the analogy: preservation of body implies preservation of soul
Since perfecting these faculties preserves the body, and the soul is the locus of these faculties, it is more fitting (awlā) that perfecting them preserves the soul itself.
This is an argument from analogy (qiyās awlā).
5
Reflect on the strength of induction as a proof
Consider the inductive generalization (dominant induction) and how it leads to a rational conclusion. What are its strengths and limitations?
Sanusi uses empirical observation as a starting point for rational inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنا نرى أن كل إنسان، كان مقبلاً على التعظيم لأمر الله تعالى ، والشفقة على خلق الله تعالى، فإنه يكون عمره طويلاً ، ويبقى طول عمره مصوناً عن الآفات والبليات. وكل من كان كثير الإقدام على القتل والإضرار ، فإنه يكون عمره قصيراً ، ولا بد وأن يقع في نوع من أنواع الآفات . وهذا هو الاستقراء الغالب . بل نقول : الحيوانات السليمة تطول أعمارها ، والحيوانات المؤذية تقصر أعمارها . ومعنى التعظيم لأمر الله تكميل القوة النظرية التي للنفس . ومعنى الشفقة على خلق الله : تكميل القوة العملية التي للخلق . فهذا الاستقراء يدل على أن الاشتغال بتكميـل هـاتـين القوتين يوجب إبقاء البدن بحسب الإمكان ، فلأن يوجب إبقاء جوهر النفس مع أن محل هاتين القوتين هو جوهر النفس كان أولى
The sixth proof: We see that every human who is devoted to magnifying the command of God Most High and having compassion for God's creatures will have a long life and remain protected from afflictions and calamities throughout his life. And whoever is bold in killing and harming will have a short life and inevitably fall into some type of affliction. This is the dominant induction. Rather we say: harmless animals have long lifespans, and harmful animals have short lifespans. The meaning of magnifying the command of God is the perfection of the theoretical faculty of the soul; and the meaning of compassion for God's creatures is the perfection of the practical faculty of the creature. So this induction indicates that occupying oneself with perfecting these two faculties necessitates preserving the body as much as possible. Then it is more fitting that it necessitates preserving the substance of the soul, given that the locus of these two faculties is the substance of the soul.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by induction (istiqra'). The argument observes a correlation: those who revere God and are compassionate live long, while the violent die young. This also holds for animals. The reasoning is that reverence perfects the theoretical faculty and compassion perfects the practical faculty. Since perfecting these preserves the body, and the soul is the locus of these faculties, the soul should be even more preservable. This is an argument from analogy (awlā).

Key Terms
الاستقراء
القوة النظرية
القوة العملية
جوهر النفس
الإبقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is purely empirical and not based on rational analogy.
Correction: While it starts with empirical induction, the conclusion uses rational analogy (awlā) based on the soul being the locus of the faculties.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what does magnifying God's command perfect?
A) the practical faculty
B) the theoretical faculty
C) both
D) neither

Key Takeaway
Induction from lifespan suggests that perfecting the soul's theoretical and practical faculties preserves both body and soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly magnifying God's command by perfecting your theoretical faculty—seeking knowledge, reflecting on His signs—or is your learning mere accumulation? What concrete step will you take today to turn your knowledge into reverence?
?
The proof shows that compassion preserves the soul. Examine your heart: whom have you failed to show compassion to today? Is there someone you avoid or harm by neglect? Will you reach out to that person before you sleep tonight?
?
Sanusi's induction reveals that your actions—magnifying God's command and showing compassion—have real, observable effects on your life and soul. If you were to examine your own lifespan spiritually, are you more like those who magnify or those who harm? What is one habit you must change to align with the proof?

The Seventh Proof: Three Ranks and Permanence of Spiritual Things
Sanusi classifies human states and argues for the soul's permanence via comparative longevity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of human states into three ranks and how he uses the relative permanence of spiritual things to argue for the soul's permanence.

Core Idea
Spiritual things outlast physical things, so the soul must be permanent.

This passage presents the seventh proof for the soul's permanence. Sanusi begins by categorizing human states into three types, then argues that whatever is connected to the spiritual realm enjoys greater permanence. The soul, as a spiritual substance and the locus of divine knowledge, is most deserving of permanence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three ranks
Identify the three categories of human states: purely physical, mixed, and purely psychic. See the examples given for each.
The lowest rank includes buildings for good deeds; the middle includes speech; the highest includes true knowledge.
2
Note the argument from permanence
Observe how Sanusi compares the longevity of physical things connected to spirituality (like mosques and scriptures) with ordinary physical things.
He uses the examples of the Sacred Mosque and the Aqsa Mosque, and the revealed books.
3
Apply the reasoning to the soul
See how the soul, being purely spiritual and the receptacle of the greatest elixir (knowledge of God), must be permanent.
The argument is a fortiori: if attached physical things are long-lasting, the soul itself is even more so.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples of the Sacred Mosque and the Aqsa Mosque, and the revealed books, to see the pattern of relative permanence.
These examples are not meant to be eternal but to show exceptional longevity compared to other physical things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن أحوال الإنسانية على ثلاثة أقسام : فأدونها : الأحوال الجسمانية المحضة . مثل : الأبنية التي تبنى للخيرات ، كالمساجد والمدارس والخانقاهات ، وأوسطها : الأحوال التي تكون من جهة جسمانية ومن جهة روحانية [ وهو الكلام، فإنه من حيث إنه صوت يتولد عن الآلات الجسمانية فهو الجسمانيات (۱)] ومن حيث إنه يدل على المعاني الروحانية النفسانية كان من الروحانيات. وأعلاها الأحوال النفسانية المحضة ، وهي العلوم الحقيقية والمعارف الصادقة.
·
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : من الظاهر : أن الجسمانيات أقل قبولاً للبقاء من الروحانيات . ثم إنا نرى كل ما ينسب إلى أصحاب الروحانيات من الجسمانيات ، فإنه ينتهي في البقاء إلى أقصى الغايات الممكنة . وإن شئت فتعرف أحوال المسجد الحرام والمسجد الأقصى ، فإنك لا تجد في الدنيا بنـاء بساوتهما في البقاء
وأما المرتبة الثانية : فكل كلام يتعلق بالمعاني الروحانية ، مثل : صفات الإله سبحانه وتعالى ، وأفعال الخيرات . فإنه يبقى إلى أقصى الإمكان . وإن شئت فتعرف كتب الله تعالى المنزلة . كالتوراة والإنجيل والزبور والفرقان . ومثل : كتب الحكمة. على اختلاف أصنافها فإنها باقية على وجه الدهر ، فلما رأينا أن الجسمانيات لما تعلقت بالروحانيات بقيت بقاء عظيما ، مع أن الأصل في الجسمانيات هو الفناء والانقراض والانقضاء . فالنفس التي هي جوهرة روحانية ، بحسب الذات ، وبحسب جميع الصفات، ثم إنها محل معرفة الله تعالى ، التي هي الإكسير الأعظم الموجب للبقاء الدائم ، لأن تكون موصوفة بالبقاء والنقاء مبرأة (۱) ] عن التغير والفناء ، كان أولى
The seventh proof: The human states are of three types: the lowest are purely physical states, such as buildings erected for good deeds, like mosques, schools, and hospices. The middle states are those that are partly physical and partly spiritual - namely speech, for insofar as it is sound generated by physical instruments, it is physical, and insofar as it indicates spiritual-psychic meanings, it is spiritual. The highest are purely psychic states, namely true sciences and sincere cognitions.
If you have understood this, we say: it is evident that physical things are less capable of permanence than spiritual things. Moreover, we see that every physical thing attributed to spiritual people attains the utmost possible permanence. If you wish, consider the state of the Sacred Mosque and the Aqsa Mosque; you will find no building in the world that equals them in permanence.
As for the second rank: every speech concerning spiritual meanings, such as the attributes of God Most High and acts of goodness, remains to the utmost possibility. If you wish, consider the revealed books of God, such as the Torah, the Gospel, the Psalms, and the Criterion, and likewise the books of wisdom of various kinds; they remain throughout the ages. So when we see that physical things, when connected to spiritual things, remain with great permanence, even though the origin of physical things is annihilation, passing, and termination - then the soul, which is a spiritual substance by essence and by all attributes, and moreover is the locus of knowledge of God Most High, which is the greatest elixir necessitating eternal permanence, is more deserving to be described with permanence and purity, free from change and annihilation.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents a proof by classification and comparison. The author divides human states into three: purely physical (buildings), mixed (speech), and purely spiritual (knowledge). He then argues that spiritual things are more permanent and that physical things attached to spirituality (like mosques and scriptures) share in that permanence. Since the soul is purely spiritual and the receptacle of divine knowledge (the greatest elixir of permanence), it must be most permanent. The argument uses examples from Islam (the two mosques) and previous scriptures.

Key Terms
الأحوال الجسمانية
الأحوال الروحانية
النفسانية المحضة
الكلام
الإكسير الأعظم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that all physical things are permanent or that physical things can never be permanent.
Correction: The proof acknowledges that physical things are inherently perishable, but when attached to spiritual purposes, they gain relative permanence; the argument is one of degree.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh proof, which category of human states is the highest?
A) purely physical
B) mixed physical-spiritual
C) purely psychic
D) all equal

Key Takeaway
The soul, as a purely spiritual substance and the locus of divine knowledge, is the most permanent of all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the soul is the most permanent thing because it is the locus of knowledge of God. If you truly believe that, why do you spend so much time on physical pursuits that will vanish, and so little time acquiring that knowledge? What concrete change will you make today to prioritize the elixir of divine knowledge over temporary physical concerns?
?
Sanusi says that even physical things connected to spirituality (like mosques) last longer. Consider your own actions: Are your daily habits connected to spiritual meanings, or are they merely physical? Will any of them leave a lasting trace in your soul? If not, what is one action you can realign with spiritual intention today?
?
What is the 'greatest elixir' for your soul according to this lesson? Are you actually taking that elixir, or are you neglecting it? Imagine standing before God tomorrow—would you be satisfied with your investment in the permanent? What will you do about it starting now?

The Eighth Proof: Divine Knowledge Strengthens the Soul
Sanusi presents an inductive proof that divine knowledge strengthens the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inductive proof that divine knowledge strengthens the soul, making it fearless, and that this implies the soul's potential for permanence.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge gives the soul strength that outlasts worldly fears.

Sanusi presents a proof from induction (الاستقراء) that observing the effect of divine knowledge on the human soul reveals its power. When a person experiences the light of knowledge of God, their soul becomes strong and fearless. When that light departs, they become weak again. This chapter uses the famous story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas to illustrate the point. Let's read the evidence carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the inductive claim
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi states the inductive observation: whenever divine knowledge manifests, the soul strengthens and becomes fearless of worldly powers.
The claim is general: every time this knowledge appears, this effect follows.
2
Note the reversal
Read the second part: when the unveiling departs, the person returns to fearing even small things. This shows the dependence of fearlessness on knowledge.
This contrast is crucial for the argument.
3
Examine the story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas
Read the story: during unveiling, beasts do not frighten him; after it departs, a mosquito causes distress. This illustrates the inductive principle.
The story is cut off, but the lesson is clear.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if the soul becomes so strong through divine knowledge, what does that suggest about its nature? The implication is that the soul can attain permanence through such knowledge.
This connects to the broader argument for the soul's permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : الاستقراء يدل على أن الإنسان ، كلما تجلى في قلبه نور معرفة الله تعالى ، وانكشفت له صفات جلالـه وكبريائه ، فإنه تقـوى نفسه وتكمل قوته ، ويصير بحيث لا يبالي بأعظم سلاطين العالم ، ولا يقع في عينه منصب أحد البتة
.
وأما إذا زال ذلك الكشف والتجلي ، فإنه يعود إلى حالته الأولى بحيث يخاف من أقل الأشياء ، ويحذر أصغر الموجبات. يحكى أنه إبراهيم الخواص » كان في البادية ، ومعه بعض مريديه فاتفق في بعض الليالي ، أن وقع في مقام الكشف . فاضطجع هناك ، وأحاطت به السباع ، وأنه لم يلتفت إلى شيء . منها . وأما المريدون فخافوا من تلك السباع ، فصعدوا بعض الأشجار خوفاً منها ، وفي الليلة الثانية زالت تلك الحالة ، فاضطجع ، فوقعت بعوضة على يده ، فأظهر التوجع من وقع تلك البعوضة . فقال له بعض المريدين (٢) : لم
The eighth proof: Induction indicates that whenever the light of knowledge of God Most High manifests in a person's heart and the attributes of His majesty and grandeur are unveiled to him, his soul becomes strong and its power is perfected, and he becomes such that he does not care about the greatest sultans of the world, and the rank of anyone does not fall into his eyes at all.
But when that unveiling and manifestation departs, he returns to his first state such that he fears the smallest things and is wary of the least occasions. It is narrated that Ibrahim al-Khawwas was in the desert with some of his disciples. It happened one night that he attained the station of unveiling. So he lay down there, surrounded by wild beasts, and he paid no attention to them. As for the disciples, they feared those beasts and climbed trees out of fear. On the second night, that state departed, so he lay down. A mosquito landed on his hand, and he showed distress from the bite of that mosquito. One of the disciples said to him: 'Why...'
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from induction about the effect of divine knowledge on the soul. The argument observes that knowledge of God strengthens the soul to the point of fearlessness towards worldly powers and dangers. When the knowledge departs, the person becomes weak and fearful. The story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas illustrates this: during a state of unveiling, beasts did not frighten him; when the state passed, even a mosquito caused him pain. This shows that the soul's power varies with divine knowledge, implying its potential for permanence through such knowledge. The story is cut off but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
الكشف
التجلي
نور معرفة الله
القوة النفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas proves that saints are physically protected from harm.
Correction: Sanusi uses the story to illustrate the psychological effect of divine knowledge on the soul, not physical protection. The fearlessness is a result of inner strength, not supernatural immunity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's eighth proof, what happens when the light of knowledge of God manifests in a person's heart?
A) The person becomes fearful of worldly powers.
B) The person's soul becomes strong and fearless of worldly powers.
C) The person becomes confused.
D) The person loses all emotions.

Key Takeaway
The strength of the soul is directly tied to the degree of divine knowledge it possesses, which points to its potential for permanence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are alone with your greatest fears—whether it's losing status, health, or loved ones—do you feel the strength that comes from knowing God, or do you find yourself trembling at shadows? What specific fear in your life right now would dissolve if you had a deeper unveiling of God's majesty?
?
Think of a time you felt spiritually strong and fearless. What changed? Like Ibrahim al-Khawwas, have you experienced a 'mosquito' that troubled you after a period of closeness? What does that tell you about the need to constantly renew your knowledge of God, rather than rely on past experiences?
?
The proof shows that without divine knowledge, we fear even the smallest things. What 'small things' (people's opinions, money, tomorrow's worries) are currently robbing you of courage? Commit to one concrete action today to seek the light of knowledge of God in a specific area of your life.

The Soul's Permanence: An Argument from Analogy
Argument from analogy for the immortality of the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from analogy that the soul, as a spiritual substance and locus of divine knowledge, is more deserving of permanence than physical structures.

Core Idea
The soul's connection to divine knowledge makes it more permanent than any physical thing.

This passage presents a compelling argument for the soul's immortality. The author observes that even physical buildings like mosques gain remarkable permanence when connected to spiritual meanings. Since the soul is itself a spiritual substance and the very seat of knowledge of God—the greatest elixir of permanence—it is even more fitting that it endures forever. This lesson unpacks that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the author's use of examples: mosques and scriptures.
2
Identify the two examples
The author gives two main examples: the permanence of mosques (Sacred Mosque and Al-Aqsa) and the permanence of spiritual meanings like divine attributes and revealed books.
These examples illustrate that even physical things can last when tied to spirituality.
3
Understand the principle
From these examples, derive the principle: corporeal things, when connected to spiritual meanings, gain great durability despite their natural tendency to perish.
4
Apply the principle to the soul
Now apply this principle to the soul: the soul is a spiritual substance and, most importantly, the locus of knowledge of God—the greatest elixir that causes eternal permanence.
The soul's innate spirituality plus its connection to divine knowledge makes it even more deserving of permanence.
5
Conclude with the author's claim
Therefore, the soul is more fitting to be described as permanent, pure, and free from change than any physical structure.
This is the conclusion of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتعرف أحوال المسجد الحرام والمسجد الأقصى ، فإنك لا تجد في الدنيا بنـاء بساوتهما في البقاء وأما المرتبة الثانية : فكل كلام يتعلق بالمعاني الروحانية ، مثل : صفات الإله سبحانه وتعالى ، وأفعال الخيرات . فإنه يبقى إلى أقصى الإمكان . وإن شئت فتعرف كتب الله تعالى المنزلة . كالتوراة والإنجيل والزبور والفرقان . ومثل : كتب الحكمة. على اختلاف أصنافها فإنها باقية على وجه الدهر ، فلما رأينا أن الجسمانيات لما تعلقت بالروحانيات بقيت بقاء عظيما ، مع أن الأصل في الجسمانيات هو الفناء والانقراض والانقضاء . فالنفس التي هي جوهرة روحانية ، بحسب الذات ، وبحسب جميع الصفات، ثم إنها محل معرفة الله تعالى ، التي هي الإكسير الأعظم الموجب للبقاء الدائم ، لأن تكون موصوفة بالبقاء والنقاء مبرأة عن التغير والفناء ، كان أولى
So you know the conditions of the Sacred Mosque and the Al-Aqsa Mosque, for you will not find in the world a building equal to them in permanence. And as for the second rank: every speech pertaining to spiritual meanings, such as the attributes of God, exalted and high, and the acts of goodness, it remains to the utmost possibility. And if you wish, then know the revealed books of God, like the Torah, the Gospel, the Psalms, and the Criterion. And like the books of wisdom, of their different kinds, they remain forever. So since we saw that corporeal things, when they become connected to spiritual things, remain with great permanence, despite the fact that the origin of corporeal things is annihilation, extinction, and passing away, then the soul, which is a spiritual substance, in essence and in all attributes, and moreover it is the locus of knowledge of God, which is the greatest elixir that necessitates eternal permanence, it is more deserving to be described with permanence and purity, free from change and annihilation.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from analogy for the immortality of the soul. The author begins by pointing to the remarkable permanence of physical structures like the Sacred Mosque and Al-Aqsa Mosque, which have survived for centuries despite being corporeal. He then distinguishes a second rank of things: spiritual meanings, such as the attributes of God and good deeds, which endure to the utmost possibility. He also mentions revealed scriptures and books of wisdom, which remain forever. The key observation is that even physical objects, when connected to spiritual meanings, gain great durability, although the natural state of corporeal things is extinction. Since the soul is itself a spiritual substance and the locus of knowledge of God—which is the greatest elixir causing eternal permanence—it is even more fitting that the soul be described as permanent and pure, immune to change and annihilation.

Key Terms
النفس
الروحانيات
الجسمانيات
معرفة الله
الإكسير الأعظم
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul's spiritual nature alone guarantees immortality, but the argument hinges on its connection to divine knowledge.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the soul's permanence is due to being the locus of knowledge of God, which is the greatest elixir of permanence, not merely because it is spiritual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the soul more deserving of permanence than physical buildings like mosques?
A) Because the soul is made of a finer substance.
B) Because the soul is spiritual and the locus of knowledge of God, which is the greatest elixir of permanence.
C) Because the soul does not decay like the body.
D) Because the soul is immortal by nature.

Key Takeaway
The soul's permanence is argued not merely from its spirituality but from its role as the locus of knowledge of God, the greatest elixir of permanence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul's permanence depends on its connection to knowledge of God, how much of your daily effort goes into acquiring that knowledge compared to temporary worldly pursuits?
?
The author says even physical structures like mosques endure because of their spiritual meaning. What spiritual meaning does your life carry that would give it permanence beyond your fleeting moments?
?
Are you treating your soul as a mere container for passing thoughts, or as the locus of the greatest elixir—knowledge of God? What would change if you truly believed your soul's eternity depends on that knowledge right now?
