Induction from Spiritual Experiences
The author presents an inductive proof from spiritual experiences to argue for the soul's strength and immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how spiritual experiences of divine unveiling demonstrate the soul's inherent strength and its indifference to worldly harm, supporting the soul's survival after death.

Core Idea
Spiritual unveiling makes the soul fearless, proving its power over the body.

In this proof, the author uses an inductive argument based on observable spiritual states. He presents a pattern: when a person experiences the light of divine knowledge, they become fearless; when it departs, they are weak. The famous story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas illustrates this clearly. By tracing this logic, you will see how the soul's strength at death points to its immortality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the flow of the proof and the story settle in your mind.
Notice the contrast between the two nights.
2
Identify the inductive claim
Find the opening statement that sets up the inductive principle: whenever divine light appears, the soul becomes strong and fearless. This is the core premise.
3
Analyze the illustrative story
Read the story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas. See how it provides concrete evidence for the inductive claim. Compare his states on the two nights.
The story is not just anecdotal; it is meant to be a representative case.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author then applies this principle to the moment of death. The soul, by taking refuge in God, gains strength that does not care for bodily destruction. See how this supports the soul's survival.
5
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Is this induction strong? Does it require a spiritual experience to accept, or can it be reasoned about? This will deepen your understanding.
This step connects to the lesson's objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : الاستقراء يدل على أن الإنسان ، كلما تجلى في قلبه نور معرفة الله تعالى ، وانكشفت له صفات جلالـه وكبريائه ، فإنه تقـوى نفسه وتكمل قوته ، ويصير بحيث لا يبالي بأعظم سلاطين العالم ، ولا يقع في عينه منصب أحد البتة وأما إذا زال ذلك الكشف والتجلي ، فإنه يعود إلى حالته الأولى بحيث يخاف من أقل الأشياء ، ويحذر أصغر الموجبات. يحكى أنه إبراهيم الخواص » كان في البادية ، ومعه بعض مريديه فاتفق في بعض الليالي ، أن وقع في مقام الكشف . فاضطجع هناك ، وأحاطت به السباع ، وأنه لم يلتفت إلى شيء . منها . وأما المريدون فخافوا من تلك السباع ، فصعدوا بعض الأشجار خوفاً منها ، وفي الليلة الثانية زالت تلك الحالة ، فاضطجع ، فوقعت بعوضة على يده ، فأظهر التوجع من وقع تلك البعوضة . فقال له بعض المريدين : لم تلتفت في الليلة الماضية إلى السباع التي أحاطت بك ، وفي هذه الليلة أظهرت التوجع من هذه البعوضة . فقال : أما الليلة الماضية فقد جاءني ضيف كريم ، وسلطان عظيم [ من عالم الغيب ] فلأجل قوته ما كنت أبالي بتلك السباع . وأما في هذه الليلة فأنا وحدي مع نفسي ، فلا أطيق تحمل إيذاء هذه البعوضة . فثبت بما ذكرنا : أن ظهور نور عالم الغيب يقوي جوهر الروح ، ويفيد النفس استيلاء على عالم الجسم. وإذا ثبت هذا ، فجوهر النفس عند القرب من الموت ، لا بد وأن يلتجيء إلى الله تعالى ، فتحصل المكاشفة ، فيقوى جوهر النفس قوة لا تبالي بخراب كل العالم، فكيف تبالي بخراب البدن الضعيف
The eighth proof: Induction indicates that man, whenever the light of knowledge of God, exalted, shines in his heart and the attributes of His majesty and grandeur are unveiled to him, his soul becomes strong and his power is perfected, and he becomes such that he does not care about the greatest kings of the world, and the position of anyone does not enter his eyes at all. But if that unveiling and manifestation depart, he returns to his original state, so that he fears the least things and is cautious of the smallest causes. It is narrated that Ibrahim al-Khawwas was in the desert, and with him some of his disciples. It happened one night that he attained the station of unveiling. He lay down there, and beasts of prey surrounded him, and he paid no attention to anything of them. As for the disciples, they feared those beasts, so they climbed some trees for fear of them. On the second night, that state departed, so he lay down, and a mosquito fell on his hand, and he showed pain from the bite of that mosquito. One of the disciples said to him: 'You paid no attention last night to the beasts that surrounded you, and tonight you show pain from this mosquito?' He said: 'As for last night, a noble guest and a great sultan from the unseen world came to me, and because of his strength I did not care about those beasts. As for tonight, I am alone with myself, so I cannot bear the harm of this mosquito.' So it is established by what we have mentioned that the appearance of the light of the unseen world strengthens the substance of the spirit and gives the soul mastery over the world of the body. And if this is established, then the substance of the soul at the approach of death must necessarily take refuge in God, and then unveiling occurs, and the substance of the soul becomes strong with a strength that does not care about the destruction of the entire world, so how would it care about the destruction of the weak body?
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof for the soul's immortality, based on induction from observable spiritual experiences. The author states that whenever the light of knowledge of God shines in a person's heart and the attributes of divine majesty are unveiled, his soul becomes strong and he becomes fearless of worldly powers. Conversely, when this unveiling departs, he reverts to a fearful state. The author illustrates this with the story of Ibrahim al-Khawwas: during a night of spiritual unveiling, he was fearless amidst beasts of prey; on the next night without that state, he was pained by a mosquito bite. When asked, he explained that the first night he had the company of a noble guest from the unseen, giving him strength. The author concludes that the appearance of that light strengthens the spirit and gives it mastery over the body. By analogy, at death the soul takes refuge in God and gains such strength that it does not care about the destruction of the body, proving its survival beyond bodily death.

Ninth Proof: The Dependency Argument
The author argues that if the soul depended more on the body, it would be baser, but it is nobler, so it does not perish.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dependency argument that establishes the soul's immortality through a comparison of needs between soul and body.

Core Idea
Greater need means baseness; the soul's greater need would make it baser, but it is nobler, so it survives.

This proof uses a clever logical twist: it compares how the soul and body need each other. If the soul needed the body for its own existence, it would be less noble than the body. But we know the body is only noble when animated by the soul. Therefore, the soul cannot perish with the body. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then'.
2
Identify the assumption
The argument begins by supposing the soul perishes with the body. Recognize this as a hypothetical premise.
3
Compare the needs
The author contrasts two types of need: the soul's need for the body for its essence, versus the body's need for the soul only for its attributes. Determine which need is greater.
The soul's need is for survival of its essence; the body's need is only for its qualities.
4
Apply the principle
The principle: more needy is more base. If the soul's need is greater, it would be baser than the body.
5
Recognize the contradiction
The body without the soul is utterly base, but with the soul it is noble. Therefore the soul cannot be baser. So the initial assumption is false.
Conclude that the soul does not die with the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : لو فنيت النفس بفناء الجسد ، لكانت النفس محتاجة إلى البدن ، في بقاء ذاتها ، وأما الجسد فهو محتاج إلى النفس لا في بقاء ذاته ، بل في بقاء صفاته. وهي : الكيفيات المزاجية وعلى هذا التقدير فيكون احتياج النفس إلى البدن ، أكثر من احتياج البدن إلى النفس . وما كان أكثر حاجة ، كان أخر مما هو أقل حاجة . فلو ماتت النفس بموت البدن ، لزم كون النفس أخس من البدن . وهذا باطل . لأن الجسد مع النفس يكون شريفاً ، وبدون النفس يكون في غاية الخساسة . فثبت : أن النفس لا تموت بموت البدن
The ninth proof: If the soul perished with the perishing of the body, then the soul would be in need of the body for the survival of its essence. As for the body, it needs the soul not for the survival of its essence, but for the survival of its attributes, which are the temperamental qualities. On this estimation, the soul's need for the body would be greater than the body's need for the soul. And what is more needy is more base than what is less needy. So if the soul died with the death of the body, it would necessitate that the soul is baser than the body. But this is false, because the body with the soul is noble, and without the soul it is in the utmost baseness. So it is established that the soul does not die with the death of the body.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof, which is a logical argument based on dependency. The author supposes for argument that the soul perishes with the body. If that were the case, the soul would need the body for its own survival, while the body needs the soul only for its attributes (temperamental qualities). Therefore the soul's need for the body would be greater than the body's need for the soul. Since greater need indicates baseness, the soul would be baser than the body. But this contradicts the obvious fact that the body is noble only when joined with the soul, and without the soul it is utterly base. Hence the assumption is false, and the soul does not die with the body.

Misconception: Some may think the argument is about need in general, without distinguishing the type of need (essence vs. attributes).
Correction: The argument specifically contrasts the soul's need for its own essence versus the body's need only for its attributes (temperamental qualities). The soul's need is greater because it is for its very survival, not merely for qualities.

Key Takeaway
The soul does not perish with the body because its need for the body would make it baser, contradicting its nobleness.

Tenth Proof: Death as Change of Qualities
The author defines death as change of qualities and argues the soul's relation is weaker than the body's, so soul survives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the soul survives bodily death because the accidents of life are more strongly tied to the body than to the soul.

Core Idea
What is more attached to the body can be removed without destroying the soul.

In this passage, the author presents a philosophical proof for the immortality of the soul by analyzing the nature of death. He defines death as the change of temperamental qualities and balanced accidents, and then compares the attachment of these accidents to the body versus their attachment to the soul. The argument uses a logical priority: if removing these accidents does not annihilate the body (which they are closely attached to), then it certainly does not annihilate the soul, which is only distantly attached. This lesson will guide you through this precise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very compressed style, typical of kalam.
2
Identify the definition of death
Find how the author defines death. Is it a substance or a change of qualities? The passage says death is an expression of change of qualities subsisting in the body.
The key terms are 'تغير الصفات' (change of qualities) and 'زوال الكيفية المزاجية' (removal of temperamental quality).
3
Understand the comparison of attachments
The author compares the attachment of these accidents to the body versus to the soul. Which attachment is stronger? The passage says the accidents are more completely and strongly attached to the body than to the soul.
The soul is described as 'النفس المتصرفة' (the governing soul), indicating a functional rather than a material connection.
4
Follow the logical inference
The argument uses a 'since ... then it is even more appropriate' structure. Since removal of accidents does not annihilate the body (despite close attachment), it cannot annihilate the soul (distant attachment).
This is a qiyas al-awlawi (argument of priority).
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider what this argument implies about the nature of the soul. It suggests the soul is a substance that survives the dissolution of its bodily qualities.
This is a classic proof for the soul's immortality in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن موت البدن عبارة عن تغير الصفات القائمة بتلك الأجسام ، فإن موت البدن لا معنى له إلا زوال تلك الكيفية المزاجية ، وزوال تلك الأعراض المعتدلة . فنقول : تعلق تلك الأعراض بتلك الأجسام ، أكمل وأقوى من تعلقها بالنفس المتصرفة في تلك الأجسام . ولما لم يكن زوال تلك الأعراض فناء تلك الأجسام مع ما بينها من التعلق القريب فلان لا يكون موجبا فناء النفس مع ما بينهما من التعلق البعيد ، كان ذلك أولى
The tenth proof: The death of the body is an expression of the change of the qualities subsisting in those bodies. For the death of the body has no meaning except the removal of that temperamental quality and the removal of those balanced accidents. So we say: The attachment of those accidents to those bodies is more complete and stronger than their attachment to the soul that governs in those bodies. And since the removal of those accidents was not the annihilation of those bodies, despite the close attachment between them, then it is even more appropriate that it does not necessitate the annihilation of the soul, given the distant attachment between them.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, which analyzes the nature of death. The author defines death as the change or removal of qualities (temperamental qualities and balanced accidents) that subsist in the body. He then compares the attachment of these accidents to the body versus their attachment to the soul. The accidents are more strongly attached to the body than to the soul, because the soul governs the body but is not identical with these accidents. Since the removal of these accidents does not cause the annihilation of the body (the body remains as a corpse), it follows that the removal of these accidents should not cause the annihilation of the soul, which is even less attached to them. Therefore, the soul survives the death of the body.

الكيفية المزاجية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Because the body decays at death, the soul must also decay or die.
Correction: This argument shows that the qualities that cause life (temperament, balanced accidents) are more attached to the body than to the soul. Their removal affects the body but not the soul, which is only distantly attached to them.

Key Takeaway
The soul survives death because the qualities that define life are more attached to the body than to the soul, so their removal does not destroy the soul.

The Soul's Incorruptibility: A Regress Argument
The author proves the soul's incorruptibility via a regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that proves the soul is incorruptible, specifically how composition leads to an infinite regress or circularity.

Core Idea
The soul's simplicity saves it from the infinite regress of matter.

This passage presents a rigorous philosophical proof demonstrating that the human soul cannot undergo corruption (fasad). The argument hinges on the nature of corruption itself: it requires something that undergoes change, and that something must have parts. By analyzing what it means to be corruptible, the author shows that if the soul were corruptible, it would entail an infinite regress or circularity—both impossible. Therefore, the soul must be a simple, incorruptible substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the compressed style; each sentence carries a logical step.
2
Understand the third premise
The author states that 'acceptance of corruption' is not a self-standing concept; it requires a subject to be described by it. This sets up the need for a bearer of corruption.
This is foundational for the disjunction that follows.
3
Trace the disjunctive argument
The subject of corruption must be either the thing itself (the quiddity) or a part of it. The first option is impossible because a thing cannot exist at the moment of its own corruption.
Identify why the first option is false; the existence and corruption cannot coincide.
4
Follow the regress argument
If the soul were corruptible, it would have a part (matter) that is the locus for corruption. If matter itself is corruptible, it needs more matter, leading to either circularity or infinite regress, both impossible. Therefore, the ultimate matter must be simple and incorruptible—that is the soul.
The conclusion follows from the impossibility of regress; the soul must be a simple substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقدمة الثالثة : إن قبول الفساد مفهوم غير مستقل بنفسه . بل لا بد من فرض شيء آخر . يكون ذلك الشيء موصوفاً بذلك القبول . وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمات . نقول : الموصوف بقبول الفساد إما أن يكون هو عين ذلك الشيء أو جزء من أجزائه. والأول باطل ، لأن قابل الفساد لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا حال حصول الفساد ، ووجود الشيء يمتنع أن يكون حاصلا حال حصول فساده. وإذا بطل هذا القسم ، تعين القسم الثاني ، وهو أن تكون تلك الماهية لها جزء ، ويكون ذلك الجزء موصوفا بقبول الفساد. وإمكان الفساد هو بعينه إمكان الحصول ، فكل ما يصح عليه الفساد ، وله جزء ، يكون ذلك الجزء محلاً لإمكان وجوده ، ولإمكان عدمه . وذلك الجزء هو المادة . كالطين الذي هو المادة للكوز ، بسبب أن الطين محل لإمكان حدوث صورة الكوز ، ولإمكان عدمها . فثبت : أن النفس لو كانت قابلة للفساد ، لكانت مركبة من المادة والصورة . وبيان أن كونها مركبة من المادة والصورة محال : هو أن تلك المادة ، إن كانت قابلة للفساد ، افتقرت إلى مادة أخرى ، ولزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل . وهما محالان. وإما الانتهاء إلى مادة لا مادة لها ، وحينئذ لا يكون قابلا للفساد . لما بينا : أن كل ما يقبل الفساد ، فله مادة . فالذي لا مادة له ، وجب أن لا يقبل الفساد . وعلى هذا التقدير فتلك المادة جوهر مجرد قائم بذاته . ولا معنى للنفس إلا ذلك . فلما كانت تلك المادة غير قابلة للفساد ، ثبت : أن النفس لا تقبل الفساد .
The third premise: that acceptance of corruption is a concept not independent in itself. Rather, it is necessary to suppose something else, that thing being described by that acceptance. When these premises are established, we say: the described [entity] by acceptance of corruption is either that same thing or a part of its parts. The first is false, because the receiver of corruption must exist at the time of corruption's occurrence, and the existence of a thing is impossible to exist at the time of its corruption. When this division is false, the second division is determined, which is that that quiddity has a part, and that part is described by acceptance of corruption. The possibility of corruption is exactly the possibility of obtaining, so everything that is subject to corruption and has a part, that part is a locus for the possibility of its existence and the possibility of its non-existence. That part is matter. Like clay which is the matter of the jug, because clay is a locus for the possibility of the form of the jug occurring and the possibility of its non-occurrence. So it is established: that if the soul were subject to corruption, it would be composed of matter and form. The demonstration that its being composed of matter and form is impossible: is that that matter, if it were subject to corruption, would need another matter, and either circularity or infinite regress would follow, and both are impossible. Or it ends at a matter which has no matter, and then it would not be subject to corruption. Because we have shown: that everything that accepts corruption has matter. That which has no matter must not accept corruption. On this estimation, that matter is a simple substance subsisting by itself. And there is no meaning of soul other than that. Since that matter is not subject to corruption, it is established: that the soul does not accept corruption.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a proof demonstrating that the soul is not subject to corruption (al-fasad). The author begins by stating the third premise: the concept of 'accepting corruption' (qubul al-fasad) is not self-sufficient; it requires another thing to be described by it. After establishing this, the author argues that the entity described as accepting corruption must be either the thing itself or a part of it. The first option is false because the receiver of corruption must exist at the moment of corruption, but a thing's existence cannot persist at its own corruption. Thus, the entity must have a part described by accepting corruption. The author then equates the possibility of corruption with the possibility of existence: anything subject to corruption has a part that serves as a locus for both the possibility of its existence and non-existence. That part is matter (al-mada), using the example of clay as the matter of a jug. Consequently, if the soul were corruptible, it would be composed of matter and form. The author then proves that composition from matter and form is impossible for the soul: if matter itself were corruptible, it would require another matter, leading to either circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), both of which are impossible. If it ends at a matter with no further matter, that matter would not be corruptible (by the rule that only things with matter are corruptible). Such a matter is a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad) subsisting by itself, and the soul is nothing but that. Therefore, since that ultimate matter is not corruptible, the soul is not corruptible.

جوهر مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if the soul is incorruptible, it must be eternal in the same way as matter, or that its incorruptibility is by divine decree rather than by its own nature.
Correction: The argument proves that the soul's incorruptibility follows from its metaphysical simplicity—it has no parts, so it cannot undergo the kind of change that corruption requires. It is not about pre-eternity but about the impossibility of decomposition.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why can the soul not be composed of matter and form?
A) Because matter is eternal and cannot be destroyed
B) Because if matter were corruptible, it would require another matter, leading to an infinite regress or circularity
C) Because the soul is simple and cannot accept any composition
D) Because matter and form cannot coexist in the soul

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be corrupted because if it could, it would require composition, leading to an infinite regress; therefore, the soul is a simple, incorruptible substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is truly incorruptible and will persist beyond every physical death you can imagine, what are you building your life on that will not dissolve with your body?
?
The argument shows that the soul cannot be destroyed because it is simple and has no matter. How does this truth expose the emptiness of fearing the decay of your body more than the condition of your soul?
?
Since the soul is a simple substance that cannot be corrupted, its destiny is irreversible. What specific attachment or sin are you still holding onto that, if left unrepented, would corrupt your soul eternally? Will you turn from it today?

Four Objections to the Soul's Incorruptibility
Four objections and the start of the first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main objections raised against the proof that the soul is not corruptible, and see how the author begins to answer the first objection.

Core Idea
The soul's incorruptibility is challenged by four counterexamples and logical arguments.

Having presented the proof that the soul is not corruptible because corruption requires composition and the soul is simple, the author now anticipates objections. Four strong challenges are raised, the first of which provides three counterexamples to the main premise. This lesson presents these objections and the opening of the author's first response.

Guided Path
1
Read the four objections carefully
Notice the structure: each objection attacks a different aspect of the previous proof. The first objection offers three counterexamples.
2
Identify the three counterexamples in the first objection
List them: accidents, form itself, and immaterial contingent beings (intellects, souls, prime matter).
3
Understand the logic of the second, third, and fourth objections
Each objection tries to show that the proof's premises lead to absurdities or inconsistencies.
4
Examine the beginning of the answer to the first objection
The author starts his response but it is cut off; anticipate that the full answer continues in the next lesson.
This is just the beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الكلام عليه من وجوه : السؤال الأول : إن قولكم لو كانت النفس قابلة للفساد ، لكانت مركبة من المادة والصورة منقوص بصور : إحداها : الأعراض . مثل : السواد والبياض . فإنها قابلة للعدم وليست مركبة من المادة والصورة . وثانيها : الصورة قابلة للعدم. وليست مركبة من المادة والصورة وإلا لزم أن تحصل للصورة صورة أخرى إلى غير نهاية . وثالثها : إن العقول والنفوس الفلكية ، والنفوس الناطقة ، والهيولى : كلها ممكنات ، وليس لشيء منها مادة . السؤال الثاني : لا نسلم أن قبول الفساد ، لا بد له من محل ، فإن هذا بناء على أن الإمكان والقبول صفة موجودة ، ولا بد لها من محل . وقد سبق بيان الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون صفة موجودة . السؤال الثالث : النفوس عندكم حادثة . وكما أن كل ما يقبل الفساد ، فإن إمكان فساده حاصل قبل وجوده ، فكذلك ما يكون حادثاً ، يكون إمكان حدوثه حاصلاً قبل وجوده ، فإن لزم من أحد الإمكانين كونه مركباً من المادة والصورة ، لزم من الإمكان الثاني ، كونه كذلك ، فكان يجب أن يلزم من كون النفس محدثة ، كونها مركبة من الهيولى والصورة . وحينئذ يبطل دليلكم . السؤال الرابع : إن بتقدير أن تكون النفس مركبة من المادة والصورة ، فإنه يجب بقاء المادة، لكن لا يجب بقاء الصورة، وبتقدير أن تكون تلك الصورة شرطاً لقيام الحياة والعلم والقدرة بتلك المادة وحينئذ لا يحصل للنفس سعادة ولا شقاوة ، وحينئذ يبطل ما هو الفرض الأصلي من بقاء النفس . والجواب : أما السؤال الأول : وهو النقيض بالصور المذكورة . فجوابه : أن نقول :
If it is said: discussion upon it from aspects: The first question: that your saying 'if the soul were subject to corruption, it would be composed of matter and form' is refuted by forms: One of them: accidents, such as blackness and whiteness. For they are subject to non-existence and are not composed of matter and form. And the second: form is subject to non-existence and is not composed of matter and form, otherwise it would necessitate that form would have another form ad infinitum. And the third: the intellects and the celestial souls and the rational souls and the hyle: all are contingent, and none of them has matter. The second question: we do not grant that acceptance of corruption necessarily has a locus, for this is based on the assumption that possibility and acceptance are an existent attribute and necessarily have a locus. And it has preceded the demonstration that possibility cannot be an existent attribute. The third question: souls according to you are originated (haditha). And just as everything that accepts corruption, the possibility of its corruption is present before its existence, so too whatever is originated, the possibility of its origination is present before its existence. If from one of the two possibilities it follows that it is composed of matter and form, it would follow from the second possibility that it is likewise. Then it would necessarily follow from the fact that the soul is originated that it is composed of hyle and form. And then your proof is invalidated. The fourth question: assuming that the soul is composed of matter and form, then the survival of matter is necessary, but the survival of form is not necessary. And assuming that that form is a condition for the subsistence of life, knowledge, and power in that matter, then the soul would have neither happiness nor misery. And then what is the original aim – the survival of the soul – is invalidated. The answer: As for the first question, which is the counterexample by the mentioned forms, its answer is that we say:
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents four objections the author must answer to defend the soul's incorruptibility. The first objection attacks the proof's claim that corruption requires composition by providing three counterexamples: (1) accidents like blackness and whiteness are subject to non-existence but are not composed of matter and form; (2) form itself is subject to non-existence but is not composed of matter and form (otherwise an infinite regress of forms would occur); (3) intellects, celestial souls, rational souls, and prime matter (hayula) are contingent but have no matter. The second objection denies that acceptance of corruption requires a locus, arguing that the earlier proof assumed possibility (imkan) is an existent attribute needing a locus, but it has been shown that possibility cannot be an existent attribute. The third objection points to the soul's origination (huduth): since souls are originated, their possibility of origination exists before they exist, just as corruption's possibility exists before corruption. If one possibility implies composition, then the other should too, meaning origination would also imply composition – which would invalidate the author's proof for the soul's simplicity. The fourth objection argues that even if the soul were composed of matter and form, only matter would persist, not form. If form is necessary for life, knowledge, and power, then after corruption the soul would have no happiness or misery, defeating the purpose of proving the soul's survival. The author then begins answering the first objection, but the answer is cut off at the page end; it will continue in the next window.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All things without matter are necessarily simple and cannot cease to exist.
Correction: The first objection shows that accidents (like blackness) are subject to non-existence without being composed of matter and form, so being without matter does not guarantee incorruptibility.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The soul's incorruptibility is challenged by four objections, and the first objection provides three counterexamples that the author begins to address.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that your soul is fragile because your thoughts and feelings change? This lesson lists objections that challenge the idea that change or perishing requires material composition. Are you willing to reconsider the foundation of your own inner stability?
?
The passage shows that some things can cease without being made of parts. What does that mean for your own fear of death? Could your soul's simplicity be a source of hope?
?
The objections force you to examine whether you have assumed the soul must be composite because it experiences change. If the soul is simple, how does that reshape your understanding of your own identity and accountability before Allah?

The Problem of Soul Transfer Between Bodies
Al-Razi critiques the view of the soul as a separate substance using an analogy to show it would allow transfer between bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that the view of the soul as a separate substance leads to the problematic implication that it could transfer between bodies, a consequence that contradicts experience.

Core Idea
If the soul is a separate substance, why can't it switch bodies like tools?

Al-Razi presents a critical argument against the idea that the soul is a non-corporeal substance that merely manages the body. Using a simple analogy, he shows that this view implies the soul could move between bodies, a consequence that contradicts our everyday experience. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Note the key terms and the logical flow.
Focus on the analogy of the instrument.
2
Identify the premise being critiqued
Al-Razi directs his argument against those who say the soul is a separate substance with only a managerial relation to the body. Understand this premise.
The soul is like a king ruling a city.
3
Understand the analogy
Al-Razi compares the soul's attachment to the body to a person using a tool. If the tool becomes defective, they switch to a better one. Reflect on why this analogy is chosen.
The soul should be willing to switch bodies if it finds a more suitable one.
4
Consider the implication and its problem
The analogy implies that the soul could transfer from one body to another. Since we do not observe such transfer, the premise that the soul is a separate substance becomes problematic.
5
Reflect on the dialectical nature
This argument is not a definitive proof against the soul's separateness, but a demonstration of a problematic consequence. Take note of the next lesson for an alternative view.
This is a critical step in a larger discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يحصل نفسان في بدن واحد . وتحصل نفس في أبدان كثيرة . فقد ذكرنا كيفية تقريره في الفصول السالفة . واعلم : أن هذه المباحث إنما تتوجه على من يقول : النفس جوهر مجرد قائم بذاته لا تعلق لها بذلك البدن ، إلا على سبيل التصرف والتدبير . ومن المباحث اللائقة بهذا الباب : أن يقال : إذا قلنا : إن النفس جوهر ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني ، ولا تعلق لها بهذا البدن ، إلا تعلق التصرف والتدبير . مثل تصرف الملك في البلدة، والمالك في البيت . وثبت أيضاً : أنه لا داعي للنفس على التصرف في هذا البدن ، إلا لأجل أن تستعمل هذه الأعضاء وهذه الآلات ، في تحصيل اللذات الجسمانية ، والراحات البدنية ، والشهوات النفسانية . فحينئذ جرى تعلق هذه النفس بهذا البدن ، مجرى من أراد أن يعمل عملاً ، ولا يمكنه الإتيان بذلك العمل إلا بواسطة آلة مخصوصة . فهو يجب تلك الآلة ، ليتوصل بها إلى تحصيل ذلك العمل ، إلا أنه لا تكون محبته مقصورة على تلك الآلة بعينها ، بل إذا وجدت هذه الآلة صحيحة صالحة لذلك المقصود ، استعملها الثانية . فإذا انقضى زمان ، واتفق أن صارت الآلة الثانية مختلة ، والآلة الأولى صالحة ، ترك الثانية ورجع إلى استعمال الأولى . وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو كانت النفس جوهراً مجرداً مبنايناً عن البدن ، لكان يجب أن يصح عليها أن تنتقل من هذا البدن إلى بدن آخر ، ثم بعد حين تعود إلى هذا البدن ، كما بيناه في استعمال الآلات . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، صار قولنا : النفس جوهر مجرد ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني مشكلاً
And as for the third division: it is that two souls occur in one body, and one soul occurs in many bodies. We have mentioned how to explain it in the previous chapters. And know: that these investigations are only directed to one who says: the soul is a separate substance subsisting by itself, having no attachment to the body except in the way of management and governance. Among the investigations fitting for this topic is that it is said: if we say that the soul is a substance that is not a body nor corporeal, and it has no attachment to this body except the attachment of management and governance, like the management of the king in the city and the owner in the house; and it is also established that there is no motivation for the soul to manage this body except to use these limbs and these instruments to obtain physical pleasures, bodily comforts, and psychic desires; then the attachment of this soul to this body runs on the analogy of one who wants to do a work and cannot do that work except by means of a specific instrument. He loves that instrument in order to achieve the work, but his love is not confined to that particular instrument; rather, if he finds this instrument sound and suitable for that purpose, he uses it. Then if a time passes and it happens that the second instrument becomes defective and the first instrument becomes sound, he leaves the second and returns to using the first. And if you understand this, then we say: if the soul were a separate substance separate from the body, it would have to be possible for it to transfer from this body to another body, then after a while return to this body, as we explained in the use of instruments. And since this is not the case, our statement that the soul is a separate substance not a body nor corporeal becomes problematic.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the view that the soul is a separate, non-corporeal substance that merely manages the body. The author begins by mentioning the third division of the relationship—two souls in one body or one soul in multiple bodies—but only refers to earlier discussion. He then clarifies that the following investigation is aimed at those who hold the soul is a separate self-subsisting essence with only a managerial connection to the body, like a king ruling a city. The author then presents an analogy: a person uses a tool to achieve a goal and does not love that tool for itself; if a better tool comes, he switches. Similarly, if the soul is separate and only uses the body as a tool for pleasure, it should be able to abandon a defective body for a better one and return if the original becomes suitable again. Since we do not observe such transfer, the claim that the soul is a separate substance is problematic. The argument follows a logical structure: premise (soul is separate and uses body as tool) leads to implications (transfer between bodies), which contradicts observable reality, thus the premise is questionable.

Key Terms
جوهر مجرد
تعلق التصرف والتدبير
آلة
مشكلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a definitive proof that the soul is not a separate substance.
Correction: Al-Razi does not claim to disprove the separateness of the soul; he only shows that the simple view leads to an odd consequence. The argument is dialectical, and he offers an alternative in the next lesson.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to illustrate the expected behavior of a separate soul?
A) A king governing a city
B) A person using different tools to achieve a goal
C) A plant growing in soil
D) A teacher in a classroom

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the theory of a separate soul implies the possibility of transfer between bodies, which is not observed, thus the theory is problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your body as a temporary tool for pleasure, or as an integral part of your spiritual identity? How would your actions today change if you believed your soul could leave for another body tomorrow?
?
Al-Razi's argument challenges you to examine: Is your attachment to your loved ones based on their bodies or their souls? If their soul transferred to another body, would you still love them the same way?
?
If your only reason to care for your body is to enjoy worldly desires, what happens when age or illness makes it unable to provide those pleasures? Is your faith in Allah tied solely to what your body can give you in this world?

Soul-Body Specificity and Human Intellectual Limits
Al-Razi states the rational permissibility of soul-body specificity and the limits of human intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that each soul is uniquely fitted to its body and that human reason cannot attain complete knowledge of God's mysteries.

Core Idea
Each soul is uniquely paired with its body; full comprehension of divine secrets is beyond us.

Al-Razi concludes his discussion by addressing two key points: first, the rational possibility that each soul and body have unique properties making them exclusively suited to each other; second, the inherent limit of human intellect in fully grasping the secrets of God's creation. This passage serves as a humble reminder of the boundaries of reason and the role of divine mystery.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the full meaning.
Notice the phrase 'والله أعلم' at the end, signaling submission to divine knowledge.
2
Understand the two claims
Identify the two distinct claims: (1) each soul and body have unique properties making mutual suitability exclusive; (2) human minds cannot fully attain the secrets of God's creations.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why Al-Razi adds the second claim after the first. It shows that while rational possibility exists, ultimate knowledge belongs only to God.
This resolves earlier objections without overstepping human reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنه لا يمتنع في العقل أن يكون لكل نفس خاصية ، ولكل بدن خاصية . لا تصلح تلك النفس إلا لذلك البدن ، ولا يصلح ذلك البدن إلا لتلك النفس . ووصول العقول البشرية إلى أسرار مخلوقات الله سبحانه وتعالى على سبيل التمام والكمال : محال . والله أعلم
And know: that it is not impossible in reason that every soul has a property, and every body has a property. That soul is only suitable for that body, and that body is only suitable for that soul. And the arrival of human intellects at the secrets of God's creations—exalted is He—in a complete and perfect manner is impossible. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage serves as a qualification and conclusion to the previous argument. The author begins by stating that it is not rationally impossible to posit that each soul has a unique property and each body has a unique property, such that a particular soul is only suitable for a particular body, and vice versa. This directly addresses the earlier objection: the soul does not transfer between bodies because it is specially fitted to one body. Then he adds a broader epistemological point: human intellects can never attain complete and perfect knowledge of the secrets of God's creations. The passage ends with 'And God knows best', acknowledging the limits of human understanding. This is not a proof but an acknowledgment of divine mystery and the inadequacy of reason to fully comprehend such matters.

Key Terms

أسرار مخلوقات الله
والله أعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if souls can be specific to bodies, then the soul must be material or dependent on the body.
Correction: Al-Razi only asserts rational possibility of mutual suitability, not that the soul is material or dependent. The specificity is a property, not a bodily essence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi teaches that while reason can accept the specific pairing of soul and body, it cannot fully penetrate the mysteries of God's creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Al-Razi ends with 'And God knows best.' How often do you humbly acknowledge your intellectual limits in matters of faith? In what area of your religious understanding do you need to surrender more deeply to Allah's superior knowledge?

The Tongue Dilemma: Refuting Speciality
Argument that the tongue's perception disproves the speciality of other organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the tongue's perception is used as a logical dilemma to invalidate the claim that only certain organs have special perception.

Core Idea
The tongue argument destroys the claim of perceptual speciality by a simple dilemma.

This passage presents a compact logical argument. It examines the tongue's ability to taste. The author uses this as a test case: if the tongue can taste, then the supposed speciality of other organs is not unique; if it cannot, then the tongue is like other organs. Either way, the speciality claim collapses. Let's unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma setup
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice it offers two possibilities about the tongue: it either perceives taste or it does not.
The dilemma is exhaustive.
2
Analyze each branch
Follow each branch logically. If the tongue perceives taste, that confirms perception exists in the tongue, undermining speciality. If it does not, then the tongue is no different from other organs, so speciality is false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Draw the conclusion
The conclusion is that the claim of speciality is invalid. The argument is complete and valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللسان ، فهذا اللسان هل يدرك الطعم أم لا ؟ فإن أدرك فقد حصل المطلوب ، وإن لم يدرك فحينئذ نسبة اللسان إلى إدراك المذوقات ، كنسبة سائر الأعضاء إليه . وذلك يبطل هذا الاختصاص
The tongue: does this tongue perceive taste or not? If it perceives, then the desired result is obtained; and if it does not perceive, then the tongue's relation to perceiving tastes is like the relation of the other organs to it. And that invalidates this speciality.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a brief argument against the claim that certain organs have a special, unique perception. The author poses a dilemma about the tongue: either it perceives taste or it does not. If it does, then the claim that other organs lack such perception is refuted (since the tongue also perceives). If it does not, then the tongue is no different from other organs, and the supposed speciality (that only certain organs perceive) is invalid. Thus, the argument uses the tongue as a counterexample to show that the claimed speciality is unsustainable.

Key Terms
اللسان
إدراك
اختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The tongue argument is incomplete or irrelevant.
Correction: It is actually a complete logical dilemma that disproves the speciality claim.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw if the tongue does not perceive taste?
A) The tongue is unique among organs
B) The tongue's relation to taste is like other organs, invalidating speciality
C) The tongue must perceive taste
D) The argument fails

Key Takeaway
The tongue argument shows that the claim of speciality for perception is logically unsustainable because either the tongue perceives (contradiction) or it does not (no difference).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your understanding of a matter is uniquely correct while dismissing others' views? How does the tongue dilemma force you to examine whether your claim holds up under logical scrutiny?
?
The tongue argument proves that speciality is logically invalid. Is there a belief or practice you hold that you have never tested with a similar dilemma? Are you willing to apply the same logical test to your own assumptions today?
?
If your claim of special insight were put to the same test as the tongue – either you perceive it or you don't – would your faith survive the dilemma? What would it mean for your humility if it did not?

Medical Evidence for Corporeal Faculties
Medical experiments show localized brain lesions impair specific internal perceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how medical experiments demonstrate that internal perceptions depend on specific brain regions, supporting the claim that these faculties are corporeal.

Core Idea
Physical damage to specific brain areas disrupts specific mental functions, proving they are bodily.

The author presents a second argument, this time based on empirical medical observations. He notes that damage to specific organs impairs their functions, as is obvious for the five senses. Then he applies this to internal perceptions: lesions in different brain ventricles corrupt imagination, thinking, and memory respectively. This direct correlation between physical damage and mental impairment shows that these faculties are corporeal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three brain ventricles mentioned.
2
Understand the argument structure
The argument has a premise (medical experiments show lesion → impairment), an application to external senses, then a detailed application to internal perceptions for three brain parts, and a conclusion.
The conclusion states that if these powers were not corporeal, this correlation would not hold.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does the fact that a physical lesion impairs a mental function prove that the function itself is corporeal?
The key assumption is that a purely spiritual faculty would not be affected by physical damage to a specific organ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن التجارب الطبية دلت على أن الآفة ، إذا حلت في عضو من هذه الأعضاء ، اختلت تلك الأفعال، وهذا ظاهر في الحواس الخمسة الظاهرة . وأما في الإدراكات الباطنة ، فالأفة إذا وقعت في البطن المقدم من الدماغ ، فسد التخيل . وإن وقعت في البطن الأوسط فسد التفكر . وإن وقعت في البطن الأخير، فسد التذكر . ولولا أن هذه القوى جسمانية ، وإلا لما كان الأمر كذلك
The second: Medical experiments indicate that if a lesion occurs in an organ among these organs, those actions become impaired. This is apparent in the five external senses. As for internal perceptions, if a lesion occurs in the front ventricle of the brain, imagination (takhayyul) is corrupted; if it occurs in the middle ventricle, thinking (tafakkur) is corrupted; if it occurs in the rear ventricle, memory (tadhakkur) is corrupted. Were it not that these powers are corporeal, the matter would not be so.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument that perceptions are corporeal, based on empirical medical observations. The author notes that damage to specific organs impairs their associated functions, as is obvious for the external senses. For internal perceptions, he specifies that lesions in the front, middle, and rear ventricles of the brain respectively corrupt imagination, thinking, and memory. The conclusion is that these faculties must be corporeal because their impairment depends on physical damage to specific bodily parts. If they were purely spiritual, such localized damage would not affect them.

Key Terms
التجارب الطبية
إدراكات باطنة
فساد
قوى جسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument depends on the anatomical accuracy of ancient medicine regarding brain ventricles.
Correction: The core argument is about the dependency of mental functions on physical organs, regardless of specific anatomical details. The principle that physical damage to a specific brain region impairs a specific mental function remains valid even if the exact mapping is refined.

Checkpoint

C) Memory is corrupted
D) All external senses are lost

Key Takeaway
The dependency of mental functions on specific brain regions provides empirical evidence that these faculties are corporeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If a lesion could one day rob you of the ability to remember Allah or to think clearly about His signs, what are you doing now to ensure your connection with Him transcends the health of your brain? Are you building a faith that survives even the corruption of your mental faculties?

Argument from Animal Souls
A short argument against immaterial animal souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing immaterial souls to animals based on their perceptions is considered implausible in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Animals' partial perceptions do not prove immaterial souls.

In this passage, the author presents a concise argument against the notion that perceptions must be attributed to immaterial souls. He notes that animals also have perceptions, and if those required immaterial souls, then animals would have them—a conclusion he deems far-fetched. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument's flow. The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the conditional structure
Notice the 'if...then' structure: if perceptions describe an immaterial soul, then animals would have immaterial souls. The conclusion 'that is far-fetched' implies the antecedent is false.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
Why does the author consider the conclusion implausible? Consider how this protects the uniqueness of human souls and supports the corporeal nature of animal perceptions.

The argument implies that since it is more reasonable to deny that animals have immaterial souls, perceptions must be corporeal faculties tied to the body.

The Fourth Argument: Imagining a Sphere
Imagining a sphere requires a corporeal substrate because an image cannot exist in a non-spatial entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that imagining a sphere proves the corporeality of internal perception because an image with spatial properties cannot exist in a non-spatial entity.

Core Idea
Imagining a sphere requires a spatial substrate because images have position and magnitude.

This lesson presents the fourth argument for the corporeality of internal perception. The author argues that when we imagine a sphere, its image must be impressed in the perceiver. However, an image with spatial properties cannot exist in an immaterial substance that lacks position and magnitude. Therefore, the imagination itself must be corporeal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise logical structure: premise, principle, impossibility, conclusion.
The Arabic uses 'الرابع' to indicate this is the fourth argument.
2
Identify the two premises
The first premise is that imagining a sphere requires an image of the sphere to be present in the perceiver. The second is the general principle that perception consists of an image equal to the perceived object.
The principle 'الإدراك صورة مساوية للمدرك' is key.
3
Understand the impossibility and conclusion
The image of a sphere has position and magnitude, so it cannot be impressed in something without these. Therefore, the imagining faculty must have position and magnitude, i.e., be corporeal.
This argument shows that the internal sense (imagination) is not purely spiritual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنا إذا تخيلنا ( الكرة ) وجب أن يرتسم في هذا المدرك ، صورة الكرة بناء على أن الإدراك صورة مساوية للمدرك ، تحصل في المدرك . لكن من المحال أن ترتسم صورة الكرة ، فيما لا وضع له ولا مقدار
The fourth: When we imagine the sphere, it is necessary that the image of the sphere is impressed in this perceiver, based on the principle that perception is an image equal to the perceived object, obtained in the perceiver. But it is impossible for the image of a sphere to be impressed in something that has no position (wad') and no magnitude (miqdar).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument that internal perception (imagination) is corporeal. The author reasons that when we imagine a sphere, an image of the sphere must be present in the faculty that perceives it (the imagination). This is based on the general principle that perception consists of an image that corresponds to the object and is received in the perceiver. However, a sphere has spatial properties (position and magnitude), so its image cannot exist in a non-spatial, immaterial substance (which has no position or magnitude). Therefore, the imagining faculty must be corporeal.

Key Terms
تخيل
صورة
وضع
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about confusing physical images with mental images.
Correction: The argument is about the necessary spatial quality of the image itself, not about physical vs. mental. The image of a sphere, even as a mental representation, must have position and magnitude because it represents a spatial object.

Checkpoint

B) An immaterial substance has no position or magnitude
C) The image would disappear
D) Immaterial substances only perceive non-spatial things

Key Takeaway
The image of a sphere requires a substrate with position and magnitude, so imagination must be corporeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Corporeality of External Perception
Proof that external perceptions are corporeal, with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that external perceptions must be corporeal because the immaterial soul cannot be subject to spatial distance, and the refutation of the objection that instruments mediate perception.

Core Idea
If the soul were the direct perceiver, distance would not matter — but it does.

This passage presents a specific rational argument for the corporeality of external perception. The proponents argue that if the soul itself perceived external objects, its immaterial nature would make distance irrelevant, yet we know perception depends on proximity. An objection that instruments assist the soul is then addressed, showing that the true perceiver must be a corporeal power within the organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a sense of the argument flow.
Notice the structure: claim → conditional proof → objection → response.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify why the soul cannot be the perceiver: it is immaterial and thus not bound by spatial relations. Therefore, perception should not depend on proximity.
The key is the immaterial nature of the soul.
3
Examine the objection
The objection attempts to save the soul's role by saying it uses bodily instruments that have spatial location.
4
See the response
The response shows that without a corporeal power (like seeing power) in the instrument, the instrument's location is irrelevant. So the objection fails.
This reinforces that the actual perceiver is a corporeal power.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument supports the broader claim that external perceptions are corporeal, not purely mental acts of the soul.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجوه الخاصة : فقد احتجوا على أن الإدراكات الظاهرة : جسمانية . بأن قالوا : لو كان المدرك للمحسوسات الظاهرة هو النفس ، لوجب أن لا يتوقف إحساس النفس بها، على حضورها. وكان يجب أن يكون إدراك الإنسان للقريب والبعيد والغائب والحاضر واحداً . لأن النفس جوهر غير جسماني ، فيمتنع أن يكون لها قرب وبعد من الأجسام يصح . فإن قالوا : النفس إنما تدرك هذه المحسوسات بمعاونة هذه الآلات التي عليها القرب والبعد . قلنا : العين إذا لم تكن فيها قوة باصرة ، لم يكن القرب والبعد حاصلاً بالنسبة إلى الرائي ، بل بالنسبة إلى غيره . وذلك لا فائدة
As for the special aspects: They argued that external perceptions are corporeal, by saying: If the perceiver of external sensibles were the soul, then it would be necessary that the soul's sensation of them does not depend on their presence, and it would have to be that a person's perception of the near, the far, the absent, and the present is one. Because the soul is an immaterial substance, so it cannot have nearness and distance from bodies validly. If they say: The soul perceives these sensibles only with the help of these instruments that have nearness and distance. We say: If the eye does not have a seeing power, then nearness and distance are not present relative to the seer, but relative to another. And that is of no benefit.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents a specific argument that external perceptions are corporeal, followed by an objection and its refutation. The proponents (presumably those arguing for corporeality) state that if the soul itself were the perceiver of external objects, then its perception would not depend on the object being present, since the soul is immaterial and not limited by spatial relations. Consequently, a person would perceive near, far, absent, and present objects in the same way, which is false. The objection claims that the soul perceives through bodily instruments that have spatial location. The response argues that if the eye lacks the power of vision (a corporeal faculty), then nearness and distance are not relevant to the seer but to something else, so the objection fails to rescue the immateriality of perception.

Key Terms

إدراكات ظاهرة (external perceptions)
نفس (soul)
آلات (instruments)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is dismissed simply; the response shows that the corporeal power is essential, not just the instrument.
Correction: The response does not dismiss the objection outright but demonstrates that even with instruments, a corporeal power is necessary for perception, thus the soul alone is insufficient.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Two Proofs that Imagination is Corporeal
Two arguments that imagination requires a corporeal substratum: distinction of similar images and variation in magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs that imagination requires a corporeal substrate, based on the distinction of similar images and variation in imaginal magnitudes.

Core Idea
Imagination must be corporeal because we can distinguish identical images and images vary in size.

In this passage, the author presents two arguments that the faculty of imagination is not purely spiritual but requires a physical substrate. The first argument uses the mental distinction of two identical squares, showing they must occupy separate loci. The second argument notes that imaginary images vary in size, which must correspond to the size of the part of the imagination they occupy. Both point to a corporeal imaging faculty.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof carefully
Read the Arabic text of the first proof and its translation. Notice how the argument unfolds from the distinction of two imaginary squares.
2
Analyze the first proof's logic
Identify the key steps: we distinguish identical squares, they must exist mentally, if they were in one locus they would be indistinguishable, so they must be in separate loci, implying the locus is corporeal.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Read the second proof
Read the second proof about size variation. Contrast the imagination of a sea and an atom.
4
Analyze the second proof
Understand why the size variation cannot be from the external object (since we can imagine non-existent things) and must be from the size of the part of imagination that receives the image.
This demonstrates that imagination has parts of different sizes.
5
Synthesize both proofs
Reflect on how both proofs converge on the same conclusion: imagination is corporeal. Consider why this matters for a theological understanding of the soul.
These arguments are foundational for discussing the nature of the human soul and its faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنا إذا تخيلنا مربعاً مجنحاً بمربعين متساويين فلا شك أنه يتميز أحد هذين الجناحين عن الثاني في الفهم والإدراك . والنفي المحض والعدم الصرف ، يمتنع وقوع الامتياز فيه . فهذان المربعان لا بد وأن يكونا موجودين ، وأن ذلك قد يكون غير موجود في الخارج ، فهو في الذهن . فإما أن يكون محل أحد هذين المربعين متساويان في الماهية ، وفي جميع لوازم الماهية ، فلو حصلا في محل واحد ، لارتفع الامتياز بالصفات العرضية أيضاً ، فوجب أن لا يبقى الامتياز أصلا . وذلك باطل . وأن العلم الضروري حاصل بأنا نميز أحدهما عن الآخر . ولما بطل هذان القسمان تعين الثاني ، وهو أن يكون محل أحد هذين المربعين مبايناً عن محل المربع الثاني . وهذا لا يعقل إلا إذا كان محل هذا الخيال جسماً حتى يحصل في أحد جزئي ذلك الجسم، إحدى الصورتين. وفي الجزء الثاني منه ، الصورة الثانية . الثاني : إن الصورة الخيالية متفاوتة في المقادير. فإن تخيل البحر أعظم من تخيل الذرة . وهذا التفاوت إما أن يكون بسبب المأخوذ عنه أو بسبب الأخذ والأول باطل . لأنا قد نتخيل ما لا وجود له في الخارج . فبقي الثاني ، وهو أن تكون الصورة ، تارة ترتسم في جزء كبير ، فتكون تلك الصورة أكبر ، وتارة في جزء صغير فتكون تلك الصورة أصغر
The first: When we imagine a square with two equal wings, there is no doubt that one of these two wings is distinguished from the second in understanding and perception. And pure negation and absolute non-existence cannot have distinction occur in them. So these two squares must exist, and it is apparent that they may not exist externally, so they are in the mind. Then either the locus of each of the two squares is equal in essence and in all necessary properties, then if they occur in one locus, distinction by accidental attributes would also be removed, so it would be necessary that no distinction remains at all. That is false. And necessary knowledge is obtained that we distinguish one from the other. So when these two divisions are invalidated, the second is determined: that the locus of one of the two squares is separate from the locus of the second square. And this is only conceivable if the locus of this imagination is a body, so that one of the two images is obtained in one part of that body, and the second image in the second part. The second: The imaginal image varies in magnitudes. For the imagination of the sea is greater than the imagination of an atom. This variation is either due to the thing taken from or due to the taking. The first is false, because we may imagine what has no existence externally. So the second remains: that the image is sometimes impressed in a large part, so that image is larger, and sometimes in a small part, so that image is smaller.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents the first two of three proofs that imagination (تخيل) is corporeal. The first proof: When we imagine two identical squares (wings), we can distinguish them mentally. If the imagination were purely non-spatial, two identical essences in one locus would be indistinguishable, but we do distinguish them. Therefore, each image must have a separate locus, implying that the imagination (the locus) is a body with parts. The second proof: Imaginal images vary in size (e.g., imagining a sea vs. an atom). This size variation cannot be due to the external object (since we can imagine non-existent things), so it must be due to the size of the part of the imagination in which the image is impressed. This again implies that the imagination is corporeal, with parts of different sizes.

Key Terms
تخيل (imagination)
امتياز (distinction)
محل (locus)
جزء (part)
مقدار (magnitude)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof is about distinguishing two physical squares outside the mind.
Correction: The proof is about mental images of two identical squares that must be distinguished within the mind itself.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, why must the two imaginary squares have separate loci?
A) They are physically separate
B) If in one locus, distinction would be impossible, but we do distinguish them
C) The squares are different colors
D) The mind cannot hold two images together

Key Takeaway
Imagination requires a corporeal substrate, as shown by the need for distinct loci for distinct images and by the variation in imaginal sizes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your imagination is corporeal—it is physically rooted in your brain. When you allow a sinful image to linger in your mind, do you realize that you are giving it a physical place in your body? How does that change your resolve to guard your thoughts?
?
The second proof shows that the size of a mental image depends on how much 'space' it occupies in your imaginative faculty. You can make an image of the sea larger than an atom. Now, think about the images you entertain: are you giving more mental 'space' to worldly concerns than to thoughts of Allah? What would it take to shift that proportion?
?
If your imagination is tied to a physical substrate, then your thoughts have real physical effects. This means that every time you imagine something, you are not just 'thinking'—you are literally reshaping your brain. What does this imply about the command to avoid imagining sinful acts? Will you now take more seriously the Prophet's teaching (peace be upon him) that the inner thoughts must be guarded?

The Third Objection: Brain Cannot Map All Image Parts
Presents the third objection against the corporeality of imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the corporeality of imagination: that the brain's limited size cannot accommodate the one-to-one mapping required for complex mental images.

Core Idea
The brain's small size defeats the idea of images being corporeally stored part-by-part.

This lesson examines a clever argument against the view that imagination is a physical form in the brain. The objector points out that when we imagine vast landscapes, the brain would need a dedicated spot for every tiny detail—an impossibility given its limited size. This objection challenges the entire corporeal model of imagination by highlighting a mismatch between the capacity of the brain and the richness of our mental imagery.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is presented as a concise dilemma.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the two premises: (1) man travels and sees vast imagery, (2) if every part of that image requires a specific brain part, then the brain is too small.
3
Examine the two horns of the dilemma
The argument presents two alternatives: either mapping is necessary (leading to insufficiency) or not necessary (invalidating the theory). Understand how each horn undermines the corporeal view.
This is a classic logical dilemma.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice the cause-effect: if mapping is needed → brain insufficient; if mapping not needed → theory collapses. Either way, the opponent loses.
The conclusion 'then your statement is invalidated' is the key.
5
Consider the implications
Ask yourself why this argument is significant for the debate on the nature of imagination. How does it show that imagination cannot be a corporeal form?
This connects to the broader topic of the rational soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الإنسان ربما طاف في العالم ، ورأى البلاد والجبال والبحار والمفاوز ، فلو وجب أن يحصل لكل جزء من أجزاء هذه الصورة جزء معين من الدماغ ، فهذا بعيد . لأن الجزء الصغير من الدماغ لا يفي بذلك ، وإن لم يجب هذا الامتياز فقد بطل قولكم .
Third: Indeed, man sometimes travels in the world, and sees lands, mountains, seas, and deserts. If it were necessary that for every part of the parts of this image a specific part of the brain should occur, this is far-fetched. Because the small part of the brain does not suffice for that. And if this distinction is not necessary, then your statement is invalidated.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the view that imagination is a corporeal form imprinted in the brain. The objector argues that when a person travels and sees vast landscapes, the resulting mental image would require each tiny part of that image to correspond to a specific brain part. Since the brain is small, it cannot accommodate all those parts. If the opponent denies that each part requires a specific brain part (i.e., denies the one-to-one mapping), then their core claim—that imagination is corporeal and localised in the brain—collapses because they lose the basis for any specific localisation. This is a classic argument from magnitude against physicalist theories of imagination.

Key Terms
خيال (imagination)
دماغ (brain)
صورة (image)
جزء (part)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is about memory capacity or the brain's ability to store information.
Correction: The objection is specifically about the necessity of a one-to-one mapping between image parts and brain parts, not about storage capacity in general.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the third objection against the corporeality of imagination?
A) The brain is too heavy to store images.
B) Each part of an image would require a specific brain part, but the brain is too small.
C) Images are immaterial by nature.
D) Traveling causes the brain to shrink.

Key Takeaway
The third objection uses a dilemma to show that the brain's limited size cannot support a one-to-one mapping of image parts, thereby refuting the corporeality of imagination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall a vast scene, like a mountain range or an ocean, does it feel like a physical picture stored in your brain? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what does it imply about the nature of your own soul?
?
This argument shows that the physical brain cannot contain the sheer detail of a simple memory. Think about a memory that is deeply vivid for you—has it ever felt too big for your head? What does that tell you about the reality of your inner world?
?
If the imaginative soul is not bound by physical limits, then your mind is not limited to the size of your skull. How does that change the way you view your capacity for knowledge, reflection, and spiritual growth? Will you stop letting 'I can't handle this' be an excuse?

Answering the Objection on Image Size
Answers the objection about differences in sizes of imaginary forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the size of an imaginary form does not depend on the size of its brain locus, and how a dilemma refutes the objection.

Core Idea
A small locus can hold a large image; size differences don't require different brain sizes.

This passage refutes an objection that differences in the sizes of imagined forms must be due to differences in the size of brain parts. The answer uses a powerful dilemma to show that the objection's assumption is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: the objector's claim followed by the answer.
The answer begins with 'And the answer to what they mentioned seventhly'.
2
Identify the dilemma's two horns
The answer splits the objector's assumption into two possibilities: either small locus forces small image, or it does not. Find both in the text.
The first horn is introduced by 'If the smallness of the locus necessitates...'
3
See the consequence of the first horn
If small locus forces small image, then humans could only imagine tiny things equal to a brain part. That is absurd because we imagine vast scenes. Understand why this is impossible.
This horn leads to a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand how the second horn invalidates the objection
If small locus does not force small image, then the objector's premise—that size differences require different loci—is false. The locus can be small yet host a large image.
The second horn directly refutes the objection.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: does this answer assume images are in the brain? No, it only shows the size argument fails. The objection is defeated regardless of where images reside.
This connects to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكروه سابعاً من أن اختلاف الصور الخيالية في المقادير يجب أن يكون لاختلاف الآخذين في المقدار . فنقول : إن كان صغر المحل يقتضي صغر الصورة الحالة فيه، وجب أن لا يقدر الإنسان على تخيل شيء ، إلا بمقدار ما يساوي جزءاً من أجزاء دماغه . وإن كان صغر المحل لا يوجب صغر الصورة ، ولا يمنع من كبرها ، فقد بطل دليلكم .
And the answer to what they mentioned seventhly: that the difference of imaginary forms in magnitudes must be due to the difference of the recipients in magnitude. So we say: If the smallness of the locus necessitates the smallness of the form dwelling in it, then it would be necessary that man cannot imagine anything except to the extent that equals a part of the parts of his brain. And if the smallness of the locus does not necessitate the smallness of the form and does not prevent its largeness, then your proof is invalidated.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage contains the answer to the seventh objection, which claimed that differences in sizes of imaginary forms must be due to differences in sizes of the corresponding brain parts. The answer offers a dilemma: either a smaller brain location forces the image to be small, or it does not. If it forces smallness, then humans could never imagine anything larger than a tiny brain part, which is absurd (we imagine vast scenes). If it does not force smallness, then the original objection—that differences in image size require differences in brain part size—has no basis, because a small locus can accommodate a large image. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
محل (locus)
صورة خيالية (imaginary form)
مقدار (magnitude)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer concedes that images are in the brain.
Correction: The answer only rebuts the argument from size; it does not affirm that images are corporeally located in the brain.

Checkpoint

B) Either a small locus forces a small image (impossible) or it does not (refuting the objection).
C) Either imagination is corporeal or it is not.
D) Either the image is real or it is imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The size of an imaginary form is not determined by the size of its brain locus, so the objection from size differences fails.
