The Third Condition: Analogy of Corporeal and Spiritual Lights
Analogy between physical sight and intellectual vision with an enumeration of lights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the analogy of physical sight to explain the need for spiritual illumination, and how the hierarchy of lights in the corporeal world mirrors that of the spiritual world.

Core Idea
Just as physical sight needs light, the intellect needs spiritual illumination to perceive truth.

This passage builds on the previous conditions for intellectual vision by introducing a powerful analogy. The author compares the necessity of physical light for sight to the necessity of spiritual light for the intellect. He then maps the hierarchy of lights in both realms, culminating in a classification of those who attain illumination through purification versus rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the parallel structure between corporeal and spiritual lights.
The author uses a clear 'كذلك' (likewise) to draw the analogy.
2
Identify the four lights in each world
List the four lights of the corporeal world and their corresponding spiritual lights. Understand the ranking from greatest to least.
The sun is the greatest in the corporeal world; the First Principle (Allah) is the greatest in the spiritual.
3
Understand the two types of human spirits
Note the two paths to illumination: purification and rational proof. Identify who belongs to each category.
Angels, prophets, and saints are in the first; philosophers in the second.
4
Reflect on the analogy's purpose
Ask yourself: Why does the author use this analogy? What does it teach about the intellect's dependence on divine light?
This reinforces the need for spiritual preparation and divine grace.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشرط الثالث : إن القوة الباصرة لا يمكنها إدراك المبصرات ، إلا عند صيرورة الهواء مضيئاً ، بسبب طلوع الأشياء النيرة . فكذلك العقل لا يقدر على الإبصار إلا عند طلوع النيرات الروحانية . ثم نيرات العالم الجسماني أربعة : الشمس والقمر والكواكب والنار . وأعظمها : الشمس ، ثم القمر ، ثم الكواكب ، ثم النار . فكذلك نيرات العالم الروحاني أربعة : المبدأ الأول - تعالى وتقدس - وبعده الروح الأعظم، الذي هو أشرف الأرواح المقدسة ، وبعده درجات الملائكة ، مثل : مراتب الكواكب ، وبعده العقل البشري وهو بمنزلة النار . ومراتب الأرواح البشرية على نوعين : منها ما يكون إشراقها وقوتها بسبب التصفية وتطهير النفس عن غير الله تعالى ، وبعضها بسبب تركيب البراهين اليقينية . والأولون هم الملائكة والأنبياء والأولياء . والثاني هم الحكماء
And the third condition: The power of sight cannot perceive visible objects except when the air becomes illuminated due to the rising of luminous things. Likewise, the intellect cannot see except at the rising of spiritual lights. Then the lights of the corporeal world are four: the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. The greatest of them is the sun, then the moon, then the stars, then fire. Likewise, the lights of the spiritual world are four: the First Principle—Exalted and Sanctified—and after Him the Supreme Spirit, which is the noblest of the holy spirits; after him the degrees of angels, like the ranks of the stars; and after him the human intellect, which is in the position of fire. And the ranks of human spirits are of two types: some attain illumination and strength through purification and cleansing the soul from other than Allah, and some through the composition of certain proofs. The first are the angels, prophets, and saints; the second are the philosophers.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the third condition for intellectual vision through an analogy with physical sight. The author first states that just as the power of sight requires illuminated air to see objects, the intellect requires the rising of spiritual lights to perceive truths. He then draws a parallel between the four lights of the corporeal world—sun, moon, stars, and fire—and the four lights of the spiritual world: the First Principle (Allah), the Supreme Spirit (often identified with the Holy Spirit or the highest angel), the degrees of angels, and the human intellect. The human intellect is compared to fire, the lowest of the corporeal lights. After this, the author classifies human spirits into two types: those who achieve illumination through purification (the prophets, saints, and angels) and those who achieve it through rational proofs (the philosophers). This distinction highlights the two paths to knowledge: spiritual purification and rational demonstration.

النيرات الروحانية
المبدأ الأول
الروح الأعظم
العقل البشري
التصفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the intellect can know God by its own power without spiritual illumination.
Correction: The author argues that the intellect requires spiritual lights (divine illumination) just as physical sight needs physical light. The least of these lights is the human intellect itself, which must be illuminated from above.

Checkpoint

A) The moon
B) The sun
C) Fire

Key Takeaway
The intellect cannot function without spiritual illumination, which comes in a hierarchy from Allah down to the human intellect, and is attained through purification or rational proof.

If the human intellect is like fire—the lowest light—then your unaided reason is weak. Do you humbly seek illumination from above, or do you trust your own mental fire to light the way? What would change if you begged for divine light today?

The Intellect’s Flames: First Three Defects
The author enumerates three defects of the intellect's light compared to fire and a lamp.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three inherent weaknesses of the human intellect, as illustrated through analogies to fire and a lamp.

Core Idea
The intellect’s light is mixed with doubt, burns falsehood, but is easily extinguished.

The author compares the intellect to fire and a lamp, revealing that its light is not pure. Just as fire has smoke that blackens, a burning property, and is easily extinguished, the intellect suffers from similar defects: it is clouded by doubts, can aggressively refute falsehood, and is fragile. This passage lays out the first three of these defects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the literal translation. Notice how the author uses a pattern: 'The first... The second... The third...'.
2
Understand the analogy structure
Identify the two things being compared in each defect: the light of fire / the light of the intellect; the radiance and burning of fire / the radiance and burning of intellect; the lamp going out / the intellect going out.
Ask yourself why each comparison is made.
3
Identify each defect
For each defect, write down what the defect is and what it means for the intellect. For example, defect 1: intellect is mixed with doubts like fire with smoke.
The smoke of doubts obscures clear thinking.
4
Reflect on the overall message
Consider what these defects tell us about the limitations of human reason. Why might the author be pointing this out?
This prepares us for the need for divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن نور العقل له عيوب كما أن النار لها عيوب فالأول : إن نور النار ممزوج بدخان كثير يسود الثوب ، ويجفف الدماغ ، وكذلك نور العقل ممزوج بدخان الشبهات . والثاني : إن نور النار فيه إشراق وإحراق ، وكذلك نور العقل فيه إشراق ، وهو إذا وقع على الدلائل ، وإحراق إذا وقع على الشبهات والثالث : إن نور السراج ينطفىء بأدنى سبب ، وكذلك سراج العقل ينطفىء بأدنى شبهة .
And know: The light of the intellect has defects just as fire has defects. The first: The light of fire is mixed with much smoke that blackens the garment and dries the brain; likewise, the light of the intellect is mixed with the smoke of doubts. The second: The light of fire has radiance and burning; likewise, the light of the intellect has radiance when it falls upon proofs, and burning when it falls upon doubts. The third: The light of the lamp goes out at the slightest cause; likewise, the lamp of the intellect goes out at the slightest doubt.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first three defects of the intellect's light, comparing it to fire and a lamp. After an introductory statement that the intellect has defects, the author presents three distinct analogies. Defect one: just as fire's light is mixed with smoke that blackens and dries, the intellect's light is mixed with the smoke of doubts, obscuring truth. Defect two: fire has both radiance (beneficial) and burning (harmful); similarly, the intellect's light illuminates proofs but can burn or refute doubts—this implies a destructive power against falsehood. Defect three: a lamp is easily extinguished; likewise, the intellect is easily confused by even minor doubts, indicating its fragility.

Key Terms
دخان الشبهات
إشراق
إحراق
ينطفىء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect is a pure, reliable source of truth without any mixture of falsehood.
Correction: The author demonstrates that the intellect is mixed with doubts (smoke) and is fragile, so it cannot be trusted entirely on its own.

Checkpoint
According to the second defect, what does the light of the intellect do when it falls upon doubts?
A) It illuminates them
B) It extinguishes them
C) It burns them
D) It ignores them

Key Takeaway
The intellect, like fire, has flaws: it is clouded by doubt, can burn falsehood, but is easily extinguished by uncertainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Fourth Defect: Intellect's Light Dims in Lofty Subjects
The author describes the fourth defect using the lamp metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth defect of the intellect's light: its limitation to lowly subjects and its extinction when faced with high metaphysical inquiries.

Core Idea
The intellect shines in worldly calculations but is extinguished by questions about the soul and God.

This lesson continues the critique of the intellect's light. The fourth defect compares the intellect to a lamp that only illuminates a small room; in a vast desert it's useless. Similarly, the intellect works well for arithmetic and geometry but fails when tackling high spiritual matters—it gets confused and goes out. This is why attempts to know the soul lead to the very doubts discussed in this book.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for defect four carefully. Note the lamp analogy and its application to the intellect.
2
Understand the analogy
A lamp shines in a small house but not in a wide desert. Relate this to the intellect: it works on lowly subjects (math, geometry) but fails on high subjects.
Lowly subjects are earthly and tangible; high subjects are metaphysical.
3
Reflect on the soul's self-knowledge
The author adds that when the soul seeks knowledge of itself, the intellect is extinguished, leading to the doubts discussed in the book. Consider why self-knowledge is a 'high subject'.
This is a key example of the intellect's limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع : إن السراج إنما يضيء إذا وضع في بيت صغير ، وإما إذا وضع في صحراء واسعة فإنه يقل ضوؤه ، ويصير كالمظلم . وكذلك سراج العقل ، إنما يظهر نوره إذا استعمل في المطالب الحقيرة ، كالحساب والهندسة . فأما إذا استعمل في المطالب العالية ، فإنه ينطفىء . بل نقول : إن الروح لما طلب معرفة نفسه ، صار كالمنطفىء ، وحصلت له هذه الشبهات المشروحة في هذا الكتاب والخامس : ظهور نور السراج، مشروط بأن يحصل بينه وبين قرص الشمس حائل . فإذا وضع في مقابلة قرص الشمس : انطفا . وكذلك سراج العقل ، إذا وضع في مقابلة عالم الأرواح المطهرة : انطفأ والسادس : إن نور السراج وإن طال بقاؤه ، ولكنه بالآخرة ينطفىء . وإن قدرنا أنه يستمر ، لكنه إذا طلعت الشمس تبطل ضوءه . وكذلك نور سراج العقل . إما أن ينطفى لطريان الغفلات والشبهات ، أو إن بقي إلى آخر العمر لكنه عند موت البدن تتجلى له من عالم الغيب أنوار ، لا يبقى لنور عقله في مقابلتها أثر
The fourth: The lamp only shines when placed in a small house; but if placed in a wide desert, its light diminishes and becomes like darkness. Likewise, the lamp of the intellect only shows its light when used in lowly subjects, like arithmetic and geometry. But if used in high subjects, it goes out. Rather we say: When the soul sought knowledge of itself, it became like extinguished, and these doubts mentioned in this book befell it. The fifth: The appearance of the lamp's light is conditional upon there being a barrier between it and the sun's disk. If it is placed opposite the sun's disk, it goes out. Likewise, the lamp of the intellect goes out when placed opposite the world of purified spirits. The sixth: The light of the lamp, though it may last long, eventually goes out. And if we suppose it continues, when the sun rises, its light is nullified. Likewise, the light of the lamp of the intellect: either it goes out due to incursions of heedlessness and doubts, or if it remains until the end of life, yet at the death of the body, lights from the unseen world manifest to it, and no trace remains of its intellect's light in contrast to them.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents the last three defects (4–6) of the intellect's light, again using the lamp metaphor. Defect four: a lamp only illuminates a confined space; similarly, the intellect works well on lowly, concrete subjects like math but is extinguished by lofty metaphysical inquiries. The author adds that when the soul tries to know itself, it becomes confused—these are the very doubts discussed in the book. Defect five: a lamp needs a barrier from the sun's direct light; if placed opposite the sun, it goes out. Likewise, the intellect is overwhelmed and extinguished when faced with the pure spiritual world (the realm of angels or God's light). Defect six: a lamp eventually goes out, and even if it persists, sunrise nullifies its light. Similarly, the intellect may be extinguished by heedlessness or mortality, but even if it persists till death, at death the soul is exposed to a far greater light from the unseen world, rendering the intellect's light negligible.

المطالب العالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect can equally handle lowly and lofty subjects without limitation.
Correction: According to this defect, the intellect is only effective for lowly, material subjects and is extinguished when applied to high spiritual truths, such as the nature of the soul.

Checkpoint

A) It shines brighter
B) It goes out
C) It becomes confused but remains
D) It transforms

Key Takeaway
The intellect's light is effective only for worldly matters; it is extinguished by spiritual inquiries such as knowledge of the soul.

?

What would it look like for you to humbly turn away from intellectual pride and instead seek knowledge of your soul through the guidance of revelation and spiritual practice starting today?

The Fifth Defect: Intellect Overwhelmed by Pure Spirits
The author presents the fifth defect using the sun barrier analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth defect of the intellect's light: its need for a barrier from the pure spiritual world, without which it is extinguished.

Core Idea
The intellect is like a lamp that goes out when placed opposite the sun; it cannot face the pure spiritual realm.

The fifth defect extends the lamp analogy. A lamp needs a barrier from the sun's disk to shine; if placed directly opposite, it goes out. Similarly, the intellect's light is extinguished when confronted with the world of purified spirits (the realm of angels or God's light). This teaches that the intellect cannot directly perceive or withstand the highest spiritual realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for defect five. Notice the condition for the lamp's light and its application to the intellect.
2
Analyze the sun barrier analogy
A lamp needs a barrier ( حائل) from the sun's disk. Without it, the lamp's light is overwhelmed. What does the 'sun' represent here?
The sun symbolizes the pure spiritual world.
3
Contemplate the intellect's limitation
The intellect goes out when placed opposite 'the world of purified spirits'. This shows that direct exposure to divine realities extinguishes rational thought.
This is a strong statement about the inadequacy of reason in matters of faith.

The Sixth Defect: Intellect's Light is Temporary and Overcome by Death
The author describes the sixth defect using the lamp's eventual extinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth defect of the intellect's light: its impermanence and its vanishing in the face of greater lights from the unseen world at death.

Core Idea
The intellect's light eventually goes out or is overshadowed by the greater lights of the afterlife.

The sixth and final defect in this series addresses the temporal nature of the intellect's light. Like a lamp that eventually goes out or is nullified by sunrise, the intellect's light may be extinguished during life by heedlessness and doubts, or if it persists until death, it is completely overshadowed by the luminous realities of the unseen world that manifest to the soul at that moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage for defect six. Note the two scenarios: extinguishing during life or being overwhelmed at death.
2
Compare with lamp analogy
A lamp eventually goes out, but even if it lasts, sunrise nullifies its light. How does this apply to the intellect?
Sunrise represents the afterlife or higher realities.
3
Apply to human experience
The intellect can be lost due to heedlessness and doubts. Alternatively, at death, the soul experiences lights from the unseen that make the intellect's light insignificant.
This highlights the temporary and limited nature of rational knowledge.

The Fifth and Sixth Lamp Analogies: The Intellect's Limitations
The author presents the fifth and sixth lamp analogies illustrating the intellect's extinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses two analogies to illustrate the intellect's inability to grasp high spiritual truths without divine illumination or after death.

Core Idea
The intellect, like a lamp, is overwhelmed when confronting the divine light.

In this passage, the author presents two vivid analogies comparing the intellect to a lamp. The first analogy highlights the need for a screen (barrier) when facing intense spiritual light, while the second shows the intellect's ultimate extinction at death. These analogies emphasize the limitations of unaided reason in grasping higher realities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Read the first few lines: the intellect's light appears only for lowly matters (arithmetic, geometry) but is extinguished for high pursuits. Understand that the author is setting up the problem of intellectual limitation.
Notice the phrase 'إذا استعمل في المطالب العالية، فإنه ينطفىء' (when used for high pursuits, it is extinguished).
2
Examine the fifth analogy
Find the fifth analogy: a lamp's light requires a screen between it and the sun; without the screen, it is extinguished. Similarly, the lamp of the intellect is extinguished when placed opposite the world of purified spirits. Identify the condition and the corresponding spiritual reality.
3
Examine the sixth analogy
Read the sixth analogy: even a long-lasting lamp eventually extinguishes; if it continues, it vanishes at sunrise. Likewise, the intellect's light is extinguished by forgetfulness and doubts, or at death it is overwhelmed by divine lights. Reflect on the ultimate fate of the intellect according to the author.
This analogy emphasizes the transience and dependence of the intellect on divine illumination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما يظهر نوره إذا استعمل في المطالب الحقيرة ، كالحساب والهندسة . فأما إذا استعمل في المطالب العالية ، فإنه ينطفىء . بل نقول : إن الروح لما طلب معرفة نفسه ، صار كالمنطفىء ، وحصلت له هذه الشبهات المشروحة في هذا الكتاب والخامس : ظهور نور السراج، مشروط بأن يحصل بينه وبين قرص الشمس حائل . فإذا وضع في مقابلة قرص الشمس : انطفا . وكذلك سراج العقل ، إذا وضع في مقابلة عالم الأرواح المطهرة : انطفأ . والسادس : إن نور السراج وإن طال بقاؤه ، ولكنه بالآخرة ينطفىء . وإن قدرنا أنه يستمر ، لكنه إذا طلعت الشمس تبطل ضوءه . وكذلك نور سراج العقل . إما أن ينطفى لطريان الغفلات والشبهات ، أو إن بقي إلى آخر العمر لكنه عند موت البدن تتجلى له من عالم الغيب أنوار ، لا يبقى لنور عقله في مقابلتها أثر
Its light only appears when used for lowly pursuits, like calculation and geometry. But when used for high pursuits, it is extinguished. Rather, we say: when the soul sought knowledge of itself, it became like an extinguished lamp, and these doubts explained in this book occurred. The fifth: The appearance of the lamp's light is conditioned by a screen between it and the disk of the sun. So if it is placed opposite the disk of the sun, it is extinguished. Likewise, the lamp of the intellect, if placed opposite the world of purified spirits, is extinguished. The sixth: The light of the lamp, even if it lasts long, eventually is extinguished. And if we suppose it continues, yet when the sun rises, its light vanishes. Likewise, the light of the lamp of the intellect: either it is extinguished due to the occurrence of forgetfulness and doubts, or if it lasts until the end of life, yet at the death of the body, lights from the unseen world manifest to it such that no trace of its intellect's light remains in their presence.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents two analogies (the fifth and sixth) from a series comparing the intellect to a lamp. The author first establishes a general principle: the light of the intellect appears only when used for lowly matters like arithmetic and geometry, but when applied to lofty matters, it is extinguished. He then elaborates that the soul, by seeking self-knowledge, becomes like an extinguished lamp, incurring the doubts detailed in this book. The fifth analogy: a lamp's light requires a screen between it and the sun to be visible; if placed directly opposite the sun, it is extinguished. Similarly, the lamp of the intellect is extinguished when placed opposite the world of purified spirits. The sixth analogy: even a long-lasting lamp eventually extinguishes, or if it continues, its light vanishes at sunrise. Likewise, the lamp of the intellect either extinguishes due to forgetfulness and doubts, or if it lasts until death, at death it is overwhelmed by divine lights that leave no trace of its own light. The analogies emphasize the limitations of human reason when confronting higher realities.

Correction: The author shows that the intellect is limited: it is extinguished when confronting high spiritual realities or at death, requiring a 'screen' or being overwhelmed by divine light.

Two Types of Perception and the Senses' Dual Role
Classifies perceptions into badihiyyat and nazariyyat and explains sensory assistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between self-evident and theoretical perceptions, and how the senses assist in perfecting both the soul and the body.

Core Idea
The senses help the soul acquire knowledge and the body maintain itself.

In this passage, the author classifies our perceptions into two fundamental types: those that are immediately known (badihiyyat) and those that require reasoning (nazariyyat). He then explains how the senses support both the soul's intellectual perfection and the body's physical preservation. This lesson unpacks that dual role.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first distinguishes two types of perception, then relates each to the senses' assistance.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two types of perception
Find where the author defines badihiyyat and nazariyyat. Notice the criterion: whether a perception is known through negation or affirmation immediately or not.
This classification is foundational in Islamic epistemology.
3
Understand the senses' two assistances
Distinguish between the senses' role in perfecting the soul (by providing raw data for theoretical knowledge) and perfecting the body (by distinguishing suitable from harmful substances).
The body's hot and moist nature necessitates sensory discrimination for survival.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البعض ، بالنفي أو بالإثبات . ومنها : ما لا يكون كذلك . فالأول : هو البديهيات . والثاني : هو النظريات. فهذا بيان معونة الحواس ، في تكميل جوهر النفس. وأما معونتها في تكميل جوهر البدن فهو أنا بينا : أن البدن حار رطب ، فيكون أبداً في التحلل والذبول . ولهذا السبب يحتاج إلى إيراد بدل مـا تحلل عنه ، ولا بد من التميز بين ما يكون ملائماً ، وبين ما يكون منافياً . فهذا البيان معونة الحواس في تكميل جوهر البدن .
Some of them are by negation or affirmation. And some of them are not like that. The first are the self-evident truths (badihiyyat). The second are the theoretical truths (nazariyyat). So this is an explanation of the assistance of the senses in perfecting the substance of the soul. As for their assistance in perfecting the substance of the body, it is that we have explained: the body is hot and moist, thus it is constantly in dissolution and withering. For this reason it needs to bring in a replacement for what has dissolved from it, and there must be discrimination between what is suitable and what is harmful. So this is an explanation of the assistance of the senses in perfecting the substance of the body.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage classifies perceptions into two types: those that are known through negation or affirmation (i.e., immediate judgments) and those that are not. The first are called badihiyyat (self-evident truths) and the second nazariyyat (theoretical truths). The author then states that the explanation of the senses' assistance in perfecting the soul's substance lies in this classification. Next, he turns to the body's perfection: since the body is hot and moist, it constantly undergoes dissolution (taḥallul) and withering (dhabūl), requiring replacement of lost parts. The senses help distinguish between suitable and harmful substances, thereby assisting in perfecting the body.

Key Terms
بديهيات
نظريات
معونة الحواس
تحلل
ذبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'negation or affirmation' refers to logical operators like in formal logic.
Correction: In this context, 'negation or affirmation' describes immediate sensory judgments, not logical propositions. It means the senses directly affirm or negate something (e.g., 'this is hot' is an immediate affirmation).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the body need the assistance of the senses?
A) Because the body is hot and moist and constantly dissolves.
B) Because the soul needs sensory input for theoretical knowledge.
C) Because the body is cold and dry.
D) Because the senses produce self-evident truths.

Key Takeaway
Perceptions are either self-evident or theoretical, and the senses assist both the soul's knowledge acquisition and the body's preservation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your senses are constantly providing you with data—for what purpose do you most often use them? Does your daily usage align with the dual purpose mentioned in this lesson: to perfect both your soul and your body? What is one concrete change you will make today to use your senses more intentionally for your soul's perfection?
?
The passage teaches that your body is in a constant state of dissolution. How does this reality affect your attachment to physical pleasures and worldly pursuits? Are you investing more in what fades (the body) or what lasts (the soul)?
?
You rely on your senses to distinguish between what is beneficial and harmful for your body. Do you also use them to seek knowledge that benefits your soul? What is one step you will take this week to use your senses—like hearing beneficial lectures or reading meaningful texts—to nourish your soul?

Human Moral Judgment vs. Animal Instinct
Explains the fourth human property: moral judgment, with two possible bases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the uniquely human capacity to judge good and evil, its two possible foundations (pure reason or social benefit), and how it differs from animal behavior.

Core Idea
Humans judge good and evil by reason or social need; animals only follow natural affection.

This lesson examines the fourth distinctive property of humans: the ability to morally evaluate actions as good or evil. The author presents two possible sources for this judgment—pure reason or social benefit—and then clarifies why animal behavior, such as a trained lion not preying on its owner, is fundamentally different from human moral restraint.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first, the definition of the fourth property and its two bases; second, the contrast with animals.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إما لأن صريح العقل يوجب ذلك وإما لأجل أن المصلحة'.
2
Identify the two bases for moral judgment
The passage gives two possible foundations for judging good and evil. Find them and understand each one: pure reason (صريح العقل) and social benefit (المصلحة الحاصلة بسبب المشاركة الإنسانية).
These represent two major schools in Islamic ethics: rationalist and social contract theory.
3
Compare human judgment with animal behavior
The passage uses the example of a trained lion. Contrast the lion's restraint (based on natural love for its feeder) with human moral judgment. Why does the author say they are different in kind?
The key word is 'ليس ذلك مشابهاً للحالة الحاصلة للإنسان'.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this distinction matters for theology and ethics. If morality is based on reason or social benefit, what does that say about moral responsibility?
This connects to debates about whether morality is objective or socially constructed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الرابع من خواص الإنسان : الحكم بحسن بعض الأشياء ، وقبح بعضها . إما لأن صريح العقل يوجب ذلك عند من يقول به ، وإما لأجل أن المصلحة الحاصلة بسبب المشاركة الإنسانية ، اقتضت تقريرها ، لتبقى مصالح العالم مرعية . وأما سائر الحيوانات ، فإنها إن تركت بعض الأشياء مثل الأسد المعلم فإنه لا يفترس صاحبه . وليس ذلك مشابهاً للحالة الحاصلة للإنسان ، بل هيئة أخرى. لأن كل حيوان فإنه يحب بالطبع كل من ينفعه . فلهذا السبب يكون الشخص الذي يطعمه محبوباً عنده ، فيصير ذلك مانعاً له عن افتراسه
And the fourth type of human properties: judging some things as good and some as evil. Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth human property: the ability to judge things as good or evil. It gives two possible bases: either pure reason necessitates it (for those who hold that view), or social benefit ('social interest' al-maslaha al-hasila bi-sabab al-musharaka al-insaniyya). The passage contrasts this with animal behavior, using a trained lion as example, showing that animal restraint is based on natural affection for the one who feeds it, not on moral judgment.

B) Pure reason and social benefit
C) Instinct and emotion

The Human Capacity for Moral Judgment
Sanusi defines the fourth human property: moral judgment and its basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth special human property: the ability to judge things as good or bad, and how it differs from animal behavior.

Core Idea
Humans alone judge good and bad; animals only act on natural affection.

This passage explores what makes human moral judgment unique. The author presents two possible foundations for moral judgments—reason or social utility—and then distinguishes this from the simple attraction animals feel toward those who benefit them. As you read, notice how the author uses the example of a trained lion to clarify the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through before you interpret.
Notice the two options given for the basis of moral judgment.
2
Identify the two bases for moral judgment
The passage states that moral judgment is based on either clear reason or social utility. Find these phrases in the text and note how they are described.
3
Contrast with animal behavior
Look at the example of the trained lion. The author says its restraint is not moral judgment but natural affection. Understand why this distinction matters.
The key difference: animals act from instinct, humans from reasoned judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الرابع من خواص الإنسان : الحكم بحسن بعض الأشياء ، وقبح بعضها . إما لأن صريح العقل يوجب ذلك عند من يقول به ، وإما لأجل أن المصلحة الحاصلة بسبب المشاركة الإنسانية ، اقتضت تقريرها ، لتبقى مصالح العالم مرعية . وأما سائر الحيوانات ، فإنها إن تركت بعض الأشياء مثل الأسد المعلم فإنه لا يفترس صاحبه . وليس ذلك مشابهاً للحالة الحاصلة للإنسان ، بل هيئة أخرى. لأن كل حيوان فإنه يحب بالطبع كل من ينفعه . فلهذا السبب يكون الشخص الذي يطعمه محبوباً عنده ، فيصير ذلك مانعاً له عن افتراسه
And the fourth type of human properties: judging some things as good and some as bad. Either because clear reason necessitates that, according to those who affirm it, or because the benefit resulting from human partnership necessitated its establishment, so that the interests of the world remain observed. As for other animals, if they refrain from some things like the trained lion, it does not prey upon its owner. And that is not similar to the state occurring for humans, but another disposition. Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth special characteristic of humans: the ability to judge actions as good or bad (ḥusn and qubḥ). The author presents two possible bases for this judgment: either reason itself necessitates such judgments (the view of rationalist ethicists), or social utility (al-maṣlaḥa) requires them to maintain the welfare of human society. He then contrasts this with animal behavior, using the example of a trained lion that does not attack its keeper. The lion's restraint is not a moral judgment but a natural affection born from being fed. Thus, human moral judgment is unique.

Correction: The passage explains that this is natural affection, not moral judgment. The lion refrains out of love for the one who feeds it, not because it judges that preying is wrong.

Checkpoint

A) Instinct and natural affection
B) Either clear reason or social utility
C) Divine revelation alone

Key Takeaway
Human moral judgment is unique, grounded in reason or social utility, unlike animal behavior driven by natural affection.

?

The Fifth Human Property: Remembering Past Events
Defines the fifth human property: memory of the past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth human property—memory of past events—and the caution required when judging its uniqueness to humans.

Core Idea
Certainty about memory's exclusivity to humans is problematic, even if plausible.

This lesson introduces the fifth human property: the ability to remember past events. The author notes a common claim that this capacity is absent in animals but warns that such certainty is difficult to attain. We will examine the passage and its implications for reasoning about human distinctiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

2

This shows scholarly caution.
3
Reflect on the author's caution
Ask yourself: why would certainty be 'mushkil' (problematic)? Consider that empirical evidence about animal cognition is limited.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Complete the checkpoint

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage presents the fifth human property: the ability to remember past events. The author notes a claim that this capacity is absent in animals. However, he remains cautious, stating that being certain about this, whether denying it to animals or affirming it for them alone, is problematic (mushkil). Thus, the passage does not settle the matter but highlights the difficulty of definitive judgment.

The Two Types of Thought and Their Scope
Sanusi classifies thought into two types and defines their temporal and modal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of thought (fikr) and how each relates to different categories of existence: necessary, impossible, and possible in its temporal dimensions.

Core Idea
Thought about bringing things into existence only applies to future possibles, not to the necessary, impossible, or past/present.

Sanusi begins by defining the sixth human property: thought (fikr) and deliberation (rawiyya). He immediately splits thought into two kinds: one for understanding existing states, applicable across time, and another for planning creation, which is tightly restricted. This classification is foundational for understanding how human action relates to possibility and time.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of thought into two types
Read the passage once to see the two kinds of thought: reflective and productive.
2
Notice the temporal scope of the first type
The first kind applies to past, present, and future. Understand that it is about knowing a state in itself.
3
Understand the restriction of the second type
The second kind is only possible with future contingent things, not the necessary, impossible, or past/present possibles.
This is a key point about modality and time.
4
See how thought leads to action
The judgment of this faculty results in decisive will and then bodily movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع السادس : الفكر والروية . وهذا الفكر على نوعين : أحدهما : أن يتفكر لأجل أن يعرف حالة في نفسه - وهذا النوع من الفكر : ممكن في الماضي والمستقبل والحاضر - والنوع الثاني : التفكر في كيفية إيجاده وتكوينه . وهذا النوع من الفكر لا يمكن في الواجب والممتنع ، وإنما يمكن في الممكن . ثم لا يمكن في الممكن الماضي، والحاضر وإنما يمكن في الممكن الاستقبالي . وإذا حكمت هذه القوة ، تبع حكمها حصول الإرادة الجازمة ، ويتبعها تأثير القوة والقدرة في تحريك البدن وهل لشيء من الحيوانات إدراك شيء من الكليات ؟ المشهور : إنكاره . وفيه موضع بحث . فإنها راغبة في كل ما يكون لذيذاً عندها ، نافرة عن كل ما يكون مؤلماً عندها . فوجب أن يتقرر عندها : أن كل لذيذ مطلوب ، وأن كل مؤلم مكروه . وأجيب عنه : بأن رغبتها إنما تكون في هذا اللذيذ . فكل لذيذ حضر عندها فإنها ترغب فيه ، من حيث إنه ذلك الشيء . فأما أن تعتقد أن كل لذيذ ، فهو مطلوب . فهذا ليس عندها . واعلم : أن الحكم في هذه الأشياء بالنفي والإثبات : حكم على الغيب والعلم بها ليس إلا الله العلي العليم
And the sixth type: thought and deliberation. This thought is of two kinds: one: that one thinks in order to know a state in itself – and this kind of thought is possible in the past, future, and present. And the second kind: thinking about how to bring it into existence and form it. And this kind of thought is not possible with the necessary (wājib) and impossible (mumtani‘), but is only possible with the possible (mumkin). Then it is not possible with the possible past and present, but only possible with the possible future. And when this faculty judges, its judgment is followed by the occurrence of decisive will, and it is followed by the effect of strength and power in moving the body. And do any animals have perception of any universals? The well-known view is its denial. But it is a subject of investigation. For they desire everything that is pleasurable to them and shrink from everything that is painful to them. So it would seem that it is established for them that every pleasurable thing is sought and every painful thing is disliked. And it was answered: that their desire is only for this particular pleasurable thing. So every pleasurable thing that is present to them, they desire it, insofar as it is that thing. But as for their believing that every pleasurable thing is sought – this is not present in them. And know: that judgment in these matters by negation or affirmation is a judgment about the unseen, and knowledge of them is only for God the Most High, the All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage covers the sixth human property: thought (fikr) and deliberation (rawiyya). It first classifies thought into two types: (1) thinking to know a state in itself, which applies to past, present, and future; (2) thinking about how to bring something into existence, which only applies to possible things (mumkin) in the future, not to the necessary (wājib) or impossible (mumtani‘), nor to past or present possibles. The result of this faculty is decisive will (irāda jāzima) and bodily movement. The passage then poses the question: Do animals perceive universals (kulliyyāt)? The well-known view denies it, but the author notes it is debatable. He presents an objection: animals desire pleasurable things and avoid painful ones, so it seems they have the universal concept that 'every pleasure is sought'. The answer distinguishes between desiring a particular pleasurable thing that is present and holding the universal belief that all pleasure is sought. Animals have only the particular desire, not the universal belief. The author concludes that certainty in such matters belongs to God alone.

Key Terms
الفكر
الروية
الواجب
الممتنع
الممكن
الإرادة الجازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that thought about how to create something can apply to all possible things regardless of time.
Correction: The passage clarifies it only applies to future possibles.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, on what kind of object is the second type of thought (thinking about how to bring something into existence) possible?
A) The necessary and impossible
B) The possible future
C) The possible past and present
D) All possible things regardless of time

Key Takeaway
The second type of thought (productive planning) only applies to future contingent things, not to the necessary, impossible, or past/present possibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you have been trying to 'fix' something in the past that cannot be changed. How does Sanusi's distinction between reflective thought and productive thought challenge your tendency to dwell on what is already fixed? Will you commit to redirecting that mental energy towards future possibilities?
?
When you plan for the future, do you ever try to plan for things that are impossible or unnecessary? Reflect on a goal you are pursuing: is it truly a possible future contingency, or are you wasting thought on things that cannot be brought about? What will you change today?
?
The passage says that once the faculty judges, decisive will follows. Is your will truly decisive when you intend an action, or do you often hesitate? Examine a current decision you are delaying: does your thought lead to a firm resolve, or does it remain idle speculation? What step will you take to move from thought to action?

Do Animals Perceive Universals? The Debate
Sanusi discusses the question of animal perception of universals and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for and against animal perception of universals, and the distinction between particular desire and universal belief.

Core Idea
Animals desire particular pleasures, not the universal concept that all pleasure is sought.

The author of the treatise raises a fascinating question: Do animals perceive universals? The well-known position denies it, but he finds the issue worthy of investigation. He presents an objection based on animals' consistent behavior and then answers it by distinguishing between desiring a particular and holding a universal belief. This lesson unpacks that dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from animal behavior
Animals seek pleasure and avoid pain – doesn't that imply they have the universal concepts of 'all pleasure is sought' and 'all pain is avoided'?
2
Understand the answer
The answer says animals only desire the particular pleasurable thing present to them, not the universal proposition.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends with a reminder that judging such matters belongs ultimately to God.

This passage covers the sixth human property: thought (fikr) and deliberation (rawiyya). It first classifies thought into two types: (1) thinking to know a state in itself, which applies to past, present, and future; (2) thinking about how to bring something into existence, which only applies to possible things (mumkin) in the future, not to the necessary (wājib) or impossible (mumtani‘), nor to past or present possibles. The result of this faculty is decisive will (irāda jāzima) and bodily movement. The passage then poses the question: Do animals perceive universals (kulliyyāt)? The well-known view denies it, but the author notes it is debatable. He presents an objection: animals desire pleasurable things and avoid painful ones, so it seems they have the universal concept that 'every pleasure is sought'. The answer distinguishes between desiring a particular pleasurable thing that is present and holding the universal belief that all pleasure is sought. Animals have only the particular desire, not the universal belief. The author concludes that certainty in such matters belongs to God alone.

Key Terms

اللذيذ
المؤلم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals have universal concepts because they consistently seek pleasure.
Correction: They only seek particular pleasures present to them, not the universal belief that all pleasure is desired.

Checkpoint

A) Because they believe all pleasure is good.
B) Because they desire that particular pleasurable thing present to them.
C) Because they have been taught by Allah.
D) Because they have universal knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Animals do not possess universal concepts; their desires are directed at particular individuals, not categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are blessed with an intellect that can grasp universal truths—truths about your Lord and His commands. Are you using that intellect to seek the ultimate pleasure of knowing Allah, or are you merely chasing after whatever particular pleasure is placed before you, like an animal driven by instinct? Commit to one concrete action today that shifts your pursuit from the immediate to the eternal.
?
If animals cannot conceive of universal judgments, then your ability to understand that 'every sin displeases Allah' is a gift that sets you apart. When you sin, you are knowingly violating a universal principle. How does this realization make you accountable in a way that an animal is not? Write down one sin you are struggling with and determine how you will repent and renew your intention based on this higher knowledge.
?
The lesson ends by saying that judgment about such matters belongs only to Allah. Do you ever judge others (or yourself) as if you have complete knowledge of their inner states? Recognize one person you have judged unfairly and resolve to ask Allah for forgiveness and to treat them with mercy, knowing that final judgment belongs to Him alone.

Third Argument: Physical Pleasures Make Humans Inferior to Animals
Third argument refuting physical pleasures as true happiness by animal comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a reductio ad absurdum argument demonstrates that physical pleasures cannot be the ultimate happiness.

Core Idea
If physical pleasures were happiness, humans would be worse than animals.

Imam al-Rāzī presents a third argument that undermines the claim that physical pleasures constitute the supreme good. He uses a clever comparison with animals to show the absurd consequences of such a view. This argument highlights the role of intellect as a restraining force, which actually diminishes human enjoyment of base pleasures. Prepare to follow a logical chain that leads to a self-evident conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the argument unfolds in a logical sequence.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the sentence starting with 'If these bodily pleasures were the greatest happiness...' and understand its consequence.
This is the key logical move.
3
Understand why the consequent is false
The passage argues that humanity is not baser than animals; this is intuitively obvious. Ask yourself: why does the author add the point about intellect?
Intellect (ʻaql) is a restraint, making humans worse off if physical pleasures were the goal.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the consequent is false, the antecedent (physical pleasures = happiness) must be false. This refutes the idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن الإنسان يشاركه في [لذة] الأكل والشرب ، جميع الحيوانات الخسيسة . فإنه كما أن الإنسان يلتذ بأكل السكر ، فكذلك يلتذ الجعل بأكل السرجين " فلو كانت هذه اللذات البدنية ، هي السعادة الكبرى للإنسان ، لوجب أن لا يكون للإنسان فضيلة على هذه الحيوانات [الخسيسة ، بل نزيد فنقول : لو كانت سعادة الإنسان متعلقة بهذه اللذات الحسية ، لوجب أن يكون الإنسان أخس الحيوانات ] والتالي باطل ، فالمقدم مثله . وبيان وجه الملازمة : أن الحيوانات الخسيسة مشاركة للإنسان في هذه اللذات الحسية البدنية ، إلا أن الإنسان تنتقص عليه هذه المطالب بسبب العقل . فإن العقل سمي عقلا ، لكونه عقالاً له ، وحبساً له عن أكثر ما يشتهيه ، ويميل طبعه إليه. فإذا كان التقدير أن كمال السعادة ليس إلا في هذه اللذات الحسية . ثم بينا : أن هذه اللذات الحسية حاصلة على سبيل التمام [والكمال] للبهائم والسباع من غير أن يكون الإنسان أخس الحيوانات . ولما كان هذا معلوم الفساد بالبديهة ، ثبت : أن هذه اللذات الحسية ، ليست موجبة للبهجة والسعادة .
The third argument: Man shares with all base animals in the pleasure of eating and drinking. For just as man delights in eating sugar, so the dung beetle delights in eating dung. So if these bodily pleasures were the greatest happiness for man, it would be necessary that man have no virtue over these animals. Rather, we add and say: If man's happiness were tied to these physical pleasures, then man would have to be the basest of animals. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. The explanation of the connection: Base animals share with man in these physical, bodily pleasures, except that man is diminished in these pursuits because of intellect. For the intellect is called 'aql because it is a restraint (iqāl) upon him, and a confinement from most of what he desires and his nature inclines to. So if it is supposed that the perfection of happiness lies only in these physical pleasures, then we have shown that these physical pleasures are possessed completely and perfectly by beasts and predators, without man being the basest of animals. And since this is known to be false by intuition, it is established that these physical pleasures do not bring delight and happiness.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-ḥujja ath-thālitha) against the claim that physical pleasures constitute true happiness. It proceeds by reductio ad absurdum. The author first states the premise: base animals (like the dung beetle) share with humans in the pleasure of eating and drinking. The dung beetle delights in dung just as humans delight in sugar. If these bodily pleasures were the greatest happiness, then humans would have no superiority over these animals. In fact, the author adds, if happiness were tied to these physical pleasures, humans would be the basest of animals. Why? Because humans have an intellect (ʿaql) which restrains them from indulging fully in such pleasures. The intellect is called 'aql because it is a restraint (ʿiqāl) and a confinement. Animals, lacking this restraint, indulge completely. Therefore, if happiness were solely in these pleasures, humans would be worse off than animals. Since it is self-evidently false that humans are the basest of animals, the original premise that physical pleasures are the greatest happiness is false.

Misconception: One might think that because humans enjoy more complex pleasures, they are superior; but the argument shows that if the simple pleasures were the only happiness, humans would be inferior due to intellectual restraint.
Correction: The argument does not deny that humans have higher pleasures; it only shows that if physical pleasures were the ultimate happiness, humans would be worse off than animals because intellect curtails indulgence.

Checkpoint

C) Animals would be able to achieve happiness as well.

Key Takeaway
Physical pleasures cannot be true happiness because if they were, humans would be inferior to animals due to the restraint of intellect.

?
The dung beetle delights in dung without shame. Are there 'dung-like' pleasures in your life that your intellect warns against but you still pursue? Will you let your 'aql truly restrain you starting today?
?
If you were to die today, would your life look more like the unrestrained pursuit of physical pleasures or the disciplined pursuit of higher goals? What one change would align your daily choices with the happiness that befits a human being?

Fourth Argument: Physical Pleasures Are Merely Removal of Pain
Fourth argument shows physical pleasures are not positive enjoyments but relief from pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that physical pleasures are essentially the removal of pain, and why they are not considered true happiness or perfection.

Core Idea
Physical pleasures are not positive gains but relief from underlying pain.

In this passage, the author presents the fourth argument to demonstrate that physical pleasures are not genuine sources of happiness. By analyzing common experiences like eating when hungry, intercourse after abstinence, and wearing clothes, he shows that these pleasures are merely the removal of pain. This lesson guides you through the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the full argument unfold without analysis.
The passage is a continuous argument with examples.
2
Identify the main claim and evidence
Find the central claim that physical pleasures are only removal of pain. Then identify the three examples: hunger-eating, intercourse after abstinence, and wearing clothes.
Each example follows the same pattern: prior pain → pleasure as relief.
3
Understand the reasoning
Notice how each example demonstrates that pleasure is proportional to prior pain. The author concludes that the pleasure is just the cessation of pain.
The key is that the 'pleasure' is not a positive addition but a return to neutral.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The argument ends by stating that since these pleasures are just removal of pain, they cannot be happiness or perfection. The prior pain is a positive state, and the pleasure is merely absence.
This connects to the broader theme of true happiness being independent of physical states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن هذه اللذات الحسية، إذا بحث عنها ، فهي في الحقيقة ليست لذات . بل حاصلها يرجع إلى دفع الآلام والدليل عليه : أن الإنسان كلما كان أكثر جوعاً ، كان التذاذه بالأكل أكمل ، وكلما كان الجوع أقل، كان التذاذه بالأكل أقل . وأيضاً : إذا طال عهد الإنسان بالوقاع، اجتمع المني الكثير في أوعية المني ، فحصلت في تلك الأوعية دغدغة شديدة ، وتمدد وثقل ، وكلما كانت هذه الأحوال المؤذية أكثر ، كانت اللذة الحاصلة عند اندفاع ذلك المني أقوى . ولهذا السبب فإن لذة الوقاع في حق من طال عهده الوقاع ، تكون أكمل منها في حق من قرب عهده به . فثبت : أن هذه الأحوال ، التي يظن أنها لذات جسمانية ، فهي في الحقيقة ليست إلا دفع الآلام . وهكذا القول في اللذة الحاصلة بسبب لبس الثياب فإنه لا حاصل لتلك اللذة ، إلا دفع ألم الحر والبرد . وإذا ثبت أنه لا حاصل لهذه اللذات ، إلا دفع الآلام . فنقول : ظهر أنه ليس فيها سعادة . لأن الحالة السابقة هي حصول الألم ، والحالة الحاضرة عدم الألم . وهذا العدم كان حاصلاً عند العدم الأصلي . فثبت : أن هذه الأحوال ليست سعادات ولا كمالات البتة .
The fourth argument: These physical pleasures, if examined, are in reality not pleasures. Rather, their essence reduces to the removal of pains. The evidence for this is that the hungrier a person is, the more complete his enjoyment of eating; and the less hungry, the less his enjoyment of eating. Also, if the time between intercourses is long for a person, much semen collects in the seminal vessels, and there occurs in those vessels a strong tickling, stretching, and heaviness; and the more these painful conditions are, the stronger the pleasure upon the discharge of that semen. For this reason, the pleasure of intercourse for one who has been long without it is more complete than for one who has recently had it. Thus it is established that these states, which are thought to be physical pleasures, are in reality nothing but the removal of pains. The same applies to the pleasure obtained from wearing clothes: that pleasure has no result except the removal of the pain of heat and cold. And since it is established that these pleasures have no result except the removal of pains, we say: it is clear that there is no happiness in them, because the preceding state is the occurrence of pain, and the present state is the absence of pain. And this absence existed in the original state of non-existence. So it is established that these states are neither happiness nor perfections at all.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-ḥujja ar-rābiʿa), which analyzes the nature of physical pleasures and concludes that they are not positive enjoyments but merely the removal of pain. The author provides empirical evidence: the pleasure of eating is directly proportional to the intensity of hunger. Similarly, the pleasure of intercourse is stronger after prolonged abstinence because the accumulation of semen causes physical discomfort (tickling, stretching, heaviness), and the relief of that discomfort is experienced as pleasure. The same logic applies to the pleasure of wearing clothes, which is simply the removal of the pain from heat or cold. Since these pleasures are nothing but the cessation of pain, they cannot be true happiness. The author reasons that the state of happiness should be a positive perfection; but here, the positive experience is the pain, and the pleasure is merely the return to a neutral state—a state of absence (ʿadam) that existed before birth (al-ʿadam al-aṣlī). Therefore, these physical states are neither genuine happiness nor perfection.

اللذات الحسية
دفع الآلام
الجوع
الوقاع
العدم الأصلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Physical pleasures are positive enjoyments independent of any prior pain.
Correction: The argument shows that physical pleasures are contingent on prior pain and are essentially just the removal of that pain, not positive additions.

Checkpoint

A) Physical pleasures are all pains.
B) Physical pleasures are actually just the removal of underlying pains.
C) Physical pleasures are imaginary.
D) Physical pleasures are only found in eating and drinking.

Key Takeaway
Physical pleasures, as analyzed in this argument, are merely the removal of pain and therefore do not constitute true happiness or perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The source says that these pleasures are 'neither happiness nor perfections at all.' If you truly believed that, would it affect how you spend your time, money, and energy? What is one physical pleasure you could try to see differently starting today?

The Fifth Argument: Humanity Limits Animal Pleasures
Fifth argument: human specific difference (insaniyya) reduces animal pleasures, proving they are not happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against identifying happiness with animal pleasures, based on the limiting effect of humanity (insaniyya).

Core Idea
Humanity limits animal pleasures, so they cannot be true happiness.

This fifth argument from al-Matalib al-Aliya focuses on a specific feature of human nature: the quality that distinguishes us from animals actually reduces the perfection of physical pleasures. The author argues that if those pleasures were happiness itself, then being human would be a disadvantage—a conclusion that intuition rejects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement (if X then Y) followed by an appeal to intuition.
The passage contains a possible OCR error in the word 'يؤذي' (harms) - the correct meaning may be 'copulates'.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the shared animal activities (eating, drinking, etc.) and the distinguishing human quality (insaniyya). Understand how insaniyya limits those activities.
Insaniyya is the specific difference of humans – it prevents perfection of animal pleasures.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the conditional: if animal pleasures were happiness, then humans would be deficient. Since intuition (badīha) judges the consequent false, the antecedent (that those pleasures are happiness) is false.
This is a modus tollens argument: ¬Q → ¬P.
4
Reflect on the role of intuition
The argument relies on immediate intuitive knowledge. Consider why the author thinks this is self-evident.
Intuition here is a rational faculty that recognizes basic truths without proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الإنسان من حيث يأكل ويشرب ويجامع ويؤذي ، يشارك سائر الحيوانات . وإنما يمتاز عنها بالإنسانية ، وهي مانعة من تكميل تلك الأحوال ، وموجبة لنقصانها ولتقليلها . فلو كانت هذه الأحوال عين السعادة لكان الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان ، ناقصاً شقياً خسيساً . ولما حكمت البديهة يفاد هذا التالي ، ثبت فساد المقدم .
The fifth argument: Man, insofar as he eats, drinks, copulates, and harms, shares with all animals. He is only distinguished from them by humanity (al-insāniyya), which prevents the perfection of those states and causes their deficiency and reduction. So if these states were happiness itself, then man, insofar as he is human, would be deficient, wretched, and base. And since intuition judged the falsity of this consequent, the antecedent is proven false.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-ḥujja al-khāmis), which focuses on the specific human quality (al-insāniyya). Humans share with animals the activities of eating, drinking, and copulating. The text includes the word 'yūdhī' (harms), though this is possibly a corruption. The key point is that what distinguishes humans—their humanity—actually prevents the perfection of these animal states and causes them to be deficient and diminished. If these animal states were identical to happiness, then the very quality that makes humans human would render them deficient, wretched, and base. Since intuition (badīha) immediately recognizes that humans are not inherently wretched by virtue of being human, the premise that these animal pleasures constitute happiness is false.

Key Terms

الإنسانية
السعادة

Correction: The argument states the opposite: humanity (insaniyya) prevents the perfection of animal pleasures and causes them to be deficient and reduced. Thus, if those pleasures were happiness, humanity would be a disadvantage.

Key Takeaway

The Fourth Argument: Sensory Pleasures Are Only the Removal of Pain
Razi argues that sensory pleasures are merely the removal of pain, not true pleasures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that sensory pleasures are not true pleasures but merely the removal of pain, and why this undermines the claim that happiness consists in sensory pleasures.

Core Idea
Sensory pleasures are just pain relief, not real happiness.

Razi presents a fourth proof against the idea that happiness lies in sensory pleasures. He argues that when we examine what we call pleasures, they are actually only the removal of prior pains. This short passage is a powerful reductio ad absurdum that challenges our intuitions about pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: claim, evidence, conclusion.
Razi uses a very compact argument style.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi claims that sensory pleasures are not real pleasures but merely the repulsion of pains (دفع الآلام). This is the thesis of the argument.
The claim is stated in the first sentence.
3
Examine the evidence
Razi provides three examples: hunger and eating, sexual abstinence, and wearing clothes. For each, note how the pleasure is proportional to the preceding pain.
The examples are empirical observations.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that since these states are only the absence of pain, they were present in original non-existence, and thus cannot be happiness or perfection.
The conclusion is that sensory pleasures are not genuine sa‘ādah.
5
Reflect on the implication
If pleasure is just removal of pain, then it is not a positive acquisition. This challenges the idea that happiness can be achieved through sensory experiences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن هذه اللذات الحسية، إذا بحث عنها ، فهي في الحقيقة ليست لذات . بل حاصلها يرجع إلى دفع الآلام والدليل عليه : أن الإنسان كلما كان أكثر جوعاً ، كان التذاذه بالأكل أكمل ، وكلما كان الجوع أقل، كان التذاذه بالأكل أقل . وأيضاً : إذا طال عهد الإنسان بالوقاع، اجتمع المني الكثير في أوعية المني ، فحصلت في تلك الأوعية دغدغة شديدة ، وتمدد وثقل ، وكلما كانت هذه الأحوال المؤذية أكثر ، كانت اللذة الحاصلة عند اندفاع ذلك المني أقوى . ولهذا السبب فإن لذة الوقاع في حق من طال عهده الوقاع ، تكون أكمل منها في حق من قرب عهده به . فثبت : أن هذه الأحوال ، التي يظن أنها لذات جسمانية ، فهي في الحقيقة ليست إلا دفع الآلام . وهكذا القول في اللذة الحاصلة بسبب لبس الثياب فإنه لا حاصل لتلك اللذة ، إلا دفع ألم الحر والبرد . وإذا ثبت أنه لا حاصل لهذه اللذات ، إلا دفع الآلام . فنقول : ظهر أنه ليس فيها سعادة . لأن الحالة السابقة هي حصول الألم ، والحالة الحاضرة عدم الألم . وهذا العدم كان حاصلاً عند العدم الأصلي . فثبت : أن هذه الأحوال ليست سعادات ولا كمالات البتة .
The fourth proof: These sensory pleasures, if investigated, are in reality not pleasures. Rather, their outcome reduces to the repulsion of pains. The evidence for it is that the hungrier a person is, the more complete his enjoyment of eating, and the less the hunger, the less his enjoyment of eating. Also, if a person's period of abstinence from intercourse (al-waqā‘) is long, much semen collects in the seminal vessels, so a severe tickling, stretching, and heaviness occur in those vessels, and the more these harmful states are, the stronger the pleasure resulting from the discharge of that semen. For this reason, the pleasure of intercourse in the case of one who has been long abstinent is more complete than in the case of one whose abstinence has been short. So it is established that these states, which are thought to be bodily pleasures, are in reality nothing but the repulsion of pains. And likewise the statement concerning the pleasure arising from wearing clothes: there is no outcome for that pleasure except the repulsion of the pain of heat and cold. And when it is established that there is no outcome for these pleasures except the repulsion of pains, we say: it appears that there is no happiness (sa‘ādah) in them, because the preceding state is the occurrence of pain, and the present state is the absence of pain. And this absence was present in the original non-existence. So it is established that these states are not happinesses nor perfections at all.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against the claim that happiness consists in sensory pleasures. It is a reductio ad absurdum argument showing that so-called sensory pleasures are not genuine pleasures but merely the removal of pre-existing pains. The proof begins by asserting that when examined, sensory pleasures are not real pleasures but rather their outcome returns to the repulsion of pains. The evidence: the more hungry a person is, the more they enjoy eating; the less hungry, the less enjoyment. Similarly for sexual pleasure: after a long period of abstinence, the pleasure of intercourse is stronger because of the accumulated tension. Thus the pleasure is not a separate acquisition but the relief of the preceding pain. The same applies to wearing clothes: it only relieves the pain of heat or cold. Since all these 'pleasures' are nothing but relief from pain, they cannot be true happiness (sa‘ādah) or perfection, because the state of pain is absent and that absence existed already in the original non-existence. Hence they are not genuine perfections.

لذة
دفع الآلام
سعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pleasure is a positive addition, but the argument shows it is merely the removal of a prior pain.
Correction: Pleasure is not an independent acquisition; it is contingent on preceding pain.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what is the reality of sensory pleasures?
A) They are independent acquisitions.
B) They are the removal of pains.
C) They are illusions.
D) They are bodily perfections.

Key Takeaway
Sensory pleasures are not genuine happiness because they are only the removal of pain, and such removal is not a positive perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you next feel pleasure from eating, will you pause to realize that it is only relief from hunger? How does this change what you seek in life beyond temporary reliefs?
?
Think of a desire you currently chase. If you strip away the preceding discomfort, what remains? Are you willing to admit that the pleasure is just the absence of pain, and not a positive gain?
?
If sensory pleasures are not happiness, then what are you truly living for? What must you change today to pursue real happiness instead of mere pain relief?

The Fifth Argument: Humanity Restricts Pleasures
Razi argues humanity restricts pleasures, so they aren't happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth argument that humanity distinguishes us from animals precisely by diminishing sensory pleasures, proving that such pleasures cannot be true happiness.

Core Idea
Humanity restricts pleasures — so pleasures cannot be our ultimate happiness.

Razi presents a unique argument: what makes us human actually limits our animal pleasures. If those pleasures were happiness, then being human would make us worse off, which is absurd. This passage challenges the idea that sensory pleasures are the ultimate goal by turning our distinctiveness against itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the if-then structure and the role of intuition in judging the consequent false.
The Arabic has some unclear words ('يؤذي', 'يفاد') but the overall logic is clear.
2
Identify the two key premises
Identify that humans share eating, drinking, etc. with animals, but humanity itself prevents the completion of those states. Understand how these two premises set up the argument.
Humanity here is the distinguishing feature, but it acts as a barrier to full sensory pleasure.
3
Understand the conditional argument
Trace the hypothetical: 'If these states were happiness, then humanity would make us deficient and wretched.' Recognize that the consequent is absurd, so the antecedent (the states being happiness) must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الإنسان من حيث يأكل ويشرب ويجامع ويؤذي ، يشارك سائر الحيوانات . وإنما يمتاز عنها بالإنسانية ، وهي مانعة من تكميل تلك الأحوال ، وموجبة لنقصانها ولتقليلها . فلو كانت هذه الأحوال عين السعادة لكان الإنسان من حيث إنه إنسان ، ناقصاً شقياً خسيساً . ولما حكمت البديهة يفاد هذا التالي ، ثبت فساد المقدم .
The fifth proof: The human being, inasmuch as he eats, drinks, engages in intercourse, and harms [؟], shares with the rest of the animals. He is distinguished from them only by humanity (al-insāniyyah), and it prevents the completion of those states and necessitates their deficiency and reduction. So if these states were the very happiness (sa‘ādah), then the human being insofar as he is human would be deficient, wretched, and base. And since intuition (al-badīhah) [؟] judges the falsity of this consequent, the antecedent is proved false.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof. It argues that humans share the activities of eating, drinking, and intercourse with animals. What distinguishes humans is their humanity (al-insāniyyah), which actually prevents the full pursuit of these pleasures and reduces them. Therefore, if these pleasures were happiness itself, then being human would make one deficient in happiness, which is absurd. Since common sense (al-badīhah) rejects that a human is worse off, the premise that these pleasures are happiness is false.

Key Terms
إنسانية (humanity)
بديهة (intuition)
سعادة (happiness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that humanity enhances sensory pleasures, making them more refined or complete.
Correction: Razi argues the opposite: humanity actually prevents the full pursuit of these pleasures and reduces them, so they cannot be the essence of happiness.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth argument claim about humanity in relation to sensory pleasures?
A) It perfects them.
B) It is neutral.
C) It prevents their completion and reduces them.
D) It creates new pleasures.

Key Takeaway
Razi uses humanity's restrictive nature to prove that sensory pleasures cannot be true happiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a pleasure you have restrained yourself from because it would be 'beneath' you or because your humanity pulled you back. Did you see that restraint as a loss or a gain? How does Razi's argument challenge you to reconsider what you truly seek in moments of desire? Will you now see that restriction as a sign of your humanity pointing you to a higher happiness?
?
If animals could pursue pleasures without restraint and you cannot, doesn't that sting your pride? Where in your life do you most feel the tension between your desires and your humanity—your conscience, your dignity, your faith? What does that tension tell you about where your ultimate happiness must lie?
?
Razi calls humanity 'manee' (preventer) and 'moojib li naqsan' (cause of deficiency) regarding pleasures. How does it feel to hear that being human makes you deficient in something that animals enjoy fully? Instead of resenting this, can you embrace it as a clue that your purpose is not pleasure but something far greater? What one pleasure will you intentionally restrict today to remind yourself of this truth?
