The Sixth Proof: Angels vs. Animals on True Happiness
Razi argues by analogy with angels who have superior happiness without sensory pleasures, proving true happiness is not sensory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's sixth proof that true happiness is not sensory pleasures, using the analogy of angels and animals and the objection about divine imitation.

Core Idea
If sensory pleasures were happiness, animals would be better than angels—absurd!

Razi presents a powerful argument by comparison. He observes that angels, despite lacking sensory pleasures, are superior to animals that enjoy them. This shows that true happiness must be something beyond mere physical sensations. The argument also addresses an objection about divine perfection being unattainable for creatures, using the concept of imitating God's attributes. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the comparison
Notice that Razi starts by stating a necessary truth: angels' happiness is more complete and noble than that of base animals like donkeys and insects. This is the foundation of the argument.
This knowledge is considered 'necessary' (بديهي) by Razi.
2
Identify the logical consequence
Angels lack sensory pleasures (eating, drinking, intercourse), while base animals have them. If ultimate happiness were only these pleasures, then animals would be higher than angels. Since that is false, the premise must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Consider the stronger point
Razi adds that God's perfection is incomparably greater, yet He is free from these base pleasures. This reinforces that perfection can exist without sensory pleasures.
4
Examine the objection and reply
An objector might say God's perfection is due to divinity, which is impossible for creatures. Razi replies that the Prophet said 'Adopt the character traits of God' and philosophers spoke of imitation of the divine—meaning reducing needs and doing good, not indulging in pleasures.
This shows that creatures can attain a form of perfection without sensory pleasures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن العلم الضروري حاصل بأن بهجة الملائكة وسعاداتها أكمل وأشرف من بهجة الحمار وسعادته . ومن بهجة الديدان والذبان وسائر الحشرات . ثم لا نزاع : أن الملائكة ليس لها لذة الأكل والشرب والوقاع . وهذه الحيوانات الخسيسة حصلت لها هذه اللذات . فلو كانت السعادة القصوى ، ليست إلا هذه اللذات، لزم كون هذه الحيوانات الخسيسة أعلى حالاً ، وأكمل درجة من الملائكة المقربين . ولما كان هذا التالي باطلاً ، كان المقدم مثله . بل ههنا ما هو أعلى وأقوى مما ذكرنا . وهو أنه لا نسبة لكمال واجب الوجود وجلاله وشرفه وعزته إلى أحوال غيره ، مع أن هذه اللذات الخسيسة ممتنعة عليه . فثبت : إن الكمال والشرف قد يحصلان بأحوال سوى هذه اللذات الجسمية . فإن قالوا : ذلك الكمال ، لأجل حصول الإلهية . وذلك في حق الخلق محال ، فنقول : لا نزاع : في أن حصول الإلهية في حق الخلق محال ، إلا أنه عليه السلام قال : « وتخلقوا بأخلاق الله » ، والفلاسفة قالوا : « الفلسفة عبارة عن التشبه بالإله ، بقدر الطاقة البشرية » ، فيجب أن نعرف تفسير هذا التخلق ، وهذا التشبه . ومعلوم : أنه لا معنى لهما إلا تقليل الحاجات ، وإفاضة الخيرات والحسنات . لا بالاستكثار من الشهوات واللذات .
The sixth proof: Necessary knowledge is obtained that the joy and happiness of the angels are more complete and more noble than the joy and happiness of the donkey, and than the joy of worms, flies, and the rest of the insects. Then there is no dispute that angels have no pleasure of eating, drinking, or intercourse. And these base animals have these pleasures. So if the ultimate happiness were nothing but these pleasures, it would necessarily follow that these base animals are in a higher state and more complete in degree than the angels brought near [to God]. And since this consequent is false, the antecedent is likewise false. Rather, here there is something higher and stronger than what we have mentioned: that there is no comparison between the perfection of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd), His majesty, honor, and might, and the states of others, even though these base pleasures are impossible for Him. So it is established that perfection and honor may be attained through states other than these bodily pleasures. If they say: that perfection is due to the attainment of divinity, and that is impossible for creatures, we say: There is no dispute that the attainment of divinity is impossible for creatures. However, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Adopt the character traits of God' (takhallaqū bi-akhlāq Allāh), and the philosophers said: 'Philosophy is an imitation of the divine according to human capacity.' So we must understand the interpretation of this 'adopting of character' and this 'imitation'. It is known that they mean nothing but the reduction of needs and the outpouring of goods and virtues, not the multiplication of appetites and pleasures.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which is a comparison argument. First, it states as a necessary truth that angels have more complete and noble happiness than donkeys and insects. Then it notes that angels lack sensory pleasures like eating, drinking, and intercourse, while those base animals have them. If happiness were only those sensory pleasures, then base animals would be higher than angels. This is false, so the premise is false. Then a stronger point: the perfection of God (the Necessary Existent) is incomparably greater, and He is free from these base pleasures, so perfection can be without them. Then an objection is anticipated: someone might say that God's perfection is due to divinity, which is impossible for creatures. The answer is that the Prophet said 'Adopt the character traits of God' and the philosophers defined philosophy as imitation of God. So there is a kind of perfection for creatures through reducing needs and doing good, not through indulgence in pleasures.

Key Terms
ملائكة (angels)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existent)
تخلق بأخلاق الله (adopting God's character traits)
تشبه بالإله (imitation of the divine)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the superiority of angels is due to their having different pleasures, but the argument highlights that they lack sensory pleasures yet are superior.
Correction: The argument shows that angels have no sensory pleasures at all, yet they are superior, proving that true happiness is not sensory.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the sixth argument?
A) Angels have pleasures unknown to humans.
B) Sensory pleasures are the only pleasures.
C) If sensory pleasures were true happiness, then animals would be superior to angels.
D) Humans cannot attain angelic happiness.

Key Takeaway
The ultimate happiness is not found in sensory pleasures but in higher states of being, as exemplified by angels and ultimately by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the angels, who lack all sensory pleasures, are far happier than animals, then why do you spend so much of your energy chasing food, entertainment, and comfort? When was the last time you deliberately chose to reduce a desire because you believed it would bring you closer to true happiness?
?
Razi says the perfection of creatures lies in 'reducing needs and outpouring goods and virtues.' Look at your last week: did your actions tilt more toward reducing your own needs or toward fulfilling them? Which specific virtue did you pour out for others without expecting anything back?
?
The Prophet said 'Adopt the character traits of God.' This is not a suggestion; it is a command. Which one of God's attributes—such as generosity, mercy, or independence from need—are you most failing to imitate? What is the first step you will take today to change that?

The Seventh Argument: Common Judgments Condemn Sensory Pleasures
Razi presents the seventh proof, showing that even materialists admire ascetics and condemn hedonists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses people's own spontaneous judgments about ascetics and hedonists to argue that sensory pleasures are not true happiness.

Core Idea
People's admiration of ascetics proves they know pleasures are not the highest good.

In this argument, Razi appeals to everyday human reactions. Even those who claim happiness is only in bodily pleasures cannot help but admire someone who abstains from them. This contradiction reveals a deeper truth: true perfection lies beyond sensory gratification. The passage presents this phenomenon and uses it to challenge the materialist view of happiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Let the description of people's reactions sink in.
Notice that the passage cuts off due to page boundary.
2
Spot the two contrasting cases
Identify the two scenarios: the admired ascetic and the despised hedonist. Look for specific keywords like 'صوم' (fasting) and 'بهيمية' (brutishness).
3
Understand the logical contradiction
Ask yourself: If these people truly believed happiness is only in pleasures, why would they admire the ascetic and condemn the pleasure-seeker? The contradiction is central to the argument.
The argument works because their reaction is involuntary and universal.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even though the passage is incomplete, the intended conclusion is clear: their own judgments show they consider renunciation as perfection and pursuit of pleasures as deficiency.
You can infer the missing conclusion from the setup.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن هؤلاء الذين حكموا بأن سعادة الإنسان ، ليست إلا في تحصيل هذه اللذات البدنية ، والراحات الجسمية . إذا رأوا إنساناً أعرض عن طلبها ، مثل أن يكون مواظباً على الصوم . مكتفياً بما جادت الأرض به ، عظم اعتقادهم فيه . وزعموا : أنه ليس من جنس البشر بل هو من زمرة الملائكة . ويعدون أنفسهم بالنسبة إليه أشقياء أراذل . وإذا رأوا إنساناً مستغرق الفكر والهمة في طلب الأكل والشرب والوقاع ، مصروف الهمة إلى تحصيل أسباب هذه الأحوال ، معرضاً عن العلم والزهد والعبادة ، قضوا عليه بالبهيمية والخزي والنكال . فلو أنه تقرر في عقولهم : أن الاشتغال بتحصيل هذه اللذات الجسدانية نقص ودناءة ، وأن الترفع عن الالتفات إليها كمال وسعادة . وإلا لما كان الأمر على ما ذكرناه ، ولكان يجب أن يحكموا على المعرض عن
The seventh proof: Those who have judged that human happiness is only in the acquisition of these bodily pleasures and physical comforts, when they see a person who turns away from seeking them, such as one who is constant in fasting, content with what the earth provides, their estimation of him becomes great. They claim that he is not of the human species but rather of the company of angels. And they consider themselves in relation to him as wretched and base. And when they see a person whose thought and ambition are absorbed in seeking food, drink, and intercourse, whose efforts are directed to acquiring the causes of these states, turning away from knowledge, asceticism, and worship, they judge him as brutish, disgraceful, and ignominious. For if it were not established in their minds that occupying oneself with acquiring these bodily pleasures is deficiency and baseness, and that rising above attending to them is perfection and happiness, then the matter would not be as we have described, and they would have to judge the one who turns away from...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which appeals to common human judgment. It describes how even those who claim happiness is only in bodily pleasures nonetheless admire someone who abstains from them—like a person who fasts and lives simply. They consider such a person almost angelic and themselves as wretched. Conversely, they condemn someone absorbed in food, drink, and intercourse, judging him as brutish. This shows that deep down they recognize that pursuing sensory pleasures is a deficiency and that rising above them is perfection. The argument is that their own spontaneous reaction contradicts their stated position. The passage cuts off, but the implication is clear: if they truly believed pleasure was the ultimate good, they would not admire the ascetic and despise the hedonist.

زهد (asceticism)

Key Takeaway
Razi's seventh argument uses people's own contradictory judgments to show that sensory pleasures are not the true aim of human happiness.

The Swallow and Swan: Examples of Animal Intelligence
First two examples: swallow and swan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the behaviors of the swallow and swan demonstrate that animals possess reason and understanding.

Core Idea
Animal behaviors often surpass human ability, proving they are not mere instinct.

In this passage, the author presents two striking examples of animal behavior that seem to require intelligence and planning. The swallow skillfully builds its nest and cares for its young, while the swan cunningly lures predators away from its chicks. These actions, the author argues, cannot be attributed to blind instinct alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the swallow and swan examples. Notice the detailed descriptions.
2
Identify the key behaviors
List the specific actions described for each animal that show purposeful behavior.
For the swallow: building nest, adapting to lack of clay, teaching chicks, tying legs.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author thinks these behaviors demonstrate understanding. What would instinct alone produce?
The author implies that such complex, adaptive actions require reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن الخطاف، صناع حسن في إيجاد العش لنفسه من الطين ، وقطع الخشب ، فإذا أعوزه الطين ، ابتل وتمرغ في التراب ، لتحمل جناحاه قدراً من المطين ، وإذا أفرخ بالغ في تعهد الفراخ ، ويأخذ ذرقها بمنقاره ، ويرميه عن العش ، ثم يعلمها إلقاء الذرق بالتولية نحو طرف العش [ وترابط أرجلها في العش بالشعور ما دامت صغاراً ، حتى لا يتحرك فتسقط من العش ] . الحادي عشر : إذا قرب الصائد من مكان فراخ البجعة ظهرت له البجعة ، وقربت منه ، تطمعة ، لأجل أن يتبعها ثم تذهب إلى جانب آخر سوی جانب فراخها . الثاني عشر : ناقر الخشب . قل ما يجلس على الأرض ، بل يجلس على الشجر ، وينقر الموضع الذي يعلم أن فيه دوداً . الثالث عشر : الغرانيق . تصعد في الجو جداً عند الطيران . فإن حصل ضباب أو سحاب يحجب بعضها عن بعض ، أحدثت عن أجنحتها حفيفاً مسموعاً ، ويصير ذلك الصوت سبباً لاجتماعها ، وعدم تفرقها ، وإذا نامت نامت على فرد رجل ، وقد اضطبعت الرؤوس ، إلا القائد فإنه ينام مكشوف الرأس ، فيسرع انتباهه . وإذا أحس أحدها بـآفة أو صوت ، صاح منبهاً الباقين . الرابع عشر : النعامة . إذا اجتمع لها من بيضها عشرون أو ثلاثون . قسمتها إلى ثلاثة أثلاث ، فتدفن ثلثاً منها في التراب ، وثلثاً تتركه في الشمس ، وثلثاً تحتضنه . فإذا خرجت الفراريج ، كسرت ما كان في الشمس ، وسقت تلك الفراريج ما فيها من الرطوبات التي ذوبتها الشمس ، ورققتها ، فإذا قويت تلك الفراريج ، أخرجت الثلث الثالث الذي دفنته في الأرض [ وثقبتها ] وقد اجتمع فيه النمل والذباب والديدان والحشرات ، فتجعل تلك الأشياء طعمة لتلك الفراريج ، فإذا تم ذلك ، صارت تلك الفراريج ، قادرة على الرعي والطلب . ولا شك أن هذا الطريق حيلة عجيبة في تربية الأولاد . ولنكتف من هذا النوع على هذا القدر الذي ذكرنا . فإن الاستقصاء فيه مذكور في كتاب « الحيوان ) . وقد ظهر منها أن هذه الحيوانات قد تأتي بأفعال يعجز أكثر الأذكياء من الناس . ولو لم تكن عاقلة فاهمة ، وإلا لما صح منها شيء من ذلك . فهذا ما يتعلق بالعقل
The tenth: Indeed the swallow, it skillfully builds a nest for itself from clay and pieces of wood. If clay is unavailable to it, it wets itself and rolls in dust so that its wings carry a quantity of mud. When it hatches, it intensively cares for the chicks, taking their droppings with its beak and throwing them out of the nest, then teaches them to discard droppings by turning toward the edge of the nest [and it ties their legs in the nest with hairs as long as they are small, so that they do not move and fall from the nest]. The eleventh: When the hunter approaches the place of the cygnets, the swan appears to him and draws near to him, enticing him to follow it, then goes to another side away from the side of its chicks. The twelfth: The woodpecker. Rarely does it sit on the ground; rather it sits on trees and pecks at the spot where it knows there is a worm. The thirteenth: The cranes. They ascend very high in the air when flying. If fog or clouds occur that hide some from others, they produce a audible rustling from their wings, and that sound becomes a cause for their gathering and not separating. When they sleep, they sleep on one leg, with their heads tucked under wings, except the leader, for it sleeps with its head uncovered, so it wakes quickly. If one of them senses a calamity or sound, it cries out alerting the others. The fourteenth: The ostrich. When it has twenty or thirty eggs, it divides them into three thirds: it buries a third in the soil, leaves a third in the sun, and incubates a third. When the chicks come out, they break what was in the sun, and those chicks drink the moisture that the sun has melted and thinned. When those chicks become strong, they bring out the third that was buried in the ground [and they pierce it]. In that (ground) there have gathered ants, flies, worms, and insects, and it makes those things food for those chicks. When that is done, those chicks become able to graze and seek food. There is no doubt that this method is a wonderful trick in raising offspring. We will be content with this amount from this type that we have mentioned, for the exhaustive treatment of it is mentioned in the book 'Al-Hayawan'. And it has become evident from this that these animals perform actions that most intelligent people are incapable of. If they were not rational and understanding, then none of that would be possible from them. So this is what pertains to reason.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument (العقل) for the intelligence of animals. It enumerates five examples (from the tenth to the fourteenth) of remarkable animal behaviors that seem to require understanding and planning. The swallow builds a nest with skill, adapts to lack of clay, cares for its young, and teaches them to discard droppings while tying their legs for safety. The swan lures predators away from its chicks. The woodpecker searches for worms deliberately. Cranes coordinate flight and sleep in an organized manner with sentinel duties. The ostrich divides its eggs, uses the sun to prepare food for chicks, and later uses insects as feed. The author concludes that these actions are so extraordinary that even most intelligent humans could not perform them, thus the animals must be rational (عاقلة فاهمة). The passage then states that this concludes the rational proof.

Key Terms
الخطاف
البجعة
عاقلة فاهمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals always act purely by instinct without any capacity for understanding.
Correction: This passage shows animals like the swallow and swan performing complex, adaptive behaviors that require understanding, such as tool use, teaching, and deception.

Checkpoint
What does the swallow do to protect its chicks from falling from the nest?
A) It builds a barrier of mud.
B) It ties their legs with hairs.
C) It keeps them in a hidden location.
D) It teaches them to fly immediately.

Key Takeaway
The swallow and swan exhibit behaviors that go beyond simple instinct, indicating they possess a degree of reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The swallow cares for its chicks to the point of tying their legs. When you care for others, do you go beyond the minimum and anticipate their needs, or do you only act when it is convenient? What would change if you took this level of responsibility?
?
The swan sacrifices its own safety to lead the hunter away from its young. Is there someone in your life you are called to protect, even at a cost to yourself? How will you actively shield them this week?
?
The swallow teaches its chicks a practical skill. What are you teaching those who look up to you—by word or by example? If your actions were the only lesson, what would your students learn?

The Woodpecker and Cranes: More Evidence of Animal Reason
Third and fourth examples: woodpecker and cranes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will recognize how the deliberate actions of the woodpecker and the organized behavior of cranes further support the argument for animal reason.

Core Idea
Animals engage in goal-oriented activities that require knowledge and coordination.

The second set of examples features the woodpecker, which deliberately pecks at trees where it knows worms are hidden, and the cranes, which exhibit sophisticated group coordination in flight and sentinel duty during sleep. These actions indicate awareness of cause and effect and social organization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the woodpecker and cranes examples.
2
Compare the two examples
Notice how both examples show animals acting based on knowledge—woodpecker knows where worms are, cranes coordinate.
3
Consider the implication for animal reason
Reflect on why these behaviors cannot be accidental. The author uses them as proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : إن الخطاف، صناع حسن في إيجاد العش لنفسه من الطين ، وقطع الخشب ، فإذا أعوزه الطين ، ابتل وتمرغ في التراب ، لتحمل جناحاه قدراً من المطين ، وإذا أفرخ بالغ في تعهد الفراخ ، ويأخذ ذرقها بمنقاره ، ويرميه عن العش ، ثم يعلمها إلقاء الذرق بالتولية نحو طرف العش [ وترابط أرجلها في العش بالشعور ما دامت صغاراً ، حتى لا يتحرك فتسقط من العش ] . الحادي عشر : إذا قرب الصائد من مكان فراخ البجعة ظهرت له البجعة ، وقربت منه ، تطمعة ، لأجل أن يتبعها ثم تذهب إلى جانب آخر سوی جانب فراخها . الثاني عشر : ناقر الخشب . قل ما يجلس على الأرض ، بل يجلس على الشجر ، وينقر الموضع الذي يعلم أن فيه دوداً . الثالث عشر : الغرانيق . تصعد في الجو جداً عند الطيران . فإن حصل ضباب أو سحاب يحجب بعضها عن بعض ، أحدثت عن أجنحتها حفيفاً مسموعاً ، ويصير ذلك الصوت سبباً لاجتماعها ، وعدم تفرقها ، وإذا نامت نامت على فرد رجل ، وقد اضطبعت الرؤوس ، إلا القائد فإنه ينام مكشوف الرأس ، فيسرع انتباهه . وإذا أحس أحدها بـآفة أو صوت ، صاح منبهاً الباقين . الرابع عشر : النعامة . إذا اجتمع لها من بيضها عشرون أو ثلاثون . قسمتها إلى ثلاثة أثلاث ، فتدفن ثلثاً منها في التراب ، وثلثاً تتركه في الشمس ، وثلثاً تحتضنه . فإذا خرجت الفراريج ، كسرت ما كان في الشمس ، وسقت تلك الفراريج ما فيها من الرطوبات التي ذوبتها الشمس ، ورققتها ، فإذا قويت تلك الفراريج ، أخرجت الثلث الثالث الذي دفنته في الأرض [ وثقبتها ] وقد اجتمع فيه النمل والذباب والديدان والحشرات ، فتجعل تلك الأشياء طعمة لتلك الفراريج ، فإذا تم ذلك ، صارت تلك الفراريج ، قادرة على الرعي والطلب . ولا شك أن هذا الطريق حيلة عجيبة في تربية الأولاد . ولنكتف من هذا النوع على هذا القدر الذي ذكرنا . فإن الاستقصاء فيه مذكور في كتاب « الحيوان ) . وقد ظهر منها أن هذه الحيوانات قد تأتي بأفعال يعجز أكثر الأذكياء من الناس . ولو لم تكن عاقلة فاهمة ، وإلا لما صح منها شيء من ذلك . فهذا ما يتعلق بالعقل
The tenth: Indeed the swallow, it skillfully builds a nest for itself from clay and pieces of wood. If clay is unavailable to it, it wets itself and rolls in dust so that its wings carry a quantity of mud. When it hatches, it intensively cares for the chicks, taking their droppings with its beak and throwing them out of the nest, then teaches them to discard droppings by turning toward the edge of the nest [and it ties their legs in the nest with hairs as long as they are small, so that they do not move and fall from the nest]. The eleventh: When the hunter approaches the place of the cygnets, the swan appears to him and draws near to him, enticing him to follow it, then goes to another side away from the side of its chicks. The twelfth: The woodpecker. Rarely does it sit on the ground; rather it sits on trees and pecks at the spot where it knows there is a worm. The thirteenth: The cranes. They ascend very high in the air when flying. If fog or clouds occur that hide some from others, they produce a audible rustling from their wings, and that sound becomes a cause for their gathering and not separating. When they sleep, they sleep on one leg, with their heads tucked under wings, except the leader, for it sleeps with its head uncovered, so it wakes quickly. If one of them senses a calamity or sound, it cries out alerting the others. The fourteenth: The ostrich. When it has twenty or thirty eggs, it divides them into three thirds: it buries a third in the soil, leaves a third in the sun, and incubates a third. When the chicks come out, they break what was in the sun, and those chicks drink the moisture that the sun has melted and thinned. When those chicks become strong, they bring out the third that was buried in the ground [and they pierce it]. In that (ground) there have gathered ants, flies, worms, and insects, and it makes those things food for those chicks. When that is done, those chicks become able to graze and seek food. There is no doubt that this method is a wonderful trick in raising offspring. We will be content with this amount from this type that we have mentioned, for the exhaustive treatment of it is mentioned in the book 'Al-Hayawan'. And it has become evident from this that these animals perform actions that most intelligent people are incapable of. If they were not rational and understanding, then none of that would be possible from them. So this is what pertains to reason.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument (العقل) for the intelligence of animals. It enumerates five examples (from the tenth to the fourteenth) of remarkable animal behaviors that seem to require understanding and planning. The swallow builds a nest with skill, adapts to lack of clay, cares for its young, and teaches them to discard droppings while tying their legs for safety. The swan lures predators away from its chicks. The woodpecker searches for worms deliberately. Cranes coordinate flight and sleep in an organized manner with sentinel duties. The ostrich divides its eggs, uses the sun to prepare food for chicks, and later uses insects as feed. The author concludes that these actions are so extraordinary that even most intelligent humans could not perform them, thus the animals must be rational (عاقلة فاهمة). The passage then states that this concludes the rational proof.

Key Terms
ناقر الخشب
الغرانيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animal behavior is purely reflexive without any cognitive component.
Correction: The woodpecker's targeted pecking and the cranes' organized sleeping arrangements indicate awareness and intention.

Checkpoint
How do cranes maintain cohesion when fog hides them from each other?
A) They emit high-pitched calls.
B) They produce a rustling sound from their wings.
C) They fly in a tight formation.
D) They return to the ground.

Key Takeaway
The woodpecker and cranes display knowledge and social coordination that go beyond instinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a woodpecker knows precisely where to find its worm, what excuse do you have for ignoring the signs of your Creator that are clearer than a worm in a tree? Will you finally turn to Him with full attention?
?
The cranes coordinate through rustling sounds to stay together in fog. When you face confusion or darkness in your life, do you actively seek the 'rustling' of guidance from the Qur’an and community, or do you drift alone?
?
The leader crane sacrifices its comfort by sleeping with its head uncovered for the safety of the group. In your family or circle, are you carrying that burden of vigilance, or are you content to sleep soundly while others are spiritually unaware?

The Ostrich: A Marvel of Parental Care and the Final Proof
Fifth example: ostrich and concluding argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ostrich's complex egg-rearing strategy provides a powerful conclusion to the argument that animals are rational and understanding.

Core Idea
The ostrich's three-part egg management is a 'wonderful trick' that reveals intelligent design.

The final example is the ostrich, which divides its eggs into three groups with a specific plan for each: one for incubation, one for sun heating, and one for burial. This multi-step strategy shows foresight and resourcefulness. After this example, the author concludes that these animals perform actions that even intelligent humans cannot match, thus proving they possess reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the ostrich example
Read the description of the ostrich's egg division and processing.
2
Analyze the strategy
Note the three stages: bury, sun-expose, incubate. Then the use of sun-melted moisture and insects.
3
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentences where the author declares these actions as proof of animal reason.

Rational Proof for Animal Intelligence
Rational argument for animal intelligence via observed behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that animals possess intelligence and understanding based on their complex, ingenious behaviors.

Core Idea
Animals perform clever acts that require reason and understanding.

Animals often surprise us with their seemingly intelligent behaviors. The author presents a rational proof that such actions cannot occur without some form of intelligence. Using the example of insects and birds cooperating to feed chicks, he argues that these creatures must possess reason and understanding. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the specific example given and the logical conclusion drawn.
Notice the phrase 'حيلة عجيبة' (wonderful trick) — it highlights the author's view of the behavior.
2
Identify the core argument
The author observes a complex behavior (insects feeding chicks) and concludes that the animals must be rational. Identify the premise and conclusion.
The premise: animals perform acts that even intelligent humans cannot. The conclusion: they must be rational and understanding.
3
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the author thinks this behavior cannot happen without intelligence. What would be the alternative?
The author implies that pure instinct is insufficient to explain such clever, coordinated actions.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some might think this is anthropomorphizing. The author is not saying animals have human-like intellect, but a limited form of reason. Note the distinction.
The passage uses 'عاقل' and 'فاهم' which imply a degree of understanding, but not necessarily full human reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اجتمع فيه النمل والذباب والديدان والحشرات، فتجعل تلك الأشياء طعمة لتلك الفراريج، فإذا تم ذلك، صارت تلك الفراريج قادرة على الرعي والطلب. ولا شك أن هذا الطريق حيلة عجيبة في تربية الأولاد. ولنكتف من هذا النوع على هذا القدر الذي ذكرنا. فإن الاستقصاء فيه مذكور في كتاب «الحيوان». وقد ظهر منها أن هذه الحيوانات قد تأتي بأفعال يعجز أكثر الأذكياء من الناس. ولو لم تكن عاقلة فاهمة، وإلا لما صح منها شيء من ذلك. فهذا ما يتعلق بالعقل.
And there gather in it ants, flies, worms, and insects, and they make those things as food for those chicks. When that is completed, those chicks become capable of grazing and seeking food. There is no doubt that this method is a wonderful trick in raising offspring. Let us suffice with this amount from this type, for a thorough investigation of it is mentioned in the book 'al-Hayawan'. And it has become apparent from these things that these animals perform acts that most intelligent people are incapable of. If they were not rational and understanding, none of that would be sound from them. This is what pertains to the intellect.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof (dalil 'aqli) for the claim that animals possess intelligence and understanding. The author describes how certain animals, like ants and flies, cooperate to feed chicks in a clever manner, which he calls a 'wonderful trick' (hila 'ajiba) for raising offspring. He then states that such intricate actions are beyond the ability of most intelligent humans, implying that animals must have some form of reason (aql) and comprehension (fahm) to perform them. The argument concludes that without these faculties, such actions could not occur. The author references the book 'al-Hayawan' for a fuller treatment, but the present example suffices to establish the point.

Key Terms
عاقل
فاهم
العقل
حيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is saying animals have human-like intelligence and are consciously reasoning like humans.
Correction: The author argues for a limited form of rationality appropriate to animals, not full human intellect. The terms 'عاقل' and 'فاهم' denote a degree of understanding, not necessarily self-aware reasoning.

Checkpoint

A) Animals are superior to humans in intelligence.

C) The Quran mentions animal intelligence.
D) Animals are incapable of learning.

Key Takeaway
Observing complex and ingenious behaviors in animals provides rational evidence that they possess some form of intelligence and understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows animals performing a 'wonderful trick' in raising their young that even the most intelligent humans cannot replicate. When did you last allow an animal's behavior to truly humble you? What keeps you from regularly reflecting on the signs in Allah's creation?
?
The author argues that animals possess rational understanding because of their complex actions. If Allah gave such intelligence to animals, what does that imply about your responsibility to treat them with respect and care? Are there ways you have overlooked or mistreated animals that need to change?
?
The proof here is based on observation. Have you ever observed an animal's action and dismissed it as mere instinct without considering the wisdom behind it? How can you develop a deeper sense of awe and gratitude for the intricate design in every living creature, as part of your faith?

Debate on Animal Intellect: Arguments and Resolution
The author records the argument of those who affirm animal intellect and those who deny it, with a responder's answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for and against animal intellect and the nuanced resolution offered by the responder.

Core Idea
Intellect may come in degrees; denying it entirely is judging the unseen.

This passage presents a classical debate: can animals be said to possess intellect? Affirmers point to their subtle behaviors; deniers argue that intellect would show clear effects absent in animals. The responder offers a middle path: perhaps animals have a specific type of rationality suited to their nature. This lesson walks through each position.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmers' argument
Start by reading the affirmers' case: they argue from animals' subtle stratagems to the conclusion that animals know their Lord. Notice how they build on prior evidence.
The affirmers consider it improbable to deny such knowledge given the observed behavior.
2
Read the deniers' objection
Then read the deniers' counter: if animals were rational, its effects would be apparent—but animals do not avoid harm or distinguish good from bad. Unused rationality would be futile, unworthy of a Wise Agent.
The deniers equate rationality with its observable manifestations.
3
Examine the responder's solution
Now study the responder's nuanced answer: degrees of knowledge vary, and souls differ in essence. Perhaps each animal has a specific type or degree of rationality, not necessarily the full human intellect.
The responder introduces the concept of different types of intellect.
4
Reflect on the principle of the unseen
Finally, note the concluding principle: total denial or affirmation of animal intellect is a judgment about the unseen, which only Allah knows. This humbles our certainty.
This is a key epistemic limit in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أنصار هذا القول : قالوا : لما بينا بالدلائل : أن هذه الحيوانات تهتدي إلى الحيل اللطيفة ، فأي استبعاد في أن يقال : إنها تعرف أن لها رباً مديراً وخالقاً ؟ . فهذا تمام القول في دلائل هذه الطائفة . واحتج المنكرون بكونها عائلة عارفة [؟] : بأن قالوا : لو كانت عاقلة لوجب أن تكون آثار العقل ظاهرة في حقها . لأن حصول العقل لها ، مع أنه لا يمكنها الانتفاع البتة بذلك العقل : عبث . وهذا لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم . وآثار العقل غير ظاهرة فيها ، لأنها لا تحترز عن الأفعال القبيحة ، ولا تميز بين ما ينفعها وبين ما يضرها . فوجب القطع بأنها غير عاقلة . ولمجيب أن يجيب فيقول : إن درجات العلوم والمعارف كثيرة ، واختلاف النفوس في ماهياتها : محتمل . فلعل خصوصية نفس كل واحد منها ، لا تقتضي إلا لنوع معين من العقل ، وإلا لقسم مخصوص من المعرفة . فإن كان المراد بالعقل جميع العلوم الحاصلة للإنسان ، فحق أنها ليست عاقلة . وإن كان المراد بالعقل معرفة نوع من هذه الأنواع. فظاهر أنها موصوفة بهذه المعرفة . وبالجملة : فالحكم عليها بالثبوت والعدم : حكم على الغيب . ولا يعلم الغيب إلا الله تعالى . وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في النفوس الحيوانية [ والله أعلم ] .
Then the supporters of this statement said: Since we have established with evidences that these animals are guided to subtle stratagems, so what improbability is there in saying that they know that they have a managing Lord and Creator? So this is the complete statement on the evidences of this group. And the deniers objected that they are [؟] knowing, by saying: If they were rational, then it would be necessary that the effects of rationality appear in their case. Because the possession of rationality for them, while they cannot benefit from that rationality at all, is futile. And this is not befitting the Wise Agent. And the effects of rationality are not apparent in them, because they do not avoid ugly actions, nor do they distinguish between what benefits them and what harms them. So it is necessary to decide that they are not rational. And the responder may respond by saying: Indeed the degrees of sciences and knowledges are many, and the difference of souls in their quiddities is possible. So perhaps the specificity of the soul of each one of them necessitates only a specific type of rationality, or only a specific division of knowledge. So if by rationality is meant all the sciences acquired by humans, then it is true that they are not rational. And if by rationality is meant knowledge of one of these types, then it is apparent that they are described with that knowledge. In summary: judging them with affirmation or negation is a judgment about the unseen. And none knows the unseen except Allah, the Exalted. And let this be the end of our speech on animal souls. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange about animal intellect. First, the affirmers (أنصار هذا القول) build on the earlier evidence, arguing that since animals exhibit subtle stratagems, it is not far-fetched to say they know God as their Creator and Manager. Then the deniers (المنكرون) object that if animals possessed rationality ('aql), its effects would be apparent—but they are not, because animals do not avoid ugly actions or distinguish benefit from harm. Moreover, having unused rationality would be futile (عبث), which is unworthy of a Wise Agent. The responder (المجيب) offers a nuanced solution: degrees of knowledge vary, and souls differ in essence (ماهيات). Perhaps each animal soul is uniquely suited for only a specific type of rationality or knowledge. The argument's crux is that total denial of rationality is a judgment on the unseen (حكم على الغيب), which only God can know. This leaves the question open, acknowledging different levels of cognition across species.

Misconception: Intellect is binary: either fully present or fully absent.
Correction: The responder shows that intellect can have degrees and types; animals may possess a specific kind of rationality suited to their nature.

The debate over animal intellect teaches us that rationality may not be uniform; judgments about other creatures' inner states require caution, as only Allah knows the unseen.

?
The deniers argued that if rationality existed, its effects would be obvious in behavior. Look at your own life: if someone examined your actions, would they see the effects of your faith—avoiding harm, pursuing good? Or is your intellect hidden and unused? What one change can you make this week to let your faith manifest visibly?
?
The responder says that judging animals' rationality is judging the unseen, known only to Allah. How often do you make definitive statements about others' intentions, beliefs, or inner states—things you cannot see? Will you commit to stopping such judgments and leaving the unseen to Allah alone?

The Three Principles of Human Actions
The author enumerates the three principles of human actions in causal order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causal principles of human actions: knowledge, inclination, and capability, and how they lead to action.

Core Idea
Human actions arise from three ordered principles: knowledge, inclination, and capability.

In this passage, the author dismantles human action into its foundational components. He argues that every voluntary action requires a sequence: first, knowledge of benefit or harm; second, an inclination based on that knowledge; third, the physical capability to act. Understanding this chain is essential for grasping Islamic theology of human agency and divine causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow from one principle to the next.
The author uses a clear 'first, second, third' structure.
2
Identify the three principles
List the three principles in order: knowledge and perception, inclination, and capability. Notice how each is defined.
The author refers to a previous chapter for more detail.
3
Reflect on the causal chain
Think about why knowledge must come before inclination. Why is this called a 'necessary' sequence?
The passage emphasizes logical necessity (luzūm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث
في
البحث عن حقيقة الإلمام بالوسوسة واستقصاء القول فيهما
اعلم : أن الأفعال الإنسانية لها مبادى ثلاثة ، مرتبة بعضها على بعض . فأعلاها وأبعدها عن قوع الفعل : العلم والإدراك . وذلك لأنه ما لم يقع في قلبه : أن الفعل الفلاني راجح [ النفع ، أو خالص النفع ، فإنه لا يفعله . وما لم يقع في قلبه أن الفعل الفلاني راجح الضرر (۱) ] أو خالص الضرر أو معادل الضرر ، فإنه لا يتركه . وقد بالغنا في تقرير هذه القاعدة في باب « الدواعي والصوارف ) من هذا الكتاب . ولأجل أن الإنسان لا يفعل فعلا ، إلا لطلب الخير، سمي هذا الفاعل فاعلاً مختاراً . والمرتبة الثانية - وهي المرتبة المتوسطة . أنه إذا حصل اعتقاد النفع الخالص أو الراجح ، ترتب عليه على سبيل اللزوم : حصول ميل قوي متأكد [ في الفعل . وإذا حصل اعتقاد الضرر الخالص أو الراجح ترتب عليه على سبيل اللزوم حصول ميل قوي متأكد في الترك (٢) ] . والمرتبة الثالثة - وهي المرتبة الأخيرة القريبة المتصلة بالفعل - أن الأعضاء إذا كانت سليمة قوية على الفعل والترك . فإذا انضم ذلك الميل المتعين إلى تلك القدرة القائمة بالعضو ، صار مجموع ذلك الميل وتلك القدرة : مؤثراً في حصول الفعل وهذا كلام ملخص معلوم قد سبق تقريره في باب الدواعي والصوارف ) .
The third chapter: On investigating the reality of prompting by whisper and exhausting the discussion concerning them. Know: that human actions have three principles, arranged one upon another. The highest and farthest from the occurrence of the action is knowledge and perception. That is because unless it occurs in his heart that a certain action is predominant in benefit, or pure benefit, he does not do it. And unless it occurs in his heart that a certain action is predominant in harm, or pure harm, or equal in harm, he does not abandon it. And we have elaborated on establishing this principle in the chapter on 'Motives and Deterrents' of this book. And because man does not do an action except seeking good, this agent is called a free agent. The second rank – and it is the intermediate rank – is that when belief in pure or predominant benefit occurs, there follows necessarily a strong, confirmed inclination towards the action. And when belief in pure or predominant harm occurs, there follows necessarily a strong, confirmed inclination towards abandonment. The third rank – and it is the last rank, close and attached to the action – is that when the limbs are sound and strong for action and abandonment, then when that specific inclination is joined with that power residing in the limb, the sum of that inclination and that power becomes effective in producing the action. And this is summarized speech, known, and its elaboration has preceded in the chapter on 'Motives and Deterrents'.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal explanation of the three principles (mabādi) of human actions, arranged in a causal sequence. The author begins by stating that human actions have three principles, ordered one upon another. The first and highest principle, farthest from the actual occurrence of the action, is knowledge and perception (al-ʿilm wa al-idrāk). The rationale is that unless a person's heart perceives that an action is predominantly beneficial or purely beneficial, he will not perform it; similarly, unless he perceives that an action is predominantly harmful, purely harmful, or equally harmful, he will not refrain from it. The author notes that this rule has been elaborated in the chapter 'al-Dawāʿī wa al-Ṣawārif' (Motives and Deterrents) of this book. Because man only acts in pursuit of good, the agent is called a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār). The second rank, the intermediate one, is that once belief (iʿtiqād) in pure or predominant benefit arises, there necessarily follows a strong, confirmed inclination (mayl qawī mutaʾakkid) towards the action. Similarly, belief in pure or predominant harm necessarily produces a strong inclination towards avoidance. The third and final rank, closest to the action, is that when the limbs are sound and capable of action or inaction, and that specific inclination is joined with the power (qudra) residing in the limb, the combination of inclination and power becomes the effective cause (muʾaththir) in bringing about the action. The author concludes that this is a summary of what has been fully established in the chapter on Motives and Deterrents.

الميل

الدواعي والصوارف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the first principle is inclination.
Correction: The author clearly states that knowledge and perception are the highest and farthest principle.

Checkpoint

B) Power (qudra)

D) The limb's capability

Key Takeaway
Human actions necessarily follow a three-step causal sequence: knowledge, then inclination, then capability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last fell into a sin you regret, which of these three principles was missing or corrupted? Was it your knowledge of its harm (you didn't truly believe it was harmful), your inclination (you didn't have a strong enough desire to avoid it), or your capability (you felt powerless to resist)? What does this tell you about the specific step you need to strengthen in your repentance?
?
You claim to believe that obeying Allah brings true benefit and that sin brings true harm. But if that knowledge were truly 'occurring in your heart' as the author describes, wouldn't your inclination necessarily follow? Why then do you often find yourself inclined toward disobedience? What would it take for your belief to become so certain that it produces the inclination without hesitation?
?
The author says that when the limbs are sound and the inclination is present, the combination necessarily produces the action. Consider an act of worship you consistently delay—like Fajr prayer or giving charity. Your limbs are sound, and you know the benefit. So why is the inclination not strong enough to move you? What is blocking the necessity, and what will you do today to remove that block?

First Proof: Motion Requires Life
The author presents the first philosophical proof for celestial life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical proof that celestial bodies are alive, based on the necessary classification of motion into natural, forced, or voluntary and the elimination of the first two.

Core Idea
Celestial motion must be voluntary, proving the spheres are alive.

In this passage, the author presents the first of several philosophical proofs for the existence of life in the heavens. The argument is rooted in the nature of motion and the principle that every specific effect must have an adequate cause. By analysing the three possible types of motion and eliminating the impossible ones, the author shows that celestial spheres move by their own will, which requires life.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'if' and 'so'.
The text is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three motion types
The author divides motion into natural, forced, and voluntary. Notice how he defines each type and why he says the first two are impossible for the spheres.
3
Understand the principle of effect and power
The author argues that any specific effect from a body must come from a power within that body, otherwise the effect would be arbitrary. This principle supports the division.
This is a key logical step.
4
Focus on the argument against natural motion
Read the final part carefully: natural motion would lead to a contradiction because it would require both the existence and removal of a state to be natural. This is the core reason natural motion is ruled out.
The text cuts off here, but the conclusion is that only voluntary motion remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : وهي الحجة الفلسفية القديمة - قالوا : ثبت إما بالحس وإما بالبرهان : أن الأفلاك والكواكب متحركة بالاستدارة ، وكل متحرك فحركته إما أن تكون طبيعية أو قسرية أو إرادية . والأولان باطلان ، فتعين الثالث . وذلك يقتضي كون الأفلاك أحياء . أما بيان الحصر : فهو أنه قد ثبت في باب خواص الواجب والممكن : أن كل جسم يصدر عنه أثر مخصوص ، لا بالقسر ولا بالعرض، فإنه يجب أن يكون ذلك لقوة موجودة فيه ، تناسب ذلك الأثر . إذ لو لم يكن ذلك ، لكان حال ذلك الجسم بالنسبة إلى ذلك الأثر كحال سائر الأجسام بالنسبة إليه ، وكان ذلك التخصيص رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساويين على الآخر ، لا المرجح . وهو محال . فنقول : ذلك الأثر ، إذا لم يكن بالقسر ، فلا بد وأن يكون لقوة موجودة فيه . وتلك القوة إما أن يكون لها شعور بما صدر عنها ، وهي القوة الحيوانية الإرادية ، أو لا يكون لها شعور بذلك الأثر ، وهي القوة الطبيعية المحضة . وأما إذا صدر عنه ذلك الأثر بالقسر ، فهو الذي يكون بالقسر . فقد ثبت : أن كل جسم يختص بأثر ، فذلك الأثر إما أن يكون طبيعياً أو قسرياً أو إرادياً . وذلك يدل على صحة الحصر المذكور . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون حركة الفلك طبيعية ، لأن الحركة تقتضي إزالة حالة حاصلة . فتلك الحالة ، إما أن تكون طبيعية أو لا تكون ، فإن كانت طبيعية مع أنا فرضنا أن الطبيعية تقتضي إزالتها ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون وجود تلك الحالة طبيعياً وأن يكون زوالها طبيعياً ، وذلك
The first argument, and it is the ancient philosophical argument. They said: it is established either by sensation or by demonstration that the celestial spheres and stars are rotating. And every moving thing, its motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. The first two are false, so the third is determined. That necessitates that the celestial spheres are living. As for the explanation of the division: it is that it has been established in the chapter on the properties of the Necessary and the possible that every body from which a specific effect proceeds, not by force and not incidentally, it must be that [the effect] is due to a power existing in it, suitable for that effect. For if that were not the case, the state of that body with respect to that effect would be like the state of other bodies with respect to it, and that specification would be a preponderance of one of the two equal possibilities over the other without a preponderant, which is absurd. So we say: that effect, if not by force, then it must be due to a power existing in it. And that power either has awareness of what proceeds from it, and it is the animal voluntary power, or it does not have awareness of that effect, and it is the purely natural power. And if that effect proceeds from it by force, then it is that which is by force. So it is established that every body that is characterized by an effect, that effect is either natural, forced, or voluntary. And that indicates the correctness of the mentioned division. And we say: it is impossible for the motion of the sphere to be natural, because motion necessitates the removal of an existing state. That state is either natural or not. If it is natural, and we have assumed that the natural [state] necessitates its removal, then it would follow that the existence of that state is natural and its removal is natural, and that
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof, called the ancient philosophical argument. It is a deductive proof based on the nature of motion. The proof proceeds as follows: (1) It is known by sensation or demonstration that the celestial spheres rotate. (2) Every motion is either natural, forced, or voluntary. (3) Natural and forced motions are impossible for the spheres, leaving only voluntary motion. (4) Voluntary motion requires life (since it proceeds from a power with awareness). The author then explains the division of motions in detail, using the principle that any specific effect from a body must come from a power within it, otherwise the effect would be arbitrary. He distinguishes between powers with and without awareness, and shows that the effect must be either natural, forced, or voluntary. Finally, he begins to argue why natural motion is impossible for the celestial sphere: motion removes an existing state, and if that state is natural, then both its existence and removal would be natural, which is contradictory. The argument cuts off here, but the conclusion would be that celestial motion is voluntary, hence the spheres are alive.

Correction: The author shows natural motion is impossible because it would require a state both existing and being removed naturally, a contradiction.

Checkpoint

C) Because they move in a circle and circular motion is always voluntary.
D) Because they are moved by angels and thus forced.

Key Takeaway
The celestial spheres must be alive because their motion can only be voluntary, not natural or forced.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the celestial spheres move by their own will in perfect submission to their Creator, what does that reveal about the nature of your own daily actions? Are you moving through life with deliberate intention and obedience, or are you simply being pushed by habit and external circumstances? Where can you exchange a 'natural' routine for a 'voluntary' act of worship today?
?
The proof shows that forced motion is impossible for the spheres because no external power can override their inherent nature. But when you sin, how often do you tell yourself, 'I had no choice' or 'I was forced by the situation'? If the lifeless spheres own their motion, can you own your choices? Will you take responsibility for your actions tonight and repent for the ones that were not from a willing submission to Allah?
?
The author insists that every specific effect must come from an internal power suited to it. Consider your good deeds: do they spring from a conscious awareness of Allah (shu'ur) or are they just movements without thought—habitual prayers, automatic charity, mindless fasting? This lesson demands that you inspect your heart: is your worship alive with intention, or is it a dead natural motion? What will you do to restore awareness to your actions tomorrow?

Celestial Life: Arguments from Light and Motion
Arguments from soul's faculties to prove celestial life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two analogical arguments used to prove that celestial bodies possess life in a superior degree due to their purity and motion.

Core Idea
Purity and motion in celestial bodies demand life far beyond earthly limits.

This passage continues a proof for the life of celestial bodies. The author uses two analogies: one from the rational soul's relation to pure light, and another from motion sustaining life. By comparing earthly and celestial phenomena, he concludes that celestial life is vastly superior in honor and majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's chain of reasoning unfold.
Notice the passage begins mid-sentence; it assumes prior context.
2
Identify the two arguments
Find the first argument about the rational soul and light, and the second about voluntary motion. Note how each uses a comparison between earthly and celestial bodies.
The author uses a 'more deserving' (أولى) structure in both.
3
Understand the concluding analogy
The final summary compares lights and motions, emphasizing that celestial life is incomparably greater. Reflect on why this conclusion is necessary based on the premises.
This is a 'from the lesser to the greater' (a minori ad maius) argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من القدر القليل من تلك البخارات التي هي الأرواح لما حصل لها ذلك القدر الصفاء والنورانية ، صار المتعلق الأول للنفس الناطقة تلك الأجسام ، وصارت الأعضاء إنما تجد قوة الحياة وقوة الإحساس ، لأجل وصول تلك الأرواح إليها . فالأجرام الفلكية مع غاية صفائها وإشراقها وجلالة جوهرها ، أولى أن تكون متعلقة للنفوس الحالية المشرقة الإلهية . وأما القوة الثانية من قوى النفس وهي الحركة الإرادية : فإنا نرى أن القلب كلما كان في الحركة ، كانت القوة النفسانية باقية . فلما صار القلب مع كثافته مستعداً لقبول الحياة ، بواسطة كونه دائم الحركة . فالأجرام الفلكية مع نهاية سرعتها في الحركات ، أولى بأن تكون موصوفة بالحياة . وبالجملة : فالنور يناسب الإدراك ، والعلم والحركة يناسب الفعل . فإذا كان لا مناسبة لأنوار تلك الأجسام إلى أنوار هذا العالم ، ولا مناسبة الحركات تلك الأجرام إلى حركات هذه الأجرام ، وجب الجزم بأنه لا مناسبة لحياة تلك الأجسام في الشرف والجلالة ، إلى حياة هذه الأجسام
From the small amount of those vapors which are the spirits, when they attained that amount of purity and illumination, those bodies became the first object of the rational soul, and the organs only find the power of life and the power of sensation because of the arrival of those spirits to them. So the celestial bodies, with the utmost of their purity, radiance, and nobility of substance, are more deserving to be the objects of the luminous divine souls. As for the second faculty of the soul's faculties, which is voluntary movement: we see that the heart, whenever it is in motion, the psychic faculty remains. So when the heart, despite its density, becomes prepared to receive life by virtue of being constantly in motion, the celestial bodies, with the extremity of their speed in motions, are more deserving to be described with life. In summary: light is suited to perception, and knowledge and movement are suited to action. So if there is no comparison between the lights of those bodies to the lights of this world, and no comparison of the motions of those bodies to the motions of these bodies, then it is necessary to affirm that there is no comparison of the life of those bodies in honor and majesty to the life of these bodies.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of an argument (likely the third proof) for the life of celestial bodies. It begins by describing how spirits (al-arwah) arise from vapors and acquire purity and illumination, making bodies the first objects of the rational soul. The organs receive life and sensation through these spirits. Since celestial bodies are supremely pure and radiant, they are more fitting to be objects of divine luminous souls. The author then turns to the second faculty of the soul: voluntary motion (al-haraka al-iradiyya). He notes that the heart remains alive as long as it moves; motion enables it to receive life despite its density. Since celestial bodies move with extreme speed, they are even more deserving of life. Summarizing, light is suited to perception (idrak), while knowledge and motion are suited to action. If there is no comparison between the lights and motions of celestial bodies and those of earthly bodies, then there can be no comparison in the honor and majesty of their lives either. Thus, the life of celestial bodies is far superior. The argument proceeds by analogy: what is true for earthly bodies (motion indicating life) applies with greater force to celestial bodies.

Key Terms
الأرواح
النفس الناطقة
الحركة الإرادية
الأجرام الفلكية
النورانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think celestial bodies are lifeless because they are not organic or do not have visible biological functions.
Correction: The author argues that life is not limited to biological processes; purity, radiance, and perpetual motion indicate a higher form of life.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what makes celestial bodies more deserving of life than earthly bodies?
A) They are made of the same vapors as earthly spirits.
B) They possess extreme purity, radiance, and speed of motion.
C) They have hearts that are constantly in motion.
D) They are composed of the same elements as the heart.

Key Takeaway
Celestial bodies are alive and their life is infinitely more sublime than ours, as shown by their purity, radiance, and perpetual motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the stars tonight, will you see them as mere lights or as living beings more noble than yourself? How would that shift your sense of humility before their Creator?
?
The heart remains alive through constant motion. In your spiritual life, where have you become still? What is one movement—one act of worship or service—you can take today to revive your soul?
?
If celestial life is incomparably more honorable than earthly life, why do you spend so much energy chasing the pleasures of this world? What would it look like to live today with your gaze fixed on that higher reality?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
The Fourth Proof: Geometric Proportion and Life
Geometrical proportion argument for celestial life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof uses geometric proportion and intuitive moral reasoning to argue that celestial bodies must possess life.

Core Idea
The largest part of creation should have the best attribute: life.

This lesson examines the fourth proof for the life of celestial bodies, which draws on a striking geometrical observation: the four elements are to the celestial spheres as a point is to a great circle—negligible in size. The author then appeals to reason's intuitive judgment that life is superior to inanimateness, concluding that the vast celestial realm, being the greatest part of creation, must be alive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two distinct parts: the geometrical analogy and the argument from superiority of life.
The passage begins with 'الحجة الرابعة' indicating this is the fourth proof.
2
Understand the geometrical analogy
The author says the four elements relative to the celestial spheres are like the center to the great circle. This means the elements are extremely small compared to the heavens. Ask yourself: why does this matter for the argument?
The analogy emphasizes the vast size difference between earthly and celestial realms.
3
Grasp the intuitive premise
The author states that 'the intuition of the intellect judges that life is better than inanimateness.' This is presented as a self-evident truth. Consider why this premise is important for the conclusion.
This is a moral-aesthetic judgment, not a purely physical one.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
If the heavens are the largest part of creation and life is the best attribute, then it would be contrary to divine mercy and wisdom if the heavens lacked life. The author also adds a parallel argument comparing permanence and purity.
The argument uses a 'fittingness' or 'appropriateness' principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قد دلت الشواهد الرياضية : على أن جميع العناصر الأربعة ، بالنسبة إلى كلية الأفلاك ، كالمركز بالنسبة إلى الدائرة العظيمة . وبديهة العقل حاكمة : بأن الحياة أفضل من الجمادية . فلو كانت الأجرام الفلكية خالية عن الحياة ، لكان معظم مخلوقات الله تعالى ناقصة خالية عن آثار رحمة الله تعالى وحكمته ، وذلك بعيد . وأيضاً : فالأجسام الفلكية لا نسبة لها في البقاء والنقاء والصفاء إلى هذه الأجسام الخسيسة العنصرية . والحيــاة والعقل ، لا نسبة لهما في الشرف والفضيلة إلى الجمادية والموت . فتخصيص الأجرام الخسيسة بالصفات الشريفة العالية ، وإخلاء الأجرام الشريفة العالية الصفات الشريفة على مضادة الرحمة والحكمة . وذلك في غاية البعد
The fourth proof: The mathematical evidences have indicated that all four elements, relative to the entire celestial spheres, are like the center relative to the great circle. And the intuition of the intellect judges that life is better than inanimateness. So if the celestial bodies were devoid of life, then most of the creatures of Allah, the Exalted, would be deficient, devoid of the effects of Allah's mercy and wisdom, and that is far-fetched. Also, the celestial bodies have no comparison in permanence, purity, and clarity to these base elemental bodies. And life and intellect have no comparison in honor and virtue to inanimateness and death. So specifying the base bodies with noble, high attributes, and leaving the noble, high bodies devoid of noble attributes is contrary to mercy and wisdom. And that is extremely far-fetched.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-hujja al-rabi'a) for the life of celestial bodies. It begins with a geometrical observation: the four elements (earth, water, air, fire) are to the totality of the celestial spheres as the center is to the great circle—i.e., negligible in size. The intuition of reason (badihah al-‘aql) tells us that life is superior to inanimateness (al-jamadiyya). If the celestial bodies had no life, then most of God's creation would be deficient and lacking in the effects of His mercy and wisdom, which is improbable. Moreover, celestial bodies far surpass earthly bodies in permanence, purity, and clarity, while life and intellect far surpass inanimateness and death in honor and virtue. Hence, to attribute noble qualities to base bodies and deny them to noble bodies would contradict mercy and wisdom. Therefore, celestial bodies must be alive. The argument uses a combination of quantitative proportion (the heavens are the largest part) and qualitative comparison (the best attributes should belong to the best substances).

Key Terms
الشواهد الرياضية
العناصر الأربعة
الأفلاك
الجمادية
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reasoning behind the fourth proof?
A) The celestial bodies are small so they don't need life.
B) Since the heavens are the largest part of creation, they should have the best attribute (life).
C) Life is only found in earthly elements.
D) The celestial bodies are closer to God so they are alive.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof argues that because the heavens are the largest part of creation and life is the best attribute, the celestial bodies must possess life to reflect divine mercy and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that most of creation—the heavens—are alive with purpose. Does your daily routine reflect awe for that living universe, or do you treat the sky as a dead backdrop? What one change can you make this week to acknowledge the life above you?
?
If life is indeed better than inanimateness, then your own soul is a gift. When have you chosen spiritual lethargy over vibrant awareness of Allah? How will you awaken your heart starting today?
?
The proof argues that denying life to the heavens contradicts divine mercy. Is there any part of your belief or practice where you implicitly deny God's mercy by assuming a dead, mechanical universe? How can you actively affirm His mercy in your view of creation?
